Understanding Sarmad, Dahr, and Zaman
Defines sarmad, dahr, zaman, presents Ibn Sina's analogy and author's critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definitions of sarmad, dahr, and zaman, how Ibn Sina's analogy relates them, and the critique that the author finds it meaningless.

Core Idea
Sarmad, dahr, and zaman describe distinct modes of existence: permanent, relative-to-changing, and changing.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid introduces three key temporal concepts in Islamic philosophy: sarmad, dahr, and zaman. Understanding these terms is essential for later discussions on divine attributes and creation. The author defines them, presents Ibn Sina's well-known analogy, and ultimately dismisses it as unintelligible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the terms sarmad, dahr, and zaman and how they are used.
The first line may be slightly unclear, but the overall meaning becomes clear from context.
2
Identify the definitions
Notice how the passage first implies definitions: zaman relates to changing things, dahr to permanent things relative to changing, and sarmad to permanent things. Then Ibn Sina gives a more explicit threefold classification.
The author's initial definition suggests no extension in dahr or sarmad.
3
Understand the hierarchy and critique
The passage presents a causal hierarchy: zaman is an effect of dahr, and dahr is an effect of sarmad. Then Ibn Sina's analogy is presented and criticized as meaningless according to al-Imam.
The critique is based on the perspective of Imam al-Razi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كأنها متى للأمور الثابتة ولا يتوهم في الدهر ولا في السرمد امتداد، وإلا لكان مقداراً بالحركة، ثم الزمان كمعلول للدهر والدهر كمعلول للسرمد، فإنه لولا دوام نسبة علل الأجسام إلى مبادئها ما وجدت الأجسام فضلا عن حركاتها، ولولا دوام نسبة الزمان إلى مبدأ الزمان لم يتحقق الزمان . وقال ابن سينا: إن اعتبار أحوال المتغيرات مع المتغيرات هو الزمان واعتبار أحوال الأشياء الثابتة مع الأشياء المتغيرة هو الدهر، ومع الأشياء الثابتة هو السرمد، والدهر في ذاته من السرمد وهو بالقياس إلى الزمان، دهر وهو أن الدهر في نفسه شيء ثابت إلا أنه إذا نسب إلى الزمان الذي هو متغير ذاته دهراً، هذا ما وقع إلينا من شرح هذا الكلام والظاهر أنه ليس له معنى يحصل على ما قال الإمام .
As if it is for permanent things, and no extension is imagined in dahr nor in sarmad, otherwise it would be measured by motion. Then zaman is like an effect of dahr, and dahr like an effect of sarmad. For if not for the permanence of the relation of the causes of bodies to their principles, bodies would not exist, let alone their motions. And if not for the permanence of the relation of time to the principle of time, time would not be realized. Ibn Sina said: The consideration of the states of changing things along with the changing things is zaman, and the consideration of the states of permanent things along with changing things is dahr, and with permanent things is sarmad. Dahr in itself is from sarmad, and in relation to zaman it is dahr; that is, dahr in itself is a permanent thing, but when it is related to zaman which is changing in essence, it is dahr. This is what has come to us of the explanation of this statement, and it appears that it has no meaning that can be understood according to what the Imam said.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage defines the three temporal concepts—sarmad, dahr, and zaman—and presents Ibn Sina's interpretation, then critiques it. It begins by stating that no extension is imagined in dahr or sarmad, otherwise they would be measured by motion. Then it outlines a hierarchy: zaman is an effect of dahr, and dahr is an effect of sarmad. The reasoning is that without the permanence of the relation of bodies' causes to their principles, bodies would not exist, and without the permanence of the relation of time to its principle, time would not exist. Ibn Sina's view is then quoted: the consideration of changing things with changing things is zaman; of permanent things with changing things is dahr; and of permanent things with permanent things is sarmad. Dahr in itself is part of sarmad, but in relation to zaman it is called dahr. The author comments that this explanation is not meaningful according to what al-Imam (likely al-Razi) stated, implying a rejection.

Key Terms
السرمد
الدهر
الزمان
ابن سينا
الإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that sarmad, dahr, and zaman are synonyms or interchangeable terms.
Correction: They have distinct technical definitions: sarmad is the consideration of permanent things with permanent things; dahr is permanent things with changing things; zaman is changing things with changing things.

Checkpoint

A) Zaman
B) Dahr
C) Sarmad
D) Motion

Key Takeaway
The author rejects Ibn Sina's definition of sarmad, dahr, and zaman as unintelligible, emphasizing the importance of clear definitions.

?

?

?
If sarmad represents the perspective of permanent things with permanent things (like God's view), how does that change the way you think about your own temporary existence? Will you live today differently knowing that your life is only a temporal slice within a much larger reality?

Zaman as Measure of Motion and the Objection of Sequential Instants
Author argues zaman is measure of motion as cutting, then addresses objection of sequential instants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's preferred definition of time as the measure of motion (cutting) and how he defends it against the objection that it implies a succession of indivisible instants.

Core Idea
Time is a continuous flow, not a series of separate instants.

In this passage, the author presents his chosen definition of time and anticipates a key objection. He argues that time is the measure of motion in the sense of 'cutting'—motion occurs part by part without simultaneity. He then addresses the concern that this view reduces time to a string of discrete instants, clarifying that time is a continuous quantity that we only mentally divide.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: zaman, haraka, qat', an, tatalli al-anat.
2
Identify the author's definition
Find where the author states his choice: time is the measure of motion in the sense of cutting (qat'). Notice the description of motion as non-permanent, part-by-part existence.
3
Distinguish real vs imaginary
Notice the distinction between the real part-by-part existence and the imaginary extended sum. This is crucial for understanding the author's view.
The imaginary sum is the collection from start to end, which is not real.
4
Understand the objection
The objector claims this view leads to tatalli al-anat (succession of instants) because the indivisible 'flow' is nothing but an instant. Grasp why this is a problem.
If time is composed of instants, it would be discrete, not continuous.
5
See the author's answer
The author responds by asserting that time has no actual parts; it is continuous. Division into instants is merely conceptual, not real.
This preserves the continuity of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عن الثاني : فبأنا نختار أن الزمان مقدار للحركة بمعنى القطع وهي أمر غير قار يوجد منها جزء فجزء من غير أن يحصل جزءان دفعة وهذا معنى وجودها في الخارج، وإنما الوهمي هو المجموع الممتد من المبدأ إلى المنتهى فكذا مقدارها الذي هو الزمان يكون بحسب المجموع وهمياً لا يوجد منه جزءان دفعة بل لا يزال يتجدد وينصرم ويوجد منه شيء فشيء، وهذا يقال : إن هناك أمراً غير منقسم يفعل سيلانه (الزمان) كما أن في الحركة معنى هو الكون في الوسط يفعل سيلانه الحركة بمعنى القطع . واعترض بأن هذا قول بتتالي الآنات لأن ذلك الأمر الغير المنقسم ليس غير الآن، وأجيب بأنه لا أجزاء هناك بالفعل لأن الزمان كمية متصلة يعرض لها التجزؤ والانقسام بحسب الغرض والوهم دون الخارج
As for the second [point]: We choose that time is a measure of motion in the sense of cutting (qat'), and it is a non-permanent thing, from which part after part exists without two parts occurring simultaneously, and this is the meaning of its existence in the external world. The imaginary [thing] is the extended sum from the beginning to the end, so likewise its measure, which is time, is imaginary with respect to the sum; no two parts of it exist simultaneously, but rather it continuously renews and passes away, and something after something exists from it. And it is said: There is an indivisible thing that flows (zaman), just as in motion there is a meaning which is 'being in the middle' that flows, motion in the sense of cutting. And it was objected: This is the doctrine of sequential instants (tatalli al-anat), because that indivisible thing is nothing other than the instant. And it was answered: There are no parts actually [existing] there, because time is a continuous quantity that admits division and partitioning according to purpose and imagination, not in the external world.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents the author's preferred definition of time (zaman) and defends it against the objection of sequential instants. The author asserts that time is the measure of motion in the sense of 'cutting' (qat'), meaning that motion occurs part by part without any two parts coexisting, and this is how it exists externally. The imaginary extended sum from start to finish is not real; time as measure shares this imaginary character—no two parts of it exist simultaneously; rather, it continuously arises and passes away. The author then introduces the idea of an indivisible 'flow' that constitutes time, analogous to the 'being in the middle' that constitutes motion as cutting. An objection is raised: this view implies a succession of indivisible instants (tatalli al-anat), since the indivisible thing is nothing but the instant. The answer is that there are no actual parts in time; it is a continuous quantity that we mentally divide for analysis, but such division does not correspond to external reality.

الحركة
القطع
الآن
تتالي الآنات
الوهم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is actually composed of a series of indivisible instants.
Correction: Time is continuous; its division into instants is merely conceptual, not real.

Checkpoint

A) As a continuous extended sum
B) As a series of actual instants
C) Part after part without two parts simultaneously
D) As a permanent substance

Key Takeaway
The author defines time as a continuous measure of motion, rejecting the notion that it is composed of actual discrete instants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Can you use this truth to free yourself from despair and renew your tawba in the present flowing moment? What practical step will you take to live in the 'now' of time, accountable to Allah?

Reply to the Objection: Time's Extension and Its Grounding in Motion
Answers objection that time lacks existence and grounds time in motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the reality of time as an intelligible extension grounded in non-permanent external motion, against the objection that time has no existence.

Core Idea
Time's extension is intelligible, not externally existent, but based on non-permanent motion.

The passage begins with a strong objection: if time's parts are imaginary and non-existent, how can time exist at all? The author responds by distinguishing time from distance and motion, and argues that time is an intellectual extension rooted in a non-permanent external reality. Let's follow this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and contrast
Read the initial objection that time has no existence because its extension is imaginary. Then notice the contrast with distance (whose continuous sum exists externally) and motion (which has continuous 'being in the middle').
The objection treats time like distance or motion; the answer will show it is different.
2
Examine the answer: intellectual extension
The answer explains that time is an extension in the intellect, based on something non-permanent in the external world (motion). Its assumed parts would have priority and posteriority intrinsically.
Priority and posteriority attach to these assumed parts without requiring another time.
3
Understand the corollary of mental existence
Since two parts of time never exist together in the external world, their priority and posteriority are also mental. If they were external, they would be correlatives requiring simultaneous subjects, making time permanent.
This shows that time cannot be an external permanent thing.
4
Grasp the metaphorical usage
The author clarifies that when we say 'time is a subject for priority and posteriority', it is metaphorical; actually, priority and posteriority belong to the assumed parts of intelligible time because of the non-permanent motion.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فورد الإشكال بأنه لا وجود للزمان حينئذ لأن نفس الامتداد موهوم والجزء معدوم فماذا منه؟ وهذا بخلاف المسافة فإن أجزاءها وإن لم تكن بالفعل إلا أن المجموع المتصل الذي يتجزأ في الوهم موجود في الخارج، وبخلاف الحركة فإنه يوجد منها أمر مستمر هو الكون في الوسط من غير لزوم محال . وأجيب بأن المراد أن في الفعل امتداد لا وجود له في الخارج لكنه بحيث لو فرض وجوده وتجزؤه عرضت لأجزائه المفروضة قبليات وبعديات متجددة ومنصرفة، ولا يكون الامتداد في العقل كذلك إلا إذا كان في الخارج شيء غير قار يحصل في العقل بحسب استمراره وعدم استقراره، ذلك الامتداد الذي إذا فرض تجزية كان لحوق التقدم والتأخر لأجزائه المفروضة لذاتها من غير اقتضاء زمان آخر وكذا معيته للحركة وإذا لا وجود للجزأين معاً إلا في العقل لزم كون القبلية والبعدية العارضتين لهما كذلك، ولهذا يعرضان للعدم كيف ولو وجدتا في الخارج وهما متضايفان لزم وجود معروضيهما معاً في الخارج، ويلزم كون الزمان قار الذات، وما يقال من أن الموجود في الخارج من الزمان معروض للقبلية والبعدية فمجاز والمراد أنه متعلق بهما بمعنى أنهما بسببه يعرضان للأجزاء المفروضة للزمان المعقول، هذا غاية تحقيقهم في هذا المقام دفعاً للإشكالات الموردة من قبل الإمام، مثل أن قبلية عدم الحادث على وجوده لو اقتضت زماناً كانت قبلية الأمس على الغد، ومعية الحركة للزمان كذلك، وأن القبلية والبعدية لو وجدتا لا متنع اتصاف العدم بهما، ولكان وجودهما بالزمان وتسلسل وللزم وجود معروضيهما معاً ضرورة كونهما متضايفين فيكون الزمان قار الذات لاجتماع أجزائه المفروضة للقبلية والبعدية، ولو كانتا من الاعتبارات العقلية التي لا
Then the objection was raised that time has no existence in that case, because the extension itself is imaginary and the part is non-existent, so of what does it consist? This is unlike distance, for its parts, even if not actually [existing], yet the continuous sum that is divided in the imagination exists in the external world, and unlike motion, for there exists from it a continuous thing which is the 'being in the middle' without any impossibility. And it was answered: What is meant is that in actuality there is an extension that has no existence in the external world, but it is such that if its existence and division were assumed, their assumed parts would be characterized by renewed and passing priority and posteriority; and the extension in the intellect would not be so unless there were in the external world something non-permanent that occurs in the intellect according to its continuity and instability—that extension which, if division were assumed, the attachment of priority and posteriority to its assumed parts would be due to themselves without requiring another time, and likewise its accompaniment of motion. And when there is no existence of two parts together except in the intellect, it follows that the priority and posteriority that attach to them are likewise [in the intellect]. For this reason they attach to non-existence. Moreover, if they existed in the external world while being correlatives, the existence of their subjects together in the external world would be necessary, and it would follow that time is permanent in essence. And what is said—that what exists in the external world of time is a subject for priority and posteriority—is metaphorical; the meaning is that it is related to them in that they occur to the assumed parts of the intelligible time because of it. This is the utmost of their verification in this context, refuting the objections raised by al-Imam, such as that the priority of the non-existence of an event over its existence, if it necessitated time, would be the priority of yesterday over tomorrow, and likewise the accompaniment of motion with time; and that priority and posteriority, if they existed, non-existence could not be described by them, and they would have existence by time leading to infinite regress, and the existence of their subjects together would be necessary due to their being correlatives, so time would be permanent in essence due to the gathering of its assumed parts for priority and posteriority; and if they were from the intellectual considerations that do not...
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage addresses two objections and refutes a series of arguments from al-Imam (probably Fakhr al-Din al-Razi). First, an objection is raised: if time is imaginary and its parts non-existent, then time has no existence at all. The answer distinguishes between distance and motion: the continuous sum of distance exists in the external world even if its parts are potential, and motion has a continuous 'being in the middle' without impossibility. For time, there is an extension in the intellect that has no external existence, but it is based on something non-permanent in the external world (i.e., the motion itself). The extension's assumed parts would have priority and posteriority intrinsically, without requiring another time. Since two parts never exist together except in the mind, the priority and posteriority are also mental. If they were external, they would be correlatives requiring simultaneous subjects, making time permanent—which is absurd. Therefore, the external 'time' is metaphorically called subject of priority/posteriority; actually, these attach to the assumed parts of intelligible time. Then the author summarizes the refutation of al-Imam's objections: e.g., the priority of non-existence of an event over its existence would imply the priority of yesterday over tomorrow (absurd); the accompaniment of motion with time would also be problematic; if priority/posteriority existed, non-existence could not have them, and they would require a second time (regress), etc. The passage cuts off mid-sentence.

الإشكال
الوهم
التضايف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time must be an external, permanent thing with real parts.
Correction: Time is an intelligible extension based on non-permanent motion; its parts are assumed, and priority/posteriority are mental.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to ground the intelligible extension of time?
A) Distance
B) A non-permanent thing in the external world (motion)
C) The imaginary parts themselves
D) A permanent essence

Key Takeaway
Time is an intelligible extension rooted in non-permanent motion; its priority and posteriority are mental, not external realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think of time as a real thing that slips away from you. But this passage says time has no external existence—it is only a mental construct based on motion. How does this change your perception of the 'time' you feel you are losing or wasting? Will you stop treating time as a container that runs out and instead see each moment as a direct sign of Allah's continuous creation?
?
The priority and posteriority of time are said to be only mental. When you regret a past sin, you treat it as 'before' in a real timeline. But if priority is purely mental, what does that mean for your regret? Does it free you from the weight of a 'past' that is not really there, or does it make repentance more urgent because only Allah's decree is real? How will you change your relationship with your past based on this truth?
?
If time is not a real entity, then your plans for the future are also not grounded in a real 'after.' Yet you live as though tomorrow is a fixed point. How does this lesson challenge your attachment to future hopes and fears? Will you trust Allah more and worry less about a 'time' that is only a mental illusion?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Refutation of al-Imam's Objections on Priority, Posteriority, and Time
Summarizes refutation of al-Imam's objections on priority, posteriority, and time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific arguments of al-Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) against the reality of time and how the author refutes them by showing they lead to absurdities.

Core Idea
al-Imam's objections collapse because they treat time as an external permanent reality.

Having established the nature of time as an intellectual extension, the author now turns to refute the objections raised by al-Imam. These objections include the priority of non-existence, the accompaniment of motion, and the implications of correlative priority/posteriority. The refutation shows that these objections are based on misconstruing time as an external reality, which the author has already rejected.

Guided Path
1
Review al-Imam's objections
Read the list of objections: 1) Priority of non-existence over existence would be like yesterday over tomorrow. 2) Accompaniment of motion with time would also be problematic. 3) If priority/posteriority existed externally, non-existence could not have them, and they would require a second time (infinite regress).
These objections assume priority/posteriority are real attributes.
2
Understand the refutation of priority of non-existence
The author argues that if priority of non-existence required time, then it would be like the priority of yesterday over tomorrow, which is absurd because yesterday and tomorrow are times themselves.
This shows the objection is based on a false analogy.
3
Analyze the refutation of accompaniment of motion
The objection that motion accompanies time is refuted by showing that time is not a separate entity but an intellectual extension concurrent with motion.
Motion and time are not two separate things.
4
Grasp the refutation of correlativity and infinite regress
If priority and posteriority existed externally as correlatives, they would require simultaneous subjects, making time permanent. They also would require another time to exist, leading to infinite regress. Since these consequences are absurd, priority/posteriority cannot be external.
This reinforces the mental nature of priority/posteriority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the sequence of events in your life, do you treat priority and posteriority as real properties of time? How does this lesson challenge you to see them instead as mental constructs? Commit to recognizing that time is not an independent container holding your actions.
?
Al-Imam's objections seemed logical, but the scholars refuted them by showing they lead to absurdities like infinite regress. Are there beliefs you hold about God that seem logical from your limited perspective but might lead to contradictions? What would it take for you to submit to the scholars' reasoning even when it unsettles your assumptions? This week, choose one belief about God that you have not deeply examined and test it against the criteria of the kalam tradition.
?
This lesson shows that priority and posteriority are mere intellectual considerations. How does this affect your understanding of waiting, patience, and the timing of events in your life? If time is not an ultimate reality, how should you relate to the pressure of deadlines, the fear of being late, or the anxiety of an uncertain future? Ask yourself: Am I enslaved by time, or do I use it as a tool while knowing that Allah is beyond time? Make a concrete plan to shift your mindset today.

4 min
The Definition of Place and the Surface View
Definition of place and first view (surface).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common definition of place and the first major position that identifies it as the inner surface of the containing body.

Core Idea
Place is the inner surface of the container that touches the contained body.

This lesson begins with the basic intuitive notion of place as a vessel that holds a body. Then it presents the view of Aristotle and the Peripatetics, who define place as the inner surface of the containing body. This sets the stage for the debate between surface and dimension theories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the common definition
Look for the sentence that gives a general definition of place as a vessel. Note the key characteristics: a body moves from and to it, settles in it, and it does not accommodate another body.
This is the starting point for all subsequent views.
3
Find the first specific view
The passage then says 'the considered among the schools' and presents a view. Identify what that view is and who holds it.
The view is attributed to Aristotle and his followers.
4
Contrast surface vs container
Notice that this view specifies 'inner surface' not the whole container. Reflect on why this precision matters for later debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا خفاء في آنية شيء ينتقل الجسم عنه وإليه ويسكن فيه ولا يسع معه غيره وهو المسمى بالمكان. والمعتبر من المذاهب: أن ماهية السطح الباطن من الجسم الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من المحوى. وإليه ذهب أرسطو وأشياعه من المشائين. أو البعد الذي ينفذ فيه بعد الجسم ويتجدد به، وإليه ذهب كثير من الفلاسفة والمتكلمين. وزعموا أن من البعد ما هو مادي يحل في الجسم ويقوم به ويمتنع اجتماعه مع بعد آخر مماثل له قائم بذلك الجسم وهو المسمى بالجسم التعليمي، ومنه مفارق لا يقوم بمحل بل يحل فيه الجسم ويلاقيه بجملته ويجامعه بعد الجسم منطبقاً عليه متحداً به. إلا إنه عند المتكلمين عدم محض ونفي صرف يمكن أن لا يشغله شاغل وهو المعنى بالفراغ المتوهم الذي لو لم يشغله شاغل لكان فارغاً. وعند بعض الفلاسفة: امتداد موجود قد يكون ذراعاً وقد يكون أقل أو هذا الجسم وقد يسع ما هو أصغر منه أو أكبر وقد يسع. وتوضيحه أنا إذا توهمنا خلواً لإناء من الماء والهواء وغيرهما ففيما بين أطرافه امتداد قد يشغله الماء وقد يشغله الهواء فكذا عند الامتلاء ويسمونه البعد المفطور بمعنى أنه مشهور مفطور عليه البديهة فإن كل أحد يحكم بأن الماء فيما بين أطراف الإناء وقيل بمعنى أنه ينشق فيدخل فيه الجسم بما له من البعد. ويعبر عنه أفلاطون تارة بالهيولي لتوارد الأجسام عليه توارد الصور على المادة وتارة بالصورة لكونه عبارة عن الأبعاد الممتدة في الجهات بمنزلة الصورة الاتصالية الجسمية التي بها يقبل الجسم الأبعاد ويتميز عن المجردات. ثم إن هذا البعد عند أفلاطون وأتباعه ممتنع الخلو عن شاغل وعند البعض ممكن الخلو عنه وأصحاب الخلاء هم المتكلمون وبعض الفلاسفة. ففي هذا البحث مقامان: أحدهما في أن المكان هو السطح أو البعد وثانيهما في أن الخلاء ممكن أو ممتنع.
There is no hiddenness in that a vessel is something from which and to which a body moves, and in which it settles, and does not accommodate anything else with it, and it is called 'place' (al-makan). The considered among the schools [of thought] is that the essence of the inner surface of the containing body that touches the outer surface of the contained body. And this is the view of Aristotle and his followers among the Peripatetics. Or it is the dimension (al-bu'd) into which the body's dimension penetrates and is renewed by it, and this is the view of many philosophers and theologians (al-mutakallimun). They claimed that of the dimension there is a material one that inheres in the body and subsists by it, and it is impossible for it to gather with another like dimension subsisting in that body, and it is called 'the teaching body' (al-jism al-ta'līmī). And from it there is a separable one that does not subsist in a substrate, but rather the body inheres in it and meets it in its entirety, and it coexists with the body's dimension, conforming to it and united with it. Except that according to the theologians it is pure non-existence and absolute negation, which it is possible that no occupier occupies it, and it is meant by 'the imaginary void' (al-farāgh al-mutawahham), which if no occupier occupied it, it would be empty. And according to some philosophers: it is an existing extension which may be a cubit, or less, or this body, and it may accommodate what is smaller or larger than it. And its clarification: if we imagine a vessel empty of water and air and other things, then between its extremities there is an extension which water may occupy, or air may occupy, and likewise at the time of filling, and they call it 'the innate dimension' (al-bu'd al-mafṭūr), meaning it is well-known, innate to intuition, for everyone judges that water is between the vessel's extremities. And it is said meaning that it splits open, so the body enters into it by its dimension. And Plato expresses it sometimes as 'matter' (al-hayūlā) due to the succession of bodies upon it like the succession of forms upon matter, and sometimes as 'form' (al-ṣūra) because it is an expression of the dimensions extended in directions, akin to the continuous corporeal form by which the body receives dimensions and is distinguished from incorporeal beings. Then this dimension, according to Plato and his followers, is impossible to be empty of an occupier, and according to some it is possible to be empty of it, and the proponents of void (aṣḥāb al-khalā') are the theologians and some philosophers. So in this inquiry there are two stations: one concerning whether place is the surface or the dimension, and the second concerning whether the void is possible or impossible.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of place (al-makan) and presents two major positions, along with a discussion of the void (al-khalā'). It begins by stating the common understanding of place as a vessel into which a body moves, settles, and does not allow another body simultaneously. Then it presents two views: the first, attributed to Aristotle and the Peripatetics, identifies place as the inner surface of the containing body that touches the outer surface of the contained body. The second view, held by many philosophers and theologians (al-mutakallimun), identifies place as a dimension (al-bu'd) that the body's dimension enters and is renewed by. Within this second view, there are two types of dimension: a material dimension that inheres in the body and cannot coexist with another similar dimension (called 'the teaching body', al-jism al-ta'līmī), and a separable dimension that does not inhere in a substrate but rather the body inheres in it. Then the passage explains the different stances on whether this dimension is real or merely conceptual. For the theologians, it is pure non-existence and absolute negation, a potential void that could be unoccupied. For some philosophers, it is an existing extension that can be measured and can accommodate bodies of varying sizes, known as the 'innate dimension' (al-bu'd al-mafṭūr) because it is intuitively recognized. The passage also mentions Plato's terminology, who calls it matter (hayūlā) or form (ṣūra) depending on perspective. Finally, it introduces the two main issues to be investigated: first, whether place is the surface or the dimension; second, whether the void is possible or impossible. This sets up the subsequent discussion.

Key Terms
المكان
السطح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first considered view on the nature of place?
A) Place is a dimension in which the body exists.
B) Place is the inner surface of the containing body touching the contained body.
C) Place is the void or empty space.
D) Place is the outer surface of the containing body.

Key Takeaway
Place is commonly understood as a vessel, and one major view identifies it as the inner surface of the containing body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage reduces the world around you to surfaces and containers. Does this mathematical view of place increase your wonder at the precision of Allah's creation, or does it reduce the world to mere mechanics? How does your answer affect your gratitude and worship?
?
If your place in this world is defined by surfaces that touch you, what does that imply about your relationship to the One who created both you and your container? Will you seek to be contained by His mercy or by your own limited perception?

Place as Dimension: The Second View and Its Types
Second view: place as dimension, with its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second major view that place is a dimension, including its material and separable types.

Core Idea
Place is a dimension that can be material (in the body) or separable (body inheres in it).

After presenting the surface view, the author turns to the alternative: place is a dimension. This lesson unpacks that view, explaining that this dimension comes in two varieties: one that inheres in the body (the teaching body) and one that is separable, in which the body inheres. This sets up the further debate about the reality of the void.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the key term 'dimension' (al-bu'd).
2
Identify the two types of dimension
The passage mentions a material dimension and a separable dimension. Find where each is described.
The material dimension is called 'the teaching body'.
3
Understand the difference
Contrast the two: one inheres in the body and cannot coexist with another; the other does not inhere in a substrate but the body inheres in it.
This distinction is important for the void debate.
4
Note the proponents
The passage says this view is held by many philosophers and theologians. Reflect on why both groups might accept it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا خفاء في آنية شيء ينتقل الجسم عنه وإليه ويسكن فيه ولا يسع معه غيره وهو المسمى بالمكان. والمعتبر من المذاهب: أن ماهية السطح الباطن من الجسم الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من المحوى. وإليه ذهب أرسطو وأشياعه من المشائين. أو البعد الذي ينفذ فيه بعد الجسم ويتجدد به، وإليه ذهب كثير من الفلاسفة والمتكلمين. وزعموا أن من البعد ما هو مادي يحل في الجسم ويقوم به ويمتنع اجتماعه مع بعد آخر مماثل له قائم بذلك الجسم وهو المسمى بالجسم التعليمي، ومنه مفارق لا يقوم بمحل بل يحل فيه الجسم ويلاقيه بجملته ويجامعه بعد الجسم منطبقاً عليه متحداً به. إلا إنه عند المتكلمين عدم محض ونفي صرف يمكن أن لا يشغله شاغل وهو المعنى بالفراغ المتوهم الذي لو لم يشغله شاغل لكان فارغاً. وعند بعض الفلاسفة: امتداد موجود قد يكون ذراعاً وقد يكون أقل أو هذا الجسم وقد يسع ما هو أصغر منه أو أكبر وقد يسع. وتوضيحه أنا إذا توهمنا خلواً لإناء من الماء والهواء وغيرهما ففيما بين أطرافه امتداد قد يشغله الماء وقد يشغله الهواء فكذا عند الامتلاء ويسمونه البعد المفطور بمعنى أنه مشهور مفطور عليه البديهة فإن كل أحد يحكم بأن الماء فيما بين أطراف الإناء وقيل بمعنى أنه ينشق فيدخل فيه الجسم بما له من البعد. ويعبر عنه أفلاطون تارة بالهيولي لتوارد الأجسام عليه توارد الصور على المادة وتارة بالصورة لكونه عبارة عن الأبعاد الممتدة في الجهات بمنزلة الصورة الاتصالية الجسمية التي بها يقبل الجسم الأبعاد ويتميز عن المجردات. ثم إن هذا البعد عند أفلاطون وأتباعه ممتنع الخلو عن شاغل وعند البعض ممكن الخلو عنه وأصحاب الخلاء هم المتكلمون وبعض الفلاسفة. ففي هذا البحث مقامان: أحدهما في أن المكان هو السطح أو البعد وثانيهما في أن الخلاء ممكن أو ممتنع.
There is no hiddenness in that a vessel is something from which and to which a body moves, and in which it settles, and does not accommodate anything else with it, and it is called 'place' (al-makan). The considered among the schools [of thought] is that the essence of the inner surface of the containing body that touches the outer surface of the contained body. And this is the view of Aristotle and his followers among the Peripatetics. Or it is the dimension (al-bu'd) into which the body's dimension penetrates and is renewed by it, and this is the view of many philosophers and theologians (al-mutakallimun). They claimed that of the dimension there is a material one that inheres in the body and subsists by it, and it is impossible for it to gather with another like dimension subsisting in that body, and it is called 'the teaching body' (al-jism al-ta'līmī). And from it there is a separable one that does not subsist in a substrate, but rather the body inheres in it and meets it in its entirety, and it coexists with the body's dimension, conforming to it and united with it. Except that according to the theologians it is pure non-existence and absolute negation, which it is possible that no occupier occupies it, and it is meant by 'the imaginary void' (al-farāgh al-mutawahham), which if no occupier occupied it, it would be empty. And according to some philosophers: it is an existing extension which may be a cubit, or less, or this body, and it may accommodate what is smaller or larger than it. And its clarification: if we imagine a vessel empty of water and air and other things, then between its extremities there is an extension which water may occupy, or air may occupy, and likewise at the time of filling, and they call it 'the innate dimension' (al-bu'd al-mafṭūr), meaning it is well-known, innate to intuition, for everyone judges that water is between the vessel's extremities. And it is said meaning that it splits open, so the body enters into it by its dimension. And Plato expresses it sometimes as 'matter' (al-hayūlā) due to the succession of bodies upon it like the succession of forms upon matter, and sometimes as 'form' (al-ṣūra) because it is an expression of the dimensions extended in directions, akin to the continuous corporeal form by which the body receives dimensions and is distinguished from incorporeal beings. Then this dimension, according to Plato and his followers, is impossible to be empty of an occupier, and according to some it is possible to be empty of it, and the proponents of void (aṣḥāb al-khalā') are the theologians and some philosophers. So in this inquiry there are two stations: one concerning whether place is the surface or the dimension, and the second concerning whether the void is possible or impossible.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of place (al-makan) and presents two major positions, along with a discussion of the void (al-khalā'). It begins by stating the common understanding of place as a vessel into which a body moves, settles, and does not allow another body simultaneously. Then it presents two views: the first, attributed to Aristotle and the Peripatetics, identifies place as the inner surface of the containing body that touches the outer surface of the contained body. The second view, held by many philosophers and theologians (al-mutakallimun), identifies place as a dimension (al-bu'd) that the body's dimension enters and is renewed by. Within this second view, there are two types of dimension: a material dimension that inheres in the body and cannot coexist with another similar dimension (called 'the teaching body', al-jism al-ta'līmī), and a separable dimension that does not inhere in a substrate but rather the body inheres in it. Then the passage explains the different stances on whether this dimension is real or merely conceptual. For the theologians, it is pure non-existence and absolute negation, a potential void that could be unoccupied. For some philosophers, it is an existing extension that can be measured and can accommodate bodies of varying sizes, known as the 'innate dimension' (al-bu'd al-mafṭūr) because it is intuitively recognized. The passage also mentions Plato's terminology, who calls it matter (hayūlā) or form (ṣūra) depending on perspective. Finally, it introduces the two main issues to be investigated: first, whether place is the surface or the dimension; second, whether the void is possible or impossible. This sets up the subsequent discussion.

Key Terms
البعد
الجسم التعليمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that dimension is always physical and attached to the body.
Correction: The passage shows there is also a separable dimension that is not inherent in the body.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of dimension is called 'the teaching body'?
A) The separable dimension
B) The material dimension
C) Both types
D) Neither; it is a third type

Key Takeaway
The second view identifies place as a dimension, which can be either material (teaching body) or separable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If place can be a dimension that is separable from bodies, how does that expand your concept of existence? Are there realities you have dismissed because they do not fit into your physical understanding of space? What is one belief about the unseen that this insight challenges you to reconsider?
?
The teaching body (الجسم التعليمي) is a dimension that cannot coexist with another like dimension. What does this imply about the uniqueness and precision of Allah's creation? How does this make you appreciate the order in the universe around you?
?
This passage distinguishes between two types of dimension. How does this help you think about the nature of your own existence? Are you more attached to the material dimension or are you open to the reality of the separable dimension? What step can you take today to deepen your belief in the unseen realities that the intellect can grasp?

The Nature of the Dimension: Void, Existence, and the Two Questions
Stances on the reality of the dimension and the two inquiries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions on whether the separable dimension is non-existent or real, Plato's terminology, and the two main inquiries arising from this debate.

Core Idea
Theologians view the void as pure non-existence, while some philosophers see it as an existing extension.

This lesson delves into the deeper implications of the dimension view. We learn that theologians consider the separable dimension to be pure non-existence—a possible void—while some philosophers treat it as an actually existing extension. We also see Plato's terms for it and the two fundamental questions that structure the rest of the discussion: whether place is surface or dimension, and whether the void is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, paying attention to the different stances.
2
Compare theologians and philosophers
Identify what theologians say about the separable dimension and what some philosophers say.
Theologians call it pure non-existence; philosophers call it an existing extension.
3
Understand Plato's terminology
Find where Plato calls it 'matter' and 'form'. Note the reasons given.
This shows the philosophical depth.
4
Identify the two stations
The passage ends with two inquiries (مقامان). List them.
These structure the rest of the discussion.

لا خفاء في آنية شيء ينتقل الجسم عنه وإليه ويسكن فيه ولا يسع معه غيره وهو المسمى بالمكان. والمعتبر من المذاهب: أن ماهية السطح الباطن من الجسم الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من المحوى. وإليه ذهب أرسطو وأشياعه من المشائين. أو البعد الذي ينفذ فيه بعد الجسم ويتجدد به، وإليه ذهب كثير من الفلاسفة والمتكلمين. وزعموا أن من البعد ما هو مادي يحل في الجسم ويقوم به ويمتنع اجتماعه مع بعد آخر مماثل له قائم بذلك الجسم وهو المسمى بالجسم التعليمي، ومنه مفارق لا يقوم بمحل بل يحل فيه الجسم ويلاقيه بجملته ويجامعه بعد الجسم منطبقاً عليه متحداً به. إلا إنه عند المتكلمين عدم محض ونفي صرف يمكن أن لا يشغله شاغل وهو المعنى بالفراغ المتوهم الذي لو لم يشغله شاغل لكان فارغاً. وعند بعض الفلاسفة: امتداد موجود قد يكون ذراعاً وقد يكون أقل أو هذا الجسم وقد يسع ما هو أصغر منه أو أكبر وقد يسع. وتوضيحه أنا إذا توهمنا خلواً لإناء من الماء والهواء وغيرهما ففيما بين أطرافه امتداد قد يشغله الماء وقد يشغله الهواء فكذا عند الامتلاء ويسمونه البعد المفطور بمعنى أنه مشهور مفطور عليه البديهة فإن كل أحد يحكم بأن الماء فيما بين أطراف الإناء وقيل بمعنى أنه ينشق فيدخل فيه الجسم بما له من البعد. ويعبر عنه أفلاطون تارة بالهيولي لتوارد الأجسام عليه توارد الصور على المادة وتارة بالصورة لكونه عبارة عن الأبعاد الممتدة في الجهات بمنزلة الصورة الاتصالية الجسمية التي بها يقبل الجسم الأبعاد ويتميز عن المجردات. ثم إن هذا البعد عند أفلاطون وأتباعه ممتنع الخلو عن شاغل وعند البعض ممكن الخلو عنه وأصحاب الخلاء هم المتكلمون وبعض الفلاسفة. ففي هذا البحث مقامان: أحدهما في أن المكان هو السطح أو البعد وثانيهما في أن الخلاء ممكن أو ممتنع.

?

Spend a moment in prostration asking Allah to teach you what is beyond appearances.
?

Which of these do you find more unsettling: that the space you occupy might not be real, or that there could be empty voids beyond creation? Let your unease drive you to seek certainty in the Qur'an and Sunnah rather than in human speculation.

Definition of the Disputed Vacuum
Clarifies the exact meaning of vacuum under dispute and answers a question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of vacuum that is debated among theologians and how it can be understood as a pure negation.

Core Idea
The disputed vacuum is simply an unoccupied void, not necessarily an existent entity.

The debate about vacuum (al-khalā') often confuses students because different scholars mean different things. Sanusi clarifies exactly what is under dispute: a void that nothing occupies, regardless of whether we call it a dimension or consider it existent. Then he answers a key question about how those who deny its existence can still speak of its possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the two parts: the definition of vacuum and the question-answer pair.
The text is concise, so read carefully.
2
Identify the key distinctions
The definition includes two distinctions: whether vacuum is called a 'dimension' (bu'd) or not, and whether it is considered existent or imaginary. Understand that these distinctions cover different views.
3
Examine the question and answer
Focus on the question: how can one speak of the possibility of vacuum if it is pure negation? The answer reinterprets possibility as the ability of two bodies to exist without touching and without an intervening medium.
This shows that 'possibility' here does not mean vacuum itself exists, but that a certain configuration of bodies is possible.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this clarification is necessary for the broader debate about the existence of vacuum. It shows that the dispute is about the nature of void, not about whether it can be described.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المقام الثاني : المتنازع هو الخلاء بمعنى فراغ لا يشغله شاغل، سواء سمى بعداً أو لم يسم، وسواء جعل متحققاً موجوداً، أو موهوماً . فإن قيل : فما معنى (١) القول بإمكانه عند من جعله نفياً محضاً وعدماً صرفاً، لا يتحقق أصلا؟ قلنا : معناه أنه يمكن الجسمان، بحيث لا يتماسان ولا يكون بينهما ما يماسهما .
He said: The second station: The subject of dispute is vacuum (al-khalā') meaning a void (farāgh) that nothing occupies, whether it is called a dimension or not, and whether it is considered as actual and existent, or imaginary. So if it is said: What is the meaning of asserting its possibility for one who makes it a pure negation and pure non-existence, which is not actual at all? We say: Its meaning is that two bodies can [exist] such that they do not touch each other and there is nothing between them that touches them.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage defines the exact meaning of the vacuum under dispute. It is a void (farāgh) that has no occupant, regardless of whether it is labeled a 'dimension' (bu'd) or not, and regardless of whether it is considered real and existent or merely imaginary. Then a question is posed: If someone says vacuum is pure negation (nafy maḥḍ) and pure non-existence ('adam ṣirf), how can one speak of its possibility? The answer clarifies that the possibility means two bodies can exist without contacting each other and without any medium between them that touches them. Thus the disputed vacuum is simply the absence of any physical occupant between bodies.

Key Terms
خلاء (al-khalā')
فراغ (farāgh)
بعد (bu'd)
نفي محض (pure negation)
عدم صرف (pure non-existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think vacuum must be an existent entity to be discussed.
Correction: The passage shows that vacuum can be defined as a pure negation (nafy maḥḍ) and still be meaningfully discussed in terms of the possibility of bodies existing without contact.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the disputed meaning of vacuum?
A) An existent physical dimension
B) A void that nothing occupies
C) A space filled with air
D) A purely imaginary entity

Key Takeaway
The disputed vacuum is defined as an unoccupied void, and its possibility for negationists means two bodies can exist without physical contact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider an area of your life you view as empty or lacking. Do you treat that emptiness as a mere absence (like the vacuum defined here) or as a positive void that must be filled? How does your view affect your trust in Allah's decree?
?
The passage distinguishes between the possibility of a thing and the possibility of a state. In your own struggles, do you sometimes confuse the possibility of a situation with the existence of a problem? How can this distinction bring you peace?
?
If the disputed vacuum is pure non-existence for some scholars yet they still affirm its possibility, what does that teach you about the relationship between what you can conceive and what is real? How does this challenge you to re-evaluate your assumptions about unseen realities?

What Is Kalam?

Definition of the Disputed Concept of Void
Defines void and answers a key objection regarding its possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of void (khalaa) in Islamic kalam and its meaning for those who consider it a pure negation.

Core Idea
Void is not a thing; it is the possibility of bodies being separate without an intervening body.

In discussions of natural philosophy and theology, the concept of void (khalaa) has been controversial. The author first defines it clearly, then addresses a key question: if void is nothing, how can we say it is possible? The answer reveals that the possibility is not about void existing, but about bodies being separable. This lesson unpacks that.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without deep analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's words set the stage.
Notice that the passage begins with a response to the previous discussion on rarefaction of air.
2
Identify the definition of void
Find where the author defines void and note the key description: 'emptiness that no occupier occupies'. Also notice the qualifications about naming and existence.
3
Understand the question about possibility
The author presents a dialectical question: how can void be possible if it is considered pure negation and non-existence?
This question challenges the coherence of the concept.
4
Grasp the answer
The answer explains that 'possible' here means that two bodies can exist without touching and without anything between them. This is the meaning of possibility for those who see void as a pure negation.
The answer does not assert void's existence; it simply describes a relational state.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it allows affirming the possibility of empty space without committing to the existence of a real entity, which aligns with certain theological principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد بجواز تخلخل ما يبقى من قليل الهواء. قال: المقام الثاني: المتنازع هو الخلاء بمعنى فراغ لا يشغله شاغل، سواء سمي بعداً أو لم يسم، وسواء جعل متحققاً موجوداً، أو موهوماً. فإن قيل: فما معنى القول بإمكانه عند من جعله نفياً محضاً وعدماً صرفاً، لا يتحقق أصلا؟ قلنا: معناه أنه يمكن الجسمان، بحيث لا يتماسان ولا يكون بينهما ما يماسهما.
It was responded with the permissibility of rarefaction of what remains of little air. He said: The second station: the point of dispute is the void (khalaa) meaning an emptiness that no occupier occupies, whether it is called dimension or not, and whether it is considered actual and existent or imagined. Then if it is said: what is the meaning of saying it is possible for one who makes it a pure negation and pure non-existence, not actual at all? We said: its meaning is that two bodies can be [such that] they do not touch each other and there is nothing that touches them between them.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage begins with a response concerning the permissibility of rarefaction of a small amount of air. Then the author states that the second station concerns the disputed concept of void, which he defines as emptiness not occupied by any occupier, regardless of whether it is called a dimension or not, and regardless of whether it is considered actual existent or merely imagined. He then addresses a question: if someone considers void as a pure negation and pure non-existence, what does it mean to say it is possible? The answer: it means that two bodies can be separate such that they do not touch each other and there is no body between them. This clarifies that the possibility of void is understood as the possibility of separation without an intervening body.

Key Terms
الخلاء
فراغ
نفي محض
عدم صرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that void is impossible because it is a pure non-existence and cannot be said to be possible.
Correction: The author explains that possibility here refers to the state of two bodies being separate without an intervening body, not to the existence of void as a thing.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the meaning of the possibility of void for those who consider it a pure negation?
A) That void actually exists as a dimension.
B) That two bodies can be separate without an intervening body.
C) That void is created by God in the moment of separation.
D) That void is a pure non-existence and thus impossible.

Key Takeaway
The void is not a thing but a relational possibility: two bodies can be separate without anything in between.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author reframes the void as a relational possibility rather than a thing. In your own life, where have you mistaken a relation (e.g., distance from Allah, a gap in knowledge) for a fixed 'void' within you? How does this lesson challenge you to see it as a possibility for change?
?
If void is pure non-existence, then nothing is actually 'missing' — only bodies can be separate. When you feel a 'spiritual emptiness', are you treating it as a real entity that must be filled? How would your heart shift if you recognized it as merely the absence of connection, not a thing in itself?
?
The objection in the passage assumes that possibility implies existence. Where in your faith or doubts have you assumed that if something is 'possible' it must eventually happen or be real? What does this lesson teach you about the nature of possibility itself?

Definition of Quality (al-Kayf) and Its Refinements
Al-Taftazani defines quality and explains the problem of defining high genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the category of quality (al-kayf) in logic, why defining highest genera is challenging, and how the refinements protect the definition from counterexamples.

Core Idea
Quality is an accident that doesn't necessitate division or relation—but defining it is trickier than it seems.

Al-Taftazani's treatment of 'quality' is not just a dry definition; it reveals a deeper problem about how we define the most basic categories. In this passage, he first gives the standard definition, then explains why such definitions are incomplete, and finally refines it to avoid common pitfalls. Understanding these refinements will sharpen your logical thinking.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Taftazani uses a very technical style with many parenthetical clarifications.
2
Identify the two key parts of the definition
The passage first gives a standard definition: 'an accident that does not in itself necessitate division or relation.' Then it adds a potential increase: 'first: division.' Find these and note what they exclude.
The added 'first: division' is to exclude unity and the point.
3
Understand why highest genera are hard to define
The text explains that highest genera cannot be fully defined because they have no higher genus. Only incomplete definitions (rusūm nāqiṣa) are possible. See why the author says this leads to a search for a clearer property.
The author mentions the composite of accidentality and difference from quantity/relative accidents, but that is equal in clarity.
4
Follow the refinements and the objection/answer
The passage concludes with refinements concerning point and unity, temporal precedence, and an objection about qualities that seem to depend on other concepts. Work through each refinement to see how the definition is preserved.
The objection about knowledge and power is answered by distinguishing dependence from entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الفصل الثالث : في الكيف . هو عرض لا يقتضي لذاته قسمة أو نسبة، وقد يزاد : أولاً : قسمة احترازاً عن الوحدة والنقطة . قال : لا طريق إلى تعريف الأجناس العالية سوى الرسوم الناقصة، إذ لا لها جنس وهو ظاهر، ولأن التركب من الأمرين المتساويين ليكون كلاً يتصور منهما فصلاً مجرد احتمال عقلي لا يعرف تحققه، بل ربما تقام الدلالة على انتفائه أي انتفاء التركيب من الأمرين المتساويين. ولم يظفر للكيف بخاصة لازمه شاملة سوى المركب من العرضية والمغايرة للكم وللأعراض النسبية إلا أن التعريف بها كان تعريفاً للشيء بما يساويه في المعرفة والجهالة لأن الأجناس العالية ليس بعضها أجلى من البعض فعدلوا عن ذكر كل من الكم والأعراض النسبية إلى ذكر خاصته التي هي أجلى ، وقالوا : هو عرض لا يقتضي لذاته قسمة ، ولا يتوقف تصوره على تصور غيره، فخرج الجوهر والكم والأعراض النسبية، ومن جعل النقطة والوحدة من الأعراض زاد قيد عدم اقتضاء اللاقسمة احترازاً عنهما، وقيدوا عدم اقتضاء القسمة واللاقسمة بالذات والأولية لئلا يخرج عن التعريف العلم بالمركب وبالبسيط، حيث يقتضي القسمة واللاقسمة نظراً إلى المتعلق . فإن قيل: من الكيفيات ما يتوقف تعقله على تعقل شيء آخر كالعلم والقدرة، والاستقامة والانحناء، ونحو ذلك . قلنا ليس هذا يتوقف وإنما هو استلزام واستعقاب بمعنى أن تصوره يستلزم تصور غيره، فافترق الأمر.
He said: The third chapter: on Quality (al-Kayf). It is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that does not in itself necessitate division (qisma) or relation (nisba), and it may be increased [with the condition] first: division as a precaution against unity (waḥda) and the point (nuqṭa). He said: There is no way to define the highest genera (al-ajnās al-ʿāliya) except through incomplete definitions (rusūm nāqiṣa), since they have no genus, and that is apparent, and because composition from two equal things to form a whole from which a differentia (faṣl) is conceived is merely a rational possibility whose realization is unknown; indeed, evidence may be established for its negation—i.e., the negation of composition from two equal things. And they did not find for quality a necessary property inclusive of it other than the composite of accidentality and being different from quantity (al-kamm) and from relative accidents (al-aʿrāḍ al-nisbiyya), except that defining it that way would be defining a thing by something equal to it in knowledge and ignorance, because the highest genera are not clearer than one another. So they turned from mentioning both quantity and relative accidents to mentioning its own property which is clearer, and they said: It is an accident that does not in itself necessitate division, and whose conception does not depend on the conception of something else. Thus substance (jawhar), quantity, and relative accidents are excluded. And whoever makes the point and unity among accidents added the condition of not necessitating non-division as a precaution against them. And they restricted the lack of necessitating division and non-division to essence and primacy, lest knowledge of the composite and the simple be excluded from the definition, since it necessitates division and non-division with respect to the object. For it is said: Some qualities require in their intelligibility the intelligibility of something else, like knowledge and power, and straightness and curvature, and the like. We say: This is not dependence, but rather entailment and consequence, meaning that their conception entails the conception of something else; thus the matter is distinct.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a technical definition and refinement of the category of Quality (al-kayf) in Aristotelian logic. The author (al-Taftazani) begins by stating the standard definition: Quality is an accident that does not in itself necessitate division (i.e., it is not a quantity) or relation (i.e., it is not a relative accident). He notes that some add the condition 'first: division' to exclude unity and the point, which are accidents that are indivisible. Then he discusses the difficulty of defining the highest genera: they cannot be defined by a genus and differentia because they have no higher genus; only an incomplete definition (rasm nāqiṣ) by a proper property is possible. He explains that the only all-inclusive property found for quality is the composite of being an accident and being different from quantity and relative accidents, but that definition is equal in clarity to what it defines. Therefore they shifted to a clearer property: it is an accident whose conception does not depend on the conception of something else, and it does not necessitate division. This excludes substance (not an accident), quantity (necessitates division), and relative accidents (their conception depends on another). He then addresses a refinement: for those who consider point and unity as accidents, they add the condition 'does not necessitate non-division' to exclude them, and they restrict the negation of necessitating division or non-division to essence and primacy (i.e., not due to a relation to something else) so that knowledge of the composite (which necessitates division in its object) and knowledge of the simple (which necessitates non-division) are not mistakenly excluded. Finally, an objection is raised: some qualities like knowledge and power require understanding something else (the known object, the object of power). The answer is that this is not a dependence in the definition (tawaqquf) but merely an entailment (istilzām) — that is, the very understanding of such qualities necessarily implies understanding something else, but the quality itself is not defined by that other. The two concepts are distinct. Thus the definition holds.

Key Terms
الكيف (al-kayf)
عرض (ʿaraḍ)
قسمة (qisma)
نسبة (nisba)
الأجناس العالية (al-ajnās al-ʿāliya)
رسوم ناقصة (rusūm nāqiṣa)
العرضية (al-ʿaraḍiyya)
الكم (al-kamm)
الأعراض النسبية (al-aʿrāḍ al-nisbiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the definition 'its conception does not depend on something else' would exclude qualities like knowledge, which require an object.
Correction: The author clarifies that such qualities entail the conception of something else, but do not depend on it for their own quiddity. This is entailment (istilzām), not definitional dependence (tawaqquf).

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is it impossible to define the highest genera (al-ajnās al-ʿāliya) by a full definition (hadd tāmm)?
A) Because they have no specific differentia.
B) Because they have no genus above them.
C) Because they are simple concepts.
D) Because they are accidents.

Key Takeaway
The definition of quality is refined by excluding quantity and relative accidents, with careful caveats about divisibility and entailment to preserve logical clarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own understanding of Allah's attributes, do you often define them by what they are not (e.g., 'not limited') rather than by their true nature? How does this lesson's distinction between 'dependence' and 'entailment' challenge you to move beyond mere negation?
?
In your personal study of Islamic theology, do you tend to accept surface-level definitions (rusūm nāqiṣa) because they are comfortable? Where might you be settling for 'equal clarity' rather than pressing for a deeper grasp?
?
The author distinguishes between a quality's essence and its entailments. In your own life, are there beliefs or practices you hold that you justify by what they entail (e.g., 'it leads to good') rather than by their intrinsic worth? How can you shift to focus on essence?

The Definition of Quality and Its Exclusions
Al-Taftazani enumerates the definition and exclusions of quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of quality (kayfiyya) as an accident that does not entail division and whose conception does not depend on something else, and how this definition excludes substance, quantity, and relative accidents.

Core Idea
Quality is defined by what it does not entail: division, dependence on another concept.

In the classification of accidents, quality (kayfiyya) stands apart. The theologians defined it carefully to avoid overlap with substance, quantity, and relational accidents. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the key conditions: no division, no dependence.
Pay attention to the phrasing 'by itself' (لذاته).
2
Identify the exclusions
The passage explicitly excludes substance (جوهر), quantity (كم), and relative accidents (أعراض نسبية). Understand why each is excluded.
Also note the additional exclusion of point and unity.
3
Understand the qualification 'by essence and primacy'
This qualification prevents the definition from accidentally excluding knowledge of composite and simple things, which entail division or non-division but not by their own essence.
This shows the nuance in the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا : هو عرض لا يقتضي لذاته قسمة ، ولا يتوقف تصوره على تصور غيره، فخرج الجوهر والكم والأعراض النسبية، ومن جعل النقطة والوحدة من الأعراض زاد قيد عدم اقتضاء اللاقسمة احترازاً عنهما، وقيدوا عدم اقتضاء القسمة واللا قسمة بالذات والأولية لئلا يخرج عن التعريف العلم بالمركب وبالبسيط، حيث يقتضي القسمة واللاقسمة نظراً إلى المتعلق . فإن قيل: من الكيفيات ما يتوقف تعقله على تعقل شيء آخر كالعلم والقدرة، والاستقامة والانحناء، ونحو ذلك . قلنا ليس هذا يتوقف وإنما هو استلزام واستعقاب بمعنى أن تصوره يستلزم تصور متعلق له بخلاف النسبيات فإنها لا تتصور إلا بعد تصور المنسوب والمنسوب إليه . وبالجملة فالمعنى بالكيفية ما ذكر فلو كان شيء مما يعد في الكيفيات على خلاف ذلك لم يكن كيفية والمشهور في تعريف الكيفية أنها هيئة قارة يوجب تصورها تصور شيء خارج عنها، وعن حاملها ولا يقتضي قسمة ولا نسبة في أجزاء حاملها واحترز بالقيد الأخير عن الوضع، وبالأول أعني القارة عن الزمان أون يفعل وأن ينفعل واعترض بأن الاحتراز عن أن يفعل، وأن ينفعل حاصل بالقيد الثاني. وعن الزمان بالقيد الثالث، أعني عدم اقتضاء القسمة، على أن من الكيفيات ما ليست بقارة كالصوت، ومنها ما تصورها تصور أمر خارج كالعلم والقدرة على ما مر .
They said: It is an accident that does not entail by itself division, and its conception does not depend on the conception of something else. Thus substance, quantity, and relative accidents are excluded. Those who consider point and unity as accidents add the condition of not entailing indivisibility to avoid them. They qualified the non-entailment of division and non-division by essence and primacy so that knowledge of the composite and the simple does not leave the definition, since it entails division and non-division with respect to the object. If it is said: Some qualities require the conception of something else, like knowledge, power, straightness, curvature, and the like. We say: This is not dependence; rather it is entailment and sequel, meaning that its conception entails the conception of its object, unlike relationals, which are not conceived except after conceiving the related and that to which it relates. In short, the meaning of quality is what was mentioned; if something counted among qualities were contrary to that, it would not be a quality. The well-known definition of quality is that it is a stable state whose conception necessitates the conception of something external to it and to its bearer, and does not entail division or proportion in the parts of its bearer. The last condition guards against position; the first, i.e., stable, against time, or acting and being acted upon. It is objected that the guard against acting and being acted upon is achieved by the second condition, and against time by the third, i.e., non-entailment of division, although some qualities are not stable, like sound, and some entail the conception of an external thing, like knowledge and power, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage defines the category of quality (kayfiyya) and handles an objection about qualities that seem to depend on something else for their conception. The author begins by restating the definition: quality is an accident that does not entail division by itself, and its conception does not depend on conceiving something else. This excludes substance (which entails division through magnitude), quantity (which involves division), and relative accidents (which require two relata). Some scholars add a condition to also exclude point and unity if considered accidents, since they entail indivisibility. The non-entailment of division or indivisibility is qualified as 'by essence and primacy' so that knowledge of a composite or simple thing (which entails division or non-division of its object) is not mistakenly excluded. Then an objection is raised: some qualities, like knowledge, power, straightness, and curvature, require conceiving something else (e.g., the known, the object of power, the line, etc.). The answer is that this is not dependence but entailment (istilzam) – the conception of the quality itself entails the conception of its object, unlike relational accidents (nisbiyyat), which cannot be conceived without first conceiving both relata. The author then summarizes that if anything counted as a quality violated the definition, it would not truly be a quality. He then gives the more common definition from other works: quality is a stable (qarra) state whose conception necessitates the conception of something external to itself and to its bearer, and it does not entail division or proportion in its bearer's parts. The conditions guard against position (al-wad') and against time, acting, and being acted upon. An objection is noted that guarding against acting/being acted upon is already achieved by the condition of stability, and guarding against time by the condition of non-entailment of division, yet some qualities like sound are not stable, and some like knowledge entail an external thing. The author acknowledges this complexity, indicating that the standard definition has limitations.

Key Terms
كيفية (quality)
قسمة (division)
توقف (dependence)
جوهر (substance)
كم (quantity)
أعراض نسبية (relative accidents)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that knowledge of a composite is excluded by the definition because it entails division of its object.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the entailment is not by the essence of knowledge but by its object, so knowledge remains a quality.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is explicitly excluded from the definition of quality according to this passage?
A) Knowledge of the composite
B) Substance
C) Sound
D) Straightness

Key Takeaway
Quality is defined by not entailing division by itself and not depending on another conception, with careful qualifications to preserve genuine qualities like knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own knowledge is a quality that does not entail division by itself, yet your mind is limited and divided. When you learn about Allah, do you strive to have direct, unmediated knowledge, or do you rely on the opinions of others? Will you pick one attribute of Allah today and contemplate it directly in your prayer, without any commentary?
?
The definition of quality excludes any accident whose conception depends on another concept. Your faith in Allah should not depend on your circumstances or feelings. Are there aspects of your iman that fluctuate because they rely on external factors? What is one concrete action you can take today to make your faith more independent of your mood?
?
The scholars carefully qualified the definition to preserve knowledge of the composite and simple. Your own understanding of theological matters is often composite (learned piece by piece). Are you content with piecemeal understanding, or do you seek a simple, direct certainty in the oneness of Allah? Today, in your sujood, ask Allah for the gift of direct, simple knowledge of Him.

The Objection about Dependence and the Well-Known Definition
Al-Taftazani answers the objection and defines quality in the well-known manner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between genuine dependence (tawaqquf) and mere entailment (istilzam) in the conception of qualities, and the standard definition of quality as a stable state that necessitates an external concept.

Core Idea
Some qualities seem to depend on something else, but that is entailment, not dependence.

An objection arises: many qualities, like knowledge and power, require conceiving something else. The theologians clarify this is entailment—a necessary connection—not dependence. Then a more common definition of quality is given, along with its conditions and critiques.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the objection carefully: some qualities require conceiving something else (e.g., knowledge requires knowing the known). Then read the answer distinguishing dependence from entailment.
Note the contrast with relational accidents (nisbiyyat) that truly depend.
2
Understand the well-known definition
The author then presents the common definition: quality is a stable state whose conception necessitates an external thing, and it does not entail division or proportion in its bearer.
Note the conditions that guard against position, time, action/passion.
3
Consider the critiques
The passage ends with critiques: some qualities are not stable (sound), and some necessitate an external thing (knowledge). This shows the definition is not perfect.
Reflect on why the theologians still use this definition.

The author then summarizes that if anything counted as a quality violated the definition, it would not truly be a quality. He then gives the more common definition from other works: quality is a stable (qarra) state whose conception necessitates the conception of something external to itself and to its bearer, and it does not entail division or proportion in its bearer's parts. The conditions guard against position (al-wad') and against time, acting, and being acted upon. An objection is noted that guarding against acting/being acted upon is already achieved by the condition of stability, and guarding against time by the condition of non-entailment of division, yet some qualities like sound are not stable, and some like knowledge entail an external thing. The author acknowledges this complexity, indicating that the standard definition has limitations.

Key Terms
استلزام (entailment)
استعقاب (sequel)
هيئة قارة (stable state)
وضع (position)
عرض (accident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any quality that requires another concept for understanding is dependent (mutawaqqif) on that concept.
Correction: The passage clarifies that this is entailment (istilzam), not dependence. The concept itself entails the other; it does not require prior conception.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is knowledge not considered dependent on something else for its conception?
A) Because knowledge is not a quality.
B) Because its conception entails the conception of its object, not depends on it.
C) Because knowledge is a stable state.
D) Because it does not entail anything.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between dependence and entailment preserves the definition of quality while accounting for concepts like knowledge that necessarily involve another notion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your knowledge of Allah, do you treat it as dependent on your own reasoning (tawaqquf) or as entailed by His reality (istilzam)? Does your answer reveal how secure your faith truly is?
?
The author admits the definition of quality has exceptions yet it is accepted. Is there a belief about yourself that you hold despite obvious contradictions? What would it cost you to examine that belief carefully today?
?
Think of a time you confused entailment with dependence—where you thought you needed something before you could act, but actually the action itself entailed the thing. How might that confusion be holding you back from obedience to Allah right now?
