Division of Sensible Qualities: Rooted and Fleeting
Al-Taftazani enumerates the two kinds of sensible qualities and their characteristics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of sensible qualities into rooted (rāsikha) and non-rooted (ghayr rāsikha) types, and the reasons behind their respective names 'passive qualities' (infi'āliyyāt) and 'passions' (infi'ālāt).

Core Idea
Sensible qualities are either rooted and permanent or fleeting and passion-like.

Al-Taftazani now introduces the first division of qualities: those perceived by the senses. He explains that sensible qualities can be stable or transient, and this distinction determines their technical names. This classification is foundational for understanding how physical properties are discussed in Islamic theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two categories
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to see how al-Taftazani divides sensible qualities into rooted and non-rooted.
2
Understand the naming convention
Notice why rooted qualities are called passive qualities (infi'āliyyāt) and fleeting ones are called passions (infi'ālāt). The reasons involve their relationship to the senses and temperament.
3
Reflect on the examples
The examples of yellowness of gold, heat of fire, and whiteness of snow help illustrate the rooted qualities. Consider how these differ from fleeting qualities.

(قال) : القسم الأول : الكيفيات المحسوسة.
وهي أنواع :
النوع الأول : الملموسات وفيه مباحث).
وأجيب بأن البحث فيه قد يقع من حيث الافتقار إلى المادة، وهو بحث الوحدة والكثرة من الإلهي، وقد يقع من حيث الافتقار كالجمع والتفريق والضرب، ونحو ذلك مما في الحساب وهو من الرياضي، وفيه نظر لا يقال : المراد ما يعلق بالجسم في الجملة وإن لم يختص به، وكيفيات العدد كذلك، لأنا نقول حينئذ يكون معنى كون الكيفيات النفسانية ما لا يتعلق بالجسم، أنها لا تتعلق به أصلاً وفساده بين بل بمعنى أنها لا تتعلق به خاصة، بحيث تستغني عن النفس .

قال : القسم الأول : الكيفيات المحسوسة وهي إن كانت راسخة كصفرة الذهب، وحلاوة العسل، سميت انفعاليات لانفعال الحواس عنها أولاً، ولكونها بخصوصها أو عمومها تابعة للمزاج الحاصل من انفعال العناصر بموادها، فالخصوص كما في المركبات مثل حلاوة العسل والعموم كما في البسائط، مثل حرارة النار فإن الحرارة من حيث هي قد تكون تابعة للمزاج، ولانفعال المواد وهذا معنى قولهم تشخصها أو نوعها وإلا فالحرارة ليست نوعاً لحرارة النار، وغيرها، لا حقيقياً ولا إضافياً، وكذا البياض لبياض الثلج والعاج على ما سيجيء، وإن كانت غير راسخة سميت انفعالات، لأنها لسرعة زوالها شديدة الشبه بأن ينفعل، فخصت بهذا الاسم تمييزاً بين القسمين .
(۱) في (ب) القسم بدلاً من (الاسم) .
(He said): The first division: sensible qualities. And they are types: The first type: tangible things, and in it are discussions. And it was answered that investigation into it may occur from the perspective of need for matter, and that is the investigation of unity and multiplicity from the divine [science]; and it may occur from the perspective of need such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, and the like from arithmetic, and that is from the mathematical [science]. And there is consideration. It cannot be said: what is intended is what attaches to body in general even if not specific to it, and qualities of number likewise. For we then say: the meaning of psychic qualities being what does not attach to body is that they do not attach to it at all, and its falsity is clear. Rather, it means that they do not attach to it specifically, such that they are independent of the soul. (1) Unity is the opposite of multiplicity, because it is the thing being such that it is not divisible, and multiplicity is its being such that it is divisible. And unity in the philosophy of Ibn Sina is among the concomitants of essences, not among their constituents. He said: it has become clear through these three aspects—one of which is that unity is not essential to substances but necessary to them, the second is that unity succeeds multiplicity in matter, and the third is that unity is predicated of accidents—that the nature of unity is an accidental nature, and likewise the nature of number which follows unity and is composed from it. Refer to al-Najat p. 341. He said: The first division: sensible qualities. And if they are rooted, like the yellowness of gold and the sweetness of honey, they are called passive qualities (infi'āliyyāt) due to the senses being first affected by them, and because they, whether specifically or generally, are dependent on the temperament resulting from the impression of the elements upon their matters. Specificity is as in compounds like the sweetness of honey, and generality as in simple bodies like the heat of fire; for heat as such may be dependent on temperament and on the impression of matters. And this is the meaning of their statement: their individuation or their type. Otherwise, heat is not a type for the heat of fire and others, neither real nor relative. Likewise whiteness for the whiteness of snow and ivory, as will come. And if they are not rooted, they are called passions (infi'ālāt), because due to their rapid passing they strongly resemble that which is affected, so they were distinguished with this name to differentiate between the two divisions. (1) In (B): 'division' instead of 'name'.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage defines and divides sensible qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-maḥsūsa). It begins with an initial heading stating 'the first division: sensible qualities', then lists types starting with tangible things (al-malmūsāt). An objection and response follow: the investigation of tangible things may be considered from the perspective of dependence on matter (related to divine science discussing unity and multiplicity) or from arithmetic operations like addition and subtraction (mathematical science). The objection that qualities are merely what attaches to body in general is refuted; psychic qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya) are independent of body. A footnote inserts a definition of unity (al-waḥda) and multiplicity from Ibn Sīnā's philosophy, clarifying that unity is an accidental concomitant of essences. Then the main division is stated: sensible qualities are either rooted (rāsikha) or non-rooted (ghayr rāsikha). Rooted qualities, like the yellowness of gold and sweetness of honey, are called infi'āliyyāt (passive qualities) because the senses are first affected by them, and they depend on the temperament (mizāj) resulting from the impression of elements. This dependency can be specific (as in compounds) or general (as in simple bodies like fire's heat). The heat of fire and whiteness of snow are given as examples. Non-rooted qualities are called infi'ālāt (passions) because they are fleeting and resemble passive impressions. The term 'passions' distinguishes them from the more permanent 'passive qualities'. A textual variant note indicates that in some manuscripts 'division' (qism) replaces 'name' (ism).

الراسخة
غير الراسخة
الانفعاليات
الانفعالات
المزاج
انفعال الحواس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sensible qualities are equally permanent.
Correction: Al-Taftazani distinguishes between rooted qualities that are stable and non-rooted qualities that are fleeting.

D) Infi'āliyyāt are divine; infi'ālāt are mathematical.

Key Takeaway
Sensible qualities are divided into rooted (permanent) and non-rooted (fleeting), with the technical names reflecting their relation to sensory perception and temperament.

Mu'tazilite Classification of I'timad and Key Disputes
Matn quotation classifying i'timad and listing three disputed issues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite classification of i'timad into lazim and mujtalab, and identify three internal disagreements among them.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila disagreed on i'timad's types, persistence, and generative power.

This passage from the matn introduces a key Mu'tazilite concept—i'timad (reliance/inclination)—and highlights that even within the Mu'tazila, there were significant disagreements. We'll examine their classification and three debated issues.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words guide your understanding.
Notice the enumeration pattern.
2
Identify the two categories of i'timad
The Mu'tazila classify i'timad into natural (طبيعي) and non-natural. They call natural i'timad 'lazim' and other 'mujtalab'. Recognize this basic division.
The terms lazim and mujtalab are technical.
3
List the three disagreements
The passage mentions three points of dispute: the i'timad of air, whether lazim persists like mujtalab, and whether motion/rest are generated from i'timad. Write them down.
Each disagreement is marked by 'وفي'.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why these internal disagreements matter for Mu'tazilite theology. They show that the school was not monolithic.
This connects to the common misunderstanding.
5
Take the checkpoint quiz
Answer the multiple-choice question to test your comprehension of the passage's factual content.
The answer is based solely on the passage.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قال: والمعتزلة يسمون الطبيعي من الاعتماد لازماً وغيره مجتلباً (۱) ولهم اختلاف في أن الاعتماد الصاعد للهواء لازم أو يجتلب وفي أن اللازم هل هو باق أم لا (٢) ..؟ كالمجتلب وفي أنه هل [ يتولد من الاعتماد حركة وسكون فقيل : لا وقيل يتولد ] (٣) منه أشياء من الحركات والسكنات وغيرهما بعضها لذاته بشرط أو غير شرط وبعضها لا لذاته) .
(He said: The Mu'tazila call the natural [type] of i'timad 'lazim' and other than it 'mujtalab'. And they have disagreement concerning whether the ascending i'timad of air is lazim or mujtalab; and concerning whether the lazim is enduring or not, like the mujtalab; and concerning whether motion and rest are generated from i'timad – it is said: no, and it is said: things are generated from it, such as motions and rests and other things, some of them essentially with or without condition and some of them not essentially.)
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from the matn (the original text being commented upon). It states the Mu'tazilite classification of i'timad into two types: natural (طبيعي), which they call lazim (لازم, necessary/constant), and non-natural (غير طبيعي), which they call mujtalab (مجتلب, acquired). It then lists three points of disagreement among the Mu'tazila: (1) whether the upward i'timad of air is lazim or mujtalab; (2) whether the lazim type persists (باق) just as the mujtalab may or may not; and (3) whether i'timad generates (تولد) motion and rest, with two opinions: some say it does not generate at all, others say it generates various things (motions, rests, etc.), some essentially with or without conditions and some not essentially. The passage is purely enumerative and sets the stage for the detailed commentary that follows.

The Three Types of Inclination (Mayl)
Classification of mayl into three types with definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of inclination (mayl) into natural, forced, and psychic, and the rationale behind each category based on its originating principle.

Core Idea
Inclination is defined by its source: external, conscious, or inherent.

This passage introduces a foundational classification in natural philosophy: the division of inclination (mayl) into three types. Understanding this taxonomy helps explain motion in both animate and inanimate objects. The author presents the standard division and notes alternative views, showing the depth of scholarly debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the definitions of each type of mayl.
The passage uses technical terms; refer to the key terms list.
2
Identify the three types
Find the three categories: forced, psychic, and natural. Notice how each is defined by its principle (mabda') and whether it involves intent or external cause.
Forced: external cause; Psychic: with intent; Natural: internal without intent.
3
Examine sub-classifications
Note that natural inclination is further divided into uniform (e.g., stone falling) and variable (e.g., plant growth). Also observe the alternative terms like 'voluntary' (irādī).
This shows the complexity in classifying motions.
4
Reflect on significance
Consider why this classification matters for understanding causality and motion in natural philosophy. How does it help explain different phenomena?
It provides a framework for analyzing all types of motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي الاعتماد ميلا ويقسمونه إلى الطبيعي والقسري والنفساني، لأن مبدأه وما ينبعث هو عنه، إن كان أمراً خارجاً عن محله فقسري كميل السهم المرمي إلى فوق، وإلا فإن كان مع قصد وشعور فنفساني كاعتماد الإنساني على غيره، وإلا فطبيعي سواء اقتضته القوة على وتيرة واحدة أبداً، كميل الحجر المسكن في الجو، أو اقتضته على وتائر مختلفة كميل النبات إلى التبرز والتزيد، (۱) ومنهم من سمى المقرون بالقصد والشعور إرادياً وجعل النفساني أعم منه ومن وتر أحد (۲) قسمي الطبيعي، أعني ما لا يكون على وتيرة واحدة لاختصاصه بذوات الأنفس فربما يختلف على حسب اقتضاء النفس، فبهذا الاعتبار يسمى ميل النبات نفسانياً، ومنهم من جعله خارجاً على الأقسام لكونه مركباً على ما سيأتي في بحث الحركة مع زيادة كلام في هذا الباب.
That is, reliance is inclination (mayl), and they divide it into natural (ṭabīʿī), forced (qasrī), and psychic (nafsānī), because its principle and that from which it emanates: if it is something external to its place, then it is forced, like the inclination of an arrow shot upward; otherwise, if it is with intent and awareness, then it is psychic, like a human's reliance on another; otherwise, it is natural, whether the force necessitates it on one uniform pattern forever, like the inclination of a stone suspended in the air, or necessitates it on varying patterns, like the inclination of a plant toward growth and increase. (1) And among them are those who called what is accompanied by intent and awareness 'voluntary' (irādī) and made 'psychic' more general than it and than one (2) of the two subdivisions of natural, namely what is not on a uniform pattern, because of its specificity to ensouled beings, so perhaps it varies according to the requirement of the soul; thus in this respect the inclination of a plant is called psychic. And among them are those who made it outside the divisions, because it is composite, as will come in the discussion of motion, with additional remarks in this chapter.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies mayl (inclination), a key concept in natural philosophy. The author states that mayl is divided into three types based on its originating principle: forced (qasrī) when the cause is external to the object (e.g., an arrow shot upward), psychic (nafsānī) when it arises from conscious intent (e.g., a person leaning on something), and natural (ṭabīʿī) when it arises from the object's own nature without external cause or consciousness. Natural inclination either acts uniformly (like a stone's tendency to fall) or variably (like a plant's growth). The author notes variant classifications: some call the conscious type 'voluntary' and make 'psychic' broader; some consider psychic as a subdivision of natural due to its variability in living things; and some exclude it entirely as composite. This shows the technical debates around the taxonomy of motions.

Key Terms
ميل (mayl)
طبيعي (ṭabīʿī)
قسري (qasrī)
نفساني (nafsānī)
إرادي (irādī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural inclination is always uniform and invariable.
Correction: Natural inclination can be variable, as seen in plant growth, which follows different patterns over time.

Checkpoint

B) Forced (qasrī)
C) Psychic (nafsānī)

Key Takeaway
Mayl is classified into forced, psychic, and natural based on whether its principle is external, conscious, or inherent, with natural further divided into uniform and variable.

Intrinsic Radiance vs. Accidental Glitter
Taftazani defines and distinguishes shu'a' and bariq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between intrinsic radiance (shu'a') and accidental glitter (bariq) as defined by Taftazani in the context of kalam.

Core Idea
Intrinsic radiance flows from the object; accidental glitter is borrowed light.

Taftazani introduces two technical terms for light phenomena that will be used in later kalam discussions. He distinguishes between light that is part of the object's nature and light that merely appears on it. This passage establishes the vocabulary for discussing optical and ontological properties in theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the descriptive language of light glistening and flowing.
The passage contains a direct quote from an earlier authority (he said).
2
Identify the two types of light
Locate the terms shu'a' and bariq. See that they both describe a particular visual phenomenon: light that seems to flow from a body and almost conceals it.
3
Note the distinguishing criterion
The key distinction is whether the light is intrinsic (dhati) like the sun, or accidental ('aradi) like a mirror. Intrinsic light is called shu'a', accidental light is called bariq.
Intrinsic means the object itself is the source; accidental means it is borrowed or reflected.
4
Reflect on the examples
The sun's light is intrinsic; light from a mirror is accidental. Think of other examples to test the definition.
This distinction is used in kalam to discuss attributes and their relation to essence.
5
Consider the purpose
Ask why such precise definitions matter. They prevent confusion in theological arguments that rely on the difference between essential and accidental properties.
This connects to broader discussions on the nature of God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضوء قد يكون شعاعاً وقد يكون بريقاً (قال : إذا كان الضوء يترقرق على الجسم حتى كأنه يفيض منه ويكاد يستره يسمى الذاتي شعاعاً، كما للشمس، والعرضي بريقاً كما للمرآة) . (١) كأنه شيء يفيض قد يشاهد للضوء ترقرق وتلألؤ على الجسم حتى منه ويضطرب مجيئاً وذهاباً، بحيث يكاد يستره، فإن كان ذاتياً كما للشمس سمي شعاعاً، وإن كان عرضياً كما للمرآة سمي بريقاً .
Light may be a radiance (shu'a') and may be a glitter (bariq). (He said: When light glistens on a body until it seems to flow from it and almost conceals it, the intrinsic (dhati) is called radiance (shu'a'), as for the sun, and the accidental ('aradi) is called glitter (bariq), as for a mirror.) (1) As if it is something that flows. Light may be observed glistening and glittering on a body until it flows from it and fluctuates coming and going, such that it almost conceals it. If it is intrinsic, as for the sun, it is called shu'a', and if it is accidental, as for a mirror, it is called bariq.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage defines two terms related to light: shu'a' and bariq. The author begins by stating that light can be of two types. He then quotes a definition: when light glistens on a body until it seems to flow from it and almost conceals it, if that light is intrinsic (dhati) to the object, like the sun, it is called shu'a' (radiance); if it is accidental ('aradi), like light reflecting from a mirror, it is called bariq (glitter). Then he elaborates: one may observe light appearing to flow and fluctuate, and if it is intrinsic to the object it is shu'a', if not it is bariq. The key distinction is whether the light originates from the object itself (intrinsic) or comes from elsewhere (accidental).

Key Terms
ضوء (daw')
شعاع (shu'a')
بريق (bariq)
ذاتي (dhati)
عرضي ('aradi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shu'a' and bariq are synonyms for any bright light.
Correction: The passage distinguishes them: shu'a' is intrinsic light (like the sun), bariq is accidental light (like a mirror's reflection).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes shu'a' from bariq?
A) Shu'a' is brighter than bariq.
B) Shu'a' is intrinsic, bariq is accidental.
C) Shu'a' is accidental, bariq is intrinsic.
D) They are the same thing.

Key Takeaway
Light that flows from a body intrinsically is shu'a'; light that appears accidentally is bariq.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something good you do regularly. Do you do it because it flows from your true self (shu'a') or because you are reflecting external expectations (bariq)? How can you cultivate more shu'a' in your life?
?
The sun's light is intrinsic; it does not need anything to shine. In your relationship with Allah, are you like the sun, or are you like a mirror that only shines when light comes from elsewhere? What dependency do you need to break?
?
If your entire life were stripped of all external influences, what would remain of your faith? Would it be shu'a' or bariq?

Refutation of the Theory That Light Consists of Small Moving Particles
Taftazani refutes the particle theory of light and gives the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that light is composed of self-moving particles and the beginning of Taftazani's refutation.

Core Idea
Apparent motion of light is not body motion but repeated creation.

This passage from Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid tackles a classical philosophical theory about the nature of light. Some philosophers argued that because light appears to move—descending from the sun, following a moving lamp, reflecting off surfaces—it must consist of tiny bodies. Taftazani introduces this view and then immediately begins to dismantle it, starting with the logical impossibility of a single body moving in multiple directions at once.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first the imagined view, then the philosophers' argument, then the author's response.
The passage ends mid-proof; focus on the logical structure so far.
2
Identify the philosophers' syllogism
The philosophers use a syllogism: light is self-moving, every self-moving thing is a body, therefore light is a body. Break down the major and minor premises.
They support the minor premise with three examples: descent, following, reflection.
3
Understand the author's initial response
Taftazani denies that these phenomena involve motion; they are instead new occurrences of light. He calls the notion of motion an illusion.
This sets up the proofs that follow.
4
Examine the first proof
The first proof argues that if light were a single moving body, it could not move in different directions (descent, following, reflection) simultaneously. That is impossible.
The proof is incomplete here, but the key logical point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع: حدوث الضوء في المستضيء من مضيء (قال) : المبحث الرابع: لما كان حدوث الضوء في المستضيء قد يكون من مضيء عال أو متحرك أو متوسط بينه وبين المضيء بالذات توهم أن الضوء نفسه يتحرك انحداراً أو اتباعاً أو انعكاساً ، فهو أجسام صغار تنفصل من المضيء ويتصل بالمستضيء، ويبطله أنه لا يعقل الحركة بالطبع إلى جهات مختلفة، ولا الحركة في لحظة من فلك الشمس إلى الأرض مع خرق للأفلاك، ولا كون ما وراء الجسم المحسوس أظهر للباصرة السليمة) . زعم بعض الحكماء : أن الضوء أجسام صغار تنفصل من المضيء وتتصل بالمستضيء تمسكاً بأنه متحرك بالذات وكل متحرك بالذات جسم، أما الكبرى فظاهرة وإنما قيدنا بالذات لأن الأعراض تتحرك بتبعية المحل، وأما الصغرى فلأن الضوء ينحدر من الشمس إلى الأرض. ويتبع المضيء في الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان كما يشاهد في السراج المنقول من موضع إلى موضع ، وينعكس مما يلقاه إلى غيره، وكل ذلك حركة . والجواب المنع بل كل ذلك حدوث للضوء في المقابل للمضيء والحركة وهم ، ويدل على بطلان هذا الرأي وجوه . الأول : أنه لو كان جسماً متحركاً لامتنع حركته إلى جهات مختلفة ضرورة
The Fourth Investigation: The Occurrence of Light in the Illuminated from a Luminous Source. (He said): The Fourth Investigation: Since the occurrence of light in the illuminated may be from a luminous source that is high, moving, or intermediate between it and the intrinsically luminous, it was imagined that light itself moves in descent, following, or reflection, so it is small bodies that separate from the luminous source and connect to the illuminated. And it is invalidated by the fact that motion by nature toward different directions is not conceivable, nor motion in an instant from the sphere of the sun to the earth with penetration of the spheres, nor that what is beyond the sensible body is more apparent to the healthy eye. Some philosophers claimed that light is small bodies that separate from the luminous source and connect to the illuminated, holding that it is self-moving (mutaharrik bi al-dhat) and every self-moving thing is a body. As for the major premise, it is obvious; and we restricted it to 'self' because accidents move by following their substrate. As for the minor premise: because light descends from the sun to the earth, and it follows the luminous source in moving from place to place as observed in the lamp moved from one place to another, and it reflects from what it encounters to something else, and all of that is motion. The answer is denial; rather all that is the occurrence of light in the opposite of the luminous source, and motion is an illusion. And there are proofs for the falsehood of this view. The first: if it were a moving body, its motion toward different directions would be impossible necessarily.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents the Fourth Investigation on the occurrence of light. The author begins by explaining the reason some philosophers mistakenly think light moves: because light appears to descend, follow the source, and reflect. They then argue that light is composed of tiny particles that separate from the luminous source and attach to the illuminated, and that these particles are bodies because they are self-moving. They support this with examples like light descending from the sun, following a moving lamp, and reflecting off objects. The author refutes this by saying that all these phenomena are actually new occurrences of light in the opposite direction, not motion. He then promises several proofs for the falsehood of this view, and begins the first proof: if light were a moving body, it could not move in different directions simultaneously. The argument is cut off, but the idea is that a single body cannot have motion toward multiple directions at once, which is required for light to descend, follow, and reflect.

Key Terms
ضوء (daw')
متحرك بالذات (mutaharrik bi al-dhat)
جسم (jism)
انحدار (inhidar)
انعكاس (in'ikas)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Light itself is a moving body because it appears to travel.
Correction: Taftazani argues that the appearance of motion is actually new occurrences of light at different locations, not the translation of a body.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' argument for light being small moving bodies?
A) Light has color.
B) Light is self-moving and every self-moving thing is a body.
C) Light is an accident.
D) Light is like a shadow.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument that light is a moving body is refuted by showing that apparent motion is actually new occurrences, and that a single body cannot move in multiple directions simultaneously.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the sun's light streaming into a room, do you passively accept it as a continuous physical phenomenon, or does this lesson challenge you to see each ray as a direct, moment-by-moment act of Allah's creation? What would change in your awareness if you truly internalized that?
?
The philosophers relied on observable patterns to conclude that light is a moving body, yet Taftazani revealed a deeper truth beyond appearances. Where in your own life are you overly confident in your perceptions or reasoning about a matter of faith, without considering that the underlying reality might be different from what you assume?
?
If light's apparent motion is actually a series of discrete creations, then every glimpse of light is a direct gift from Allah. How often do you thank Him for the simple act of seeing? Starting today, will you pause at the first light you see each morning and acknowledge it as a fresh creation from Him?

The Nature of Sound: Muslim and Philosophical Views
Author defines sound and provides evidence for its external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Muslim view that sound is directly created by Allah and the philosophers' view that it arises from vibrations, as well as the rational evidence for sound's external existence.

Core Idea
Sound is either created by Allah or arises from vibration, but both agree it is externally perceived.

This lesson presents two competing accounts of sound. The author first contrasts the Muslim theological position with the philosophers' view, then provides a subtle argument to prove that sound exists externally and is perceived through our senses. This establishes a foundation for understanding broader theological discussions about causality and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of the author's style. Then read the English translation to understand the content.
Notice that the author uses very precise technical language.
2
Compare the two definitions
Identify the Muslim definition (عندنا) and the philosophical definition (عند الفلاسفة). Note the key terms: 'خلق' vs. 'كيفية تحدث بالتموج'.
3
Analyse the evidence
The author provides an argument for sound's external existence based on perception of direction and distance. Work through the logic: why does he reject the alternative explanations?
This is a rational proof, not a revealed one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول : الصوت عندنا بمحض خلق الله تعالى كسائر الحوادث وعند الفلاسفة : كيفية تحدث في الهواء بالتموج المعلول للقرع ، أو القلع، لا نفس التموج أو القرع أو القلع على ما ظن لأنها ليست مسموعة، ويدل على وجوده في الخارج، وتعلق الإحساس به هناك أيضاً إدراك جهته والتمييز بين قريبه وبعيده، وليس ذلك لمجيء الهواء القارع من تالك الجهة أو لقوة القرع القريب وضعف البعيد وإلا لما أدرك كونه من الجانب المخالف للأذن السامعة، ولما ميز بين القوى البعيد والضعيف القريب.
من الكيفيات المحسوسة المسوعات وهي الأصوات والحروف والصوت عندنا يحدث بمحض خلق الله تعالى من غير تأثير لتموج الهواء والقرع والقلع كسائر الحوادث وكثيراً ما تورد الآراء الباطلة للفلاسفة من غير تعرض لبيان
(۱) الصوت : عند بعض العلماء، هو الهواء المنضغط عن قرع جسمين، والصوت ضربان ضرب مجرد عن تنفس بشيء كالصوت الممتد ومتنفس بصورة ما وهو ضربان : ضروري كما يكون من الجمادات ومن الحيوانات، واختياري كما يكون من الإنسان، وذلك ضربان ضرب باليد كصوت العود ونحوه، وضرب بالفم، وهو أيضاً ضربان نطق وغير نطق، كصوت الناي، والنطق إما مفرد من الكلام وإما مركب كأحد الأنواع من الكلام . قال تعالى : ولا ترفعوا أصواتكم فوق صوت النبي وتخصيص الصوت بالنهي لكونه أعم من النطق والكلام، ويجوز أنه خصه لأن المكروه رفع الصوت فوق صوته لا رفع الكلام. راجع بصائر ذوي التمييزج ٣ ص ٤٤٠).
The first topic: Sound, according to us, is by sheer creation of God, the Exalted, like all other events. According to the philosophers: it is a quality arising in the air by vibration caused by impact or splitting, not the vibration or impact or splitting itself as supposed because they are not audible. And evidence for its existence externally and the attachment of sensation to it there is also the perception of its direction and the distinction between its near and far. And that is not due to the arrival of the striking air from that direction, nor to the strength of the near impact and weakness of the far one, otherwise one would not perceive it coming from the side opposite the hearing ear, nor would one distinguish between the strong distant and the weak near. From among the sensible qualities are the audibles, which are sounds and letters. Sound, according to us, occurs by sheer creation of God, the Exalted, without any effect of vibration, impact, or splitting, like all other events. And often the false opinions of the philosophers are mentioned without attempting to explain. (Footnote) Sound: according to some scholars, is the compressed air from the impact of two bodies. Sound is of two types: a type stripped of any breathing [؟] like the prolonged sound, and a type with some form of breathing, and that is of two types: necessary as occurs from inanimate objects and animals, and voluntary as occurs from humans. And that is of two types: type by hand like the sound of the lute and the like, and type by mouth. And that is also two types: speech and non-speech like the sound of the flute. Speech is either single from speech or composite like one of the types of speech. He said, the Exalted: 'And do not raise your voices above the voice of the Prophet.' And the specification of 'voice' in the prohibition is because it is more general than speech and utterance, and it is possible that He specified it because the disliked thing is raising the voice above his voice, not raising speech. Refer to Basa'ir Dhawi al-Tamyiz, vol. 3, p. 440.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a definition and contrast of two views on the nature of sound. The author begins by stating the Muslim stance (عندنا) that sound is purely created by God like all other contingent events. He then presents the philosophers' view that sound is a quality (كيفية) that arises in air due to vibration (تموج) caused by impact (قرع) or splitting (قلع). Importantly, the philosophers are said to hold that sound is not the vibration itself, because those are not audible. The author provides evidence for sound's external existence and its perception: we can perceive its direction and distinguish near from far, which would not be explained merely by the arrival of striking air from that direction or the force of near vs. far impact—otherwise we could not hear sound from the opposite side of our ear or distinguish strong distant from weak near sounds. After this, the author categorizes sound among the sensible qualities (الكيفيات المحسوسة) known as audibles (المسوعات), which include sounds (الأصوات) and letters (الحروف). He reiterates the Muslim view that sound occurs by God's direct creation without any causal effect from vibration, impact, or splitting. He notes that false philosophical opinions are often mentioned without explanation. The footnote (1) provides an alternative definition from some scholars, stating that sound is compressed air from the impact of two bodies. It then presents a classification of sounds into various types: stripped vs. with breathing, necessary vs. voluntary, by hand vs. by mouth, speech vs. non-speech. It cites the Quranic verse (49:2) about not raising voices above the Prophet's voice, noting that 'voice' (صوت) is more general than 'speech' (نطق) and 'utterance' (كلام). The passage thus defines sound, contrasts theological views, and provides a detailed classification.

Key Terms
الصوت
الحوادث
كيفية
تموج
قرع
قلع
مسموعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think sound is identical to the vibration of air, as some scientific accounts suggest.
Correction: The philosophers themselves distinguish sound from vibration, saying vibration is not audible. The author uses this distinction to argue for sound as a quality, not the vibration.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers mentioned in the passage, what is sound?
A) The vibration of air caused by impact or splitting.
B) A quality that arises in the air due to vibration caused by impact or splitting.
C) The impact or splitting itself.
D) A letter or utterance.

Key Takeaway
Sound, in both views, is something that exists externally and is perceived; the dispute is over its causation—direct divine creation vs. natural process.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever consciously attributed a sound you heard to Allah's direct creation in that moment? If not, what one sound today could you pause and acknowledge as a direct gift from Him?
?
The philosophers reduce sound to a natural process. Does your heart feel more gratitude to Allah for a 'natural process' or for a 'direct creation'? Examine your default assumption and resolve to change it.
?
You have just read the Muslim proof that sound exists externally. How does this affect your certainty that your senses are perceiving real things created by Allah? Will you now trust your hearing more as a means of knowing Allah's signs?

Classification of Sounds and the Quranic Usage
Author classifies sounds into types and cites a Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed classification of sounds according to some scholars, including the distinction between speech and non-speech, and how the Quran uses 'voice' in a general sense.

Core Idea
Sounds are classified by source, necessity, and presence of breath, culminating in speech.

This lesson presents a footnote that offers an alternative definition and detailed classification of sounds. The classification tree moves from general to specific, ending with the distinction between speech and non-speech. The author then connects this to a Quranic verse to highlight the difference between 'voice' and 'speech'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the base definition
Start with the definition of sound as compressed air from the impact of two bodies. This is the foundation for the classification.
This definition differs from the philosophers' earlier.
2
Trace the classification tree
Follow the classification: first, sound is divided into two major types: without breath and with breath. The second type further splits into necessary and voluntary, and so on. Draw a diagram.
Pay attention to the Arabic terms: ضروري, اختياري, etc.
3
Apply to the Quranic verse
Read the verse (49:2) and the author's comment. Understand why 'voice' is used instead of 'speech' or 'utterance'.
The verse prohibits raising voices above the Prophet's voice.

The Two Views on Sound: Ash'ari and Philosophers
Defines Ash'ari and philosophical views on sound with a key clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari and philosophical definitions of sound and the important clarification that sound is not identical to wave motion or striking.

Core Idea
Sound is either God's direct creation or a quality caused by wave motion.

In this lesson, we explore two competing explanations of sound: the Ash'ari view that it is a direct creation of God, and the philosophical view that it is a quality arising from wave motion in the air. The author then clarifies a common mistake — that sound is not the wave motion itself, but something else.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage defining the Ash'ari and philosophical views. Note the key phrases.
2
Identify the clarification
Notice that the author explicitly states what sound is not: it is not the wave motion, strike, or tear.
3
Ponder the reason
Why are wave motion, strike, and tear not considered sound? The answer: because they are not audible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول : الصوت عندنا بمحض خلق الله تعالى كسائر الحوادث وعند الفلاسفة : كيفية تحدث في الهواء بالتموج المعلول للقرع ، أو القلع، لا نفس التموج أو القرع أو القلع على ما ظن لأنها ليست مسموعة، ويدل على وجوده في الخارج، وتعلق الإحساس به هناك أيضاً إدراك جهته والتمييز بين قريبه وبعيده، وليس ذلك لمجيء الهواء القارع من تالك الجهة أو لقوة القرع القريب وضعف البعيد وإلا لما أدرك كونه من الجانب المخالف للأذن السامعة، ولما ميز بين القوى البعيد والضعيف القريب.
The First Section: Sound, according to us, is by sheer creation of God Most High like all other events; and according to the philosophers, it is a quality that occurs in the air due to the wave motion caused by striking or tearing, not the wave motion itself or the striking or the tearing, as some have supposed, because they are not audible. And evidence for its existence in the external world and the attachment of sensation to it there is also the perception of its direction and the distinction between its near and far. And that is not due to the air striking coming from that direction, or due to the strength of the near strike and weakness of the far strike; otherwise, one would not perceive it coming from the side opposite to the hearing ear, nor distinguish between the strong far and the weak near.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage begins by defining the two opposing positions on the nature of sound. According to the Ash'ari school, sound is a direct creation of God like all other contingent events; it has no natural cause. According to the philosophers, sound is a quality that arises in the air due to wave motion, which itself is caused by striking (قرع) or tearing (قلع) of bodies. The author then clarifies that sound is not identical to the wave motion, strike, or tear, because these are not audible (they are perceived by other senses). He then presents a proof that sound exists in the external world (outside the mind) rather than being merely a subjective phenomenon: when we hear a sound, we perceive its direction and we can distinguish between near and far sounds. This perception would be impossible if sound did not exist in a specific location externally. He anticipates an objection: perhaps this perception is due to the air wave coming from that direction or due to the strength/weakness of the strike. He refutes this by arguing that if it were due to the air wave, then when the sound comes from the right but the right ear is blocked, the left ear would still perceive it coming from the right (because the air wave would have to travel around), but we still correctly identify the direction. And if it were due to strength/weakness, we would confuse strong far sounds with weak near sounds, but we do not.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sound is the wave motion or the striking itself.
Correction: The author clarifies that wave motion, striking, and tearing are not audible; sound is a quality that occurs due to them but is distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Ash'ari view of sound?
A) Sound is a quality arising from wave motion.
B) Sound is a direct creation of God.
C) Sound is the wave motion itself.
D) Sound is a mental illusion.

Key Takeaway
Sound is not its physical cause; it is either a direct divine creation or a distinct quality caused by wave motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

Proof That Sound Exists Externally
Presents and defends a proof for sound's external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that sound exists in the external world based on perception of direction and distance, and the refutation of alternative explanations.

Core Idea
We perceive sound's direction and distance, proving it is external, not just mental.

In this lesson, we examine the author's argument for the external existence of sound. He provides evidence from our sensory experience: we perceive where a sound comes from and how far away it is. He then refutes two possible objections — that this perception is due to the air wave or to the strength of the strike.

Guided Path
1
State the evidence
Read the passage that presents the evidence: perception of direction and distance.
2
Consider the objections
Identify the two counterarguments: air wave direction and strike strength.
3
See the refutation
Understand why these objections fail: they would lead to false perceptions.

Detailed Refutation of the Philosophers' Subjectivist View of Sound
Explains and refutes the philosophers' theory of sound as a mental phenomenon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' theory of sound as a quality arising from wave motion and the Ash'ari refutation that sound exists externally, proven by our perception of its direction and distance.

Core Idea
Sound is not merely a mental impression—its external existence is known through direction and distance perception.

In this passage, the author presents the false philosophical view that sound is a quality produced by wave motion in the air, then clarifies that sound is not the wave, strike, or tear themselves. He then refutes the subjectivist claim that sound exists only in the senses by arguing that if that were true, we could not perceive direction or distance—yet we clearly do.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Note the author's style: first stating the Ash'ari position, then detailing the philosophical view, then refuting it.
The passage is long but coherent. Try to follow the logical flow.
2
Identify the two main positions
The passage opens with the Ash'ari stance (sound created directly by Allah). Then it explains the philosophers' theory: sound arises from wave motion caused by striking or tearing, requiring resistance. Notice the conditions and the clarification that sound is not the wave itself.
The author mentions that false opinions are often left unrefuted except when needed.
3
Understand the core refutation
The refutation targets the idea that sound exists only in the senses. The proof is that we perceive direction (e.g., left vs. right) and distance (near vs. far), which would be impossible if sound had no external existence. Think about why this is a strong argument.
The proof assumes that perception of location requires an external source.
4
Analyze the objections and answers
The passage presents two possible objections: (1) direction could come from the air wave, not the sound; (2) distance could come from the strength of the strike. The author refutes each with clear counterexamples. Note how he uses the blocked ear scenario and the confusion of strength with distance.
These objections are sophisticated; the refutations show careful reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الكيفيات المحسوسة المسوعات وهي الأصوات والحروف والصوت عندنا يحدث بمحض خلق الله تعالى من غير تأثير لتموج الهواء والقرع والقلع كسائر الحوادث وكثيراً ما تورد الآراء الباطلة للفلاسفة من غير تعرض لبيان البطلان إلا فيما يحتاج إلى زيادة بيان والصوت عندهم كيفية تحدث في الهواء بسبب تموجه المعلول للقرع الذي هو أساس عنيف، والقلع الذي هو تفريق عنيف بشرط مقاومة المقروع للقارع والمقلوع للقالع ، كما في قرع الماء وقلع الكرباس بخلاف القطن لعدم المقاومة، والمراد بالتموج حالة شبيهة بتموج الماء تحدث بصدم بعد صدم مع سكون بعد سكون، وليس الصوت نفس التموج، أو نفس القرع ، والقلع على ما توهمه بعضهم بناء على اشتباه الشيء بسببه القريب أو البعيد لأن التموج والقرع والقلع ليست من المسموعات قطعاً، بل ربما يدرك الأول باللمس والآخران بالبصر، وقد يتوهم وجود للصوت في الخارج وإنما يحدث في الحس عند وصول الهواء المتموج إلى الصماخ واستدل على بطلان ذلك بأنه لو لم يوجد إلا في الحس لما أدرك عند سماعه جهته وحده من القرب ،والبعد لأن التقدير أنه لا وجود له في مكان وجهة خارج الحس واللازم باطل قطعاً، لأنـا إذا سمعنا الصوت أنه لا ، تعرف أنه وصل إلينا من جهة اليمين أو اليسار ومن مكان قريب أو بعيد . لا يقال : يجوز أن يكون إدراك الجهة لأجل أن الهواء المتموج يجيء منها ويميز القريب والبعيد، لأجل أن أثر القارع القريب أقوى من البعيد، وإن لم يكن الصوت موجوداً في الجهة والمسافة، لأنا نقول : لو صح الأول لما أدركت الجهة التي على خلاف الأذن السامعة وليس كذلك لأن السامع قد يسد أذنه اليمنى ويجيء الصوت من يمينه فيسمعه بأذنه اليسرى، ويعرف أنه جاء من يمينه مع القطع بأن الهواء المتموج لا يصل إلى اليسرى إلا بعد الانعطاف عن اليمين ولو صح الثاني لزم أن تشتبه القوة والضعف بالقرب والبعد، فلم يميز بين البعيد القوى والقريب الضعيف، وظن في الصوتين المتساويين في القرب والبعد المختلفين بالقوة والضعف أنهما مختلفان في القرب والبعد وليس كذلك ولهم تردد في مقام آخر وهو أنه إذا وصل الهواء المتموج إلى الصماخ فالمسموع هو الصوت القائم بالهواء الواصل فقط،
From the sensible qualities that are heard, which are sounds and letters, and sound according to us occurs by sheer creation of God Most High without any effect of wave motion of air and striking and tearing, like all other events. And often the false opinions of the philosophers are mentioned without explanation of their falsity except where additional explanation is needed. And sound according to them is a quality that occurs in the air due to its wave motion caused by striking, which is a violent collision, and tearing, which is a violent separation, on condition of resistance of the struck to the striker and the torn to the tearer, as in the striking of water and tearing of cloth, unlike cotton for lack of resistance. And by wave motion is meant a state similar to the undulation of water that occurs by shock after shock with stillness after stillness. And sound is not the wave motion itself, nor the striking or tearing itself, as some have imagined based on confusing a thing with its near or far cause, because wave motion, striking, and tearing are definitely not audible; rather, the first may be perceived by touch and the other two by sight. And it is sometimes imagined that sound exists in the external world, but it only occurs in the sense when the wavy air reaches the eardrum. And it is argued for the invalidity of that by that if it did not exist except in the sense, then when hearing it one would not perceive its direction and its distance, because the assumption is that it has no existence in a place and direction outside the sense, and the consequent is definitely false, because when we hear the sound, we know that it reached us from the right side or the left, and from a near or far place. It cannot be said: it is possible that the perception of direction is because the wavy air comes from it, and distance is distinguished because the effect of the near striker is stronger than the far one, even if the sound does not exist in the direction and distance, because we say: if the first were correct, one would not perceive the direction that is opposite to the hearing ear, but that is not the case, because the hearer may block his right ear and the sound comes from his right, he hears it with his left ear and knows that it came from his right, with the certainty that the wavy air does not reach the left except after bending from the right. And if the second were correct, it would necessarily follow that strength and weakness are confused with nearness and distance, so one would not distinguish between the strong far and the weak near, and would think about two sounds equal in nearness and distance but different in strength and weakness that they are different in nearness and distance, but that is not the case. And they have hesitation in another context, which is that when the wavy air reaches the eardrum, what is heard is only the sound that subsists in the arriving air...
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage begins with a restatement of the Ash'ari position that sound is created directly by God, and then notes that the false opinions of the philosophers are often mentioned without refutation except where needed. The author then provides a detailed exposition of the philosophers' theory: sound is a quality in the air due to wave motion caused by striking or tearing, requiring resistance of the object. He clarifies that wave motion is like water waves, and emphasizes that sound is not the wave, strike, or tear themselves, because those are perceptible by other senses. He then addresses a subjectivist view that sound exists only in the mind at the moment the wavy air reaches the ear. He refutes this by reiterating the proof from perception of direction and distance, and then considers two objections. First objection: perception of direction could be due to the air wave coming from that direction. Answer: if that were the case, then when the sound comes from the right but the right ear is blocked, the left ear would not perceive the direction correctly because the air wave would have to travel around; but we still perceive it. Second objection: perception of distance could be due to the strength of the near strike vs weakness of the far one. Answer: this would confuse strength with distance, so we would not distinguish a strong far sound from a weak near one. He gives an example of two sounds equal in distance but different in strength – we would wrongly think they differ in distance. The passage ends with a mention of another point of hesitation: when the wavy air reaches the eardrum, what is heard is only the sound subsisting in that arriving air, implying a further discussion not completed in this excerpt.

Correction: The author explicitly clarifies that wave motion, striking, and tearing are causes perceived by touch or sight, not sound itself. Sound is a separate quality arising from them.

The Letter as a Distinguishing Quality of Sound
Definition of the letter and three scholarly views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the letter (ḥarf) as a quality that distinguishes sounds of identical pitch, and the three scholarly views on whether it is the quality itself, the sound substrate, or both.

Core Idea
The letter is a quality, not the sound itself.

The passage opens by defining the letter (ḥarf) as a quality that occurs to sound and distinguishes it from another sound of the same pitch. This definition immediately raises a question: what exactly is the letter—the quality, the sound that bears it, or both? The author presents three views, favoring the composite. In this lesson, we will unpack the definition and the three opinions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author first states the definition, then immediately clarifies it by saying it distinguishes a sound from another similar in pitch.
The term 'letter' here is a technical term in phonetics/phonology, not the alphabet.
2
Identify the three views
The passage lists three interpretations: Ibn Sīnā's view that the letter is the accidental quality; a group of verifiers that it is the sound (the substrate); and some that it is the composite of both. Note which one the author considers closer to the truth.
The author says 'وكأنه أشبه بالحق' (and it seems closer to the truth) for the composite view.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this distinction matters. If the letter is just the quality, then the sound itself is not the letter; if it is the substrate, then the quality is secondary. The composite view makes the letter a combination of the sound and its distinguishing quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني: قد تعرض للصوت كيفية بها يمتاز عما يماثله في الحدة والثقل تميزاً في المسموع وهو الحرف (۱). يتميز عن صوت آخر يماثله في الحدة والثقل تميزاً في المسموع والحرف وهي تلك الكيفية العارضة في عبارة ابن سينا (۲)، وذلك الصوت المعروض في عبارة جمع من المحققين (۳)، ومجموع العارض والمعروض في عبارة البعض وكأنه أشبه بالحق وقيد المماثلة بالحدة والثقل أي الزيرية والبمية احترازاً عنهما، فإن كلا منهما يفيد تميز صوت عن صوت آخر تميزاً في المسموع لكن في صوتين يكونان مختلفين بالحدة والثقل ضرورة. وقيد التميز بالمسموع احترازاً عن مثل الطول والقصر والطيب وغيره فإن التميز بها لا يكون تميزاً في المسموع لأنها ليست بمسموعة، إلا أن في كونها من الكيفيات نظراً. فالأولى : أنه احتراز عن مثل الغنة والبحوحة .
(۱) الحرف يقال : حرف كل شيء طرفه وشفيره وحده ، ومنه حرف الجبل وهو أعلاه المحدد. قال الفراء جمع حرف حرف كعنب قال تعالى : ( ومن الناس من يعبد الله على حرف أي على وجه وهو أن يعبده في السراء دون الضراء وقيل : على شك. راجع بصائر ذوي التمييز ج ٢ ص ٤٤٢) .
(۲) عبارة ابن سينا : بأنه كيفيه تعرض للصوت بها يمتاز عن مثله في الجدة والثقل تميزاً في المسموع.
(۳) في (أ) جميع المحققين .
(۴) في (أ) الفنة بالفاء وهو تحريف .
The second investigation: A quality occurs to sound by which it is distinguished from what is similar to it in sharpness and heaviness, a distinction in what is heard—and it is the letter (al-ḥarf) (1). It is distinguished from another sound similar to it in sharpness and heaviness, a distinction in what is heard, and the letter is that accidental quality in Ibn Sīnā's phrase (2); and that sound which is the substrate in the phrase of a group of verifiers (3); and the composite of the accident and the substrate in the phrase of some, and it seems closer to the truth. The condition of similarity in sharpness and heaviness, i.e., ziriyya (high pitch) and bamiyya (low pitch), is a precaution against them, for each of them yields a distinction of one sound from another in what is heard, but necessarily in two sounds that differ in sharpness and heaviness. The condition of distinction in what is heard is a precaution against such things as length, shortness, pleasantness, and others, for distinction by them is not a distinction in what is heard because they are not heard—except that there is consideration as to whether they are among the qualities. So the better view is that it is a precaution against such things as ghunna (nasality) and buḥḥa (hoarseness).
(1) The letter (al-ḥarf) is said: the edge, lip, and boundary of everything; from it is 'ḥarf al-jabal' (the mountain's edge) which is its highest pointed part. Al-Farrā' said the plural of ḥarf is ḥurūf like 'unab. He said, Most High: 'And among mankind is he who worships Allah on a ḥarf' (Quran 22:11) i.e., on a condition, meaning he worships Him in prosperity but not in adversity, and it is said: on doubt. See Baṣā'ir Dhawī al-Tamyīz vol. 2 p. 442.
(2) Ibn Sīnā's phrase: that it is a quality that occurs to sound by which it is distinguished from its like in sharpness and heaviness, a distinction in what is heard.
(3) In (A): 'all the verifiers'.
(4) In (A): 'al-fanna' with a fā' and it is a corruption.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage defines the 'letter' (ḥarf) as a specific quality that distinguishes sounds that are otherwise similar in pitch (sharpness and heaviness). The author first states that a quality occurs to sound, by which it is distinguished from another sound matched in pitch—this quality is the letter. He then notes three views: (a) Ibn Sīnā considers the letter to be the accidental quality itself; (b) a group of verifiers consider it to be the sound (the substrate) that bears that quality; (c) some combine both, which the author deems closer to the truth. The author explains two conditions in the definition to avoid ambiguity. First, the condition of similarity in pitch (ḥidda and thiqal, i.e., high and low pitch) excludes cases where two sounds already differ in pitch, for their auditory distinction is due to pitch, not the letter. Second, the condition that the distinction be 'in what is heard' excludes non-auditory characteristics like length, shortness, or pleasantness—these are not heard per se (or their status as qualities is debated). The better view is that it excludes nasality (ghunna) and hoarseness (buḥḥa), which are heard but are not the distinguishing quality that defines the letter. The footnotes provide lexical background for ḥarf, Ibn Sīnā's full definition, and textual variants.

Key Terms
حرف
كيفية
عارض
معروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the letter is the sound itself or the pitch itself.
Correction: The letter is a quality that distinguishes sounds of the same pitch, not the pitch itself nor the bare sound.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which view does the author seem to favor regarding what the letter is?
A) The letter is the accidental quality (Ibn Sīnā's view).
B) The letter is the sound substrate (group of verifiers).
C) The letter is the composite of accident and substrate.
D) The letter is the pitch (sharpness/heaviness).

Key Takeaway
The letter (ḥarf) is a quality that distinguishes sounds of equal pitch, and it is best understood as the composite of the quality and the sound.

Conditions of the Definition: Excluding Pitch and Non-Auditory Features
Conditions: similarity in pitch and auditory distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of the letter requires similarity in pitch and the condition that the distinction must be in what is heard, and what characteristics are excluded (such as nasality and hoarseness).

Core Idea
The letter only distinguishes sounds of equal pitch, and only auditory distinctions count.

After defining the letter and presenting the three views, the author adds two conditions to the definition to avoid confusion. The first condition ensures that pitch differences (sharpness and heaviness) are not mistaken for letters; the second ensures that only auditory qualities are considered. The passage also debates whether nasality and hoarseness are excluded as non-auditory or are indeed auditory but not the letter. This lesson clarifies these conditions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition of similarity in pitch
The author says the condition 'similarity in sharpness and heaviness' is to exclude pitch differences. If two sounds differ in pitch, their distinction is due to pitch, not the letter. So the letter only distinguishes sounds of the same pitch.
Sharpness (ḥidda) and heaviness (thiqal) correspond to high and low pitch.
2
Understand the condition of distinction in what is heard
The condition 'distinction in what is heard' excludes characteristics like length, shortness, or pleasantness—these are not heard per se, so they cannot be the distinguishing feature of a letter. The author notes a debate: some consider these as qualities, but the better view is to exclude nasality and hoarseness.
The author says 'فالأولى: أنه احتراز عن مثل الغنة والبحوحة' (the better view is that it excludes nasality and hoarseness).
3
Reflect on the overall definition
The full definition of the letter is: a quality that distinguishes a sound from another sound similar in pitch, and the distinction is in what is heard (i.e., auditory). This separates letters from pitch variations and from non-auditory features.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني: قد تعرض للصوت كيفية بها يمتاز عما يماثله في الحدة والثقل تميزاً في المسموع وهو الحرف (۱). يتميز عن صوت آخر يماثله في الحدة والثقل تميزاً في المسموع والحرف وهي تلك الكيفية العارضة في عبارة ابن سينا (۲)، وذلك الصوت المعروض في عبارة جمع من المحققين (۳)، ومجموع العارض والمعروض في عبارة البعض وكأنه أشبه بالحق وقيد المماثلة بالحدة والثقل أي الزيرية والبمية احترازاً عنهما، فإن كلا منهما يفيد تميز صوت عن صوت آخر تميزاً في المسموع لكن في صوتين يكونان مختلفين بالحدة والثقل ضرورة. وقيد التميز بالمسموع احترازاً عن مثل الطول والقصر والطيب وغيره فإن التميز بها لا يكون تميزاً في المسموع لأنها ليست بمسموعة، إلا أن في كونها من الكيفيات نظراً. فالأولى : أنه احتراز عن مثل الغنة والبحوحة .
(۱) الحرف يقال : حرف كل شيء طرفه وشفيره وحده ، ومنه حرف الجبل وهو أعلاه المحدد. قال الفراء جمع حرف حرف كعنب قال تعالى : ( ومن الناس من يعبد الله على حرف أي على وجه وهو أن يعبده في السراء دون الضراء وقيل : على شك. راجع بصائر ذوي التمييز ج ٢ ص ٤٤٢) .
(۲) عبارة ابن سينا : بأنه كيفيه تعرض للصوت بها يمتاز عن مثله في الجدة والثقل تميزاً في المسموع.
(۳) في (أ) جميع المحققين .
(۴) في (أ) الفنة بالفاء وهو تحريف .
The second investigation: A quality occurs to sound by which it is distinguished from what is similar to it in sharpness and heaviness, a distinction in what is heard—and it is the letter (al-ḥarf) (1). It is distinguished from another sound similar to it in sharpness and heaviness, a distinction in what is heard, and the letter is that accidental quality in Ibn Sīnā's phrase (2); and that sound which is the substrate in the phrase of a group of verifiers (3); and the composite of the accident and the substrate in the phrase of some, and it seems closer to the truth. The condition of similarity in sharpness and heaviness, i.e., ziriyya (high pitch) and bamiyya (low pitch), is a precaution against them, for each of them yields a distinction of one sound from another in what is heard, but necessarily in two sounds that differ in sharpness and heaviness. The condition of distinction in what is heard is a precaution against such things as length, shortness, pleasantness, and others, for distinction by them is not a distinction in what is heard because they are not heard—except that there is consideration as to whether they are among the qualities. So the better view is that it is a precaution against such things as ghunna (nasality) and buḥḥa (hoarseness).
(1) The letter (al-ḥarf) is said: the edge, lip, and boundary of everything; from it is 'ḥarf al-jabal' (the mountain's edge) which is its highest pointed part. Al-Farrā' said the plural of ḥarf is ḥurūf like 'unab. He said, Most High: 'And among mankind is he who worships Allah on a ḥarf' (Quran 22:11) i.e., on a condition, meaning he worships Him in prosperity but not in adversity, and it is said: on doubt. See Baṣā'ir Dhawī al-Tamyīz vol. 2 p. 442.
(2) Ibn Sīnā's phrase: that it is a quality that occurs to sound by which it is distinguished from its like in sharpness and heaviness, a distinction in what is heard.
(3) In (A): 'all the verifiers'.
(4) In (A): 'al-fanna' with a fā' and it is a corruption.

Commentary
This passage defines the 'letter' (ḥarf) as a specific quality that distinguishes sounds that are otherwise similar in pitch (sharpness and heaviness). The author first states that a quality occurs to sound, by which it is distinguished from another sound matched in pitch—this quality is the letter. He then notes three views: (a) Ibn Sīnā considers the letter to be the accidental quality itself; (b) a group of verifiers consider it to be the sound (the substrate) that bears that quality; (c) some combine both, which the author deems closer to the truth. The author explains two conditions in the definition to avoid ambiguity. First, the condition of similarity in pitch (ḥidda and thiqal, i.e., high and low pitch) excludes cases where two sounds already differ in pitch, for their auditory distinction is due to pitch, not the letter. Second, the condition that the distinction be 'in what is heard' excludes non-auditory characteristics like length, shortness, or pleasantness—these are not heard per se (or their status as qualities is debated). The better view is that it excludes nasality (ghunna) and hoarseness (buḥḥa), which are heard but are not the distinguishing quality that defines the letter. The footnotes provide lexical background for ḥarf, Ibn Sīnā's full definition, and textual variants.

The Criterion for Distinguishing Letters
Sanusi critiques the view that distinction in the audible requires the distinguisher to be audible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's critique of the view that distinguishing qualities in sounds must themselves be audible, and his correct definition of distinction in the audible.

Core Idea
Distinction in sound does not require the distinguisher to be audible.

Sanusi examines a key question in the phonetics of speech: does a quality that distinguishes letters have to be audible itself? He argues against this, showing that what matters is that the quality causes variation in the sound. In this passage, he critiques a rival view and clarifies the true meaning of 'distinction in the audible'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the gist of Sanusi's argument.
2
Identify Sanusi's main claim
Find the sentence beginning with 'The truth is that the meaning of distinction...' This is his central thesis.
3
Understand his critique of al-Tawali'
See why he finds the statement in al-Tawali' meaningless, noting the missing qualification he points out.
This shows careful reading of sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي النظر في دلالة قولنا تميزاً في المسموع على أن يكون ما به التميز مسموعاً، وفي أن الحدة والثقل من المسموعات بخلاف الغنة والبحوحة، والحق أن معنى التميز في المسموع ليس أن يكون ما به التميز مسموعاً، بل أن يحصل به التميز في نفس المسموع بأن يختلف باختلافه، ويتحد باتحاده كالحرف بخلاف مثل الغنة والبحوحة وغيرها. فإنها قد تختلف اتحاد المسموع وبالعكس، وما وقع في الطوالع من أن الحروف كيفيات تعرض للأصوات فيتميز بعضها عن البعض في الثقل والخفة كلام لا يعقل له معنى، وكأنه جعل قوله في الثقل متعلقاً بمحذوف أي عن البعض المماثل له في الثقل، وأراد بالخفة الحدة وترك قيد التميز في المسموع لشهرته، وكفى بهذا اختلالاً. والحق أن تعريف الحرف بما ذكر تعريف بالأخفى، وكان المقصود مزيد تفصيل للماهية الواضحة عند العقل، وتنبيه على خصوصياتها.
There remains consideration of the implication of our saying 'distinction in the audible' that what has the distinction is audible, and that shrillness and heaviness are among audible things unlike nasality and hoarseness. The truth is that the meaning of distinction in the audible is not that that by which distinction is made is audible, but rather that distinction occurs by it in the very audible, such that it varies with its variation and is uniform with its uniformity, like the letter, unlike nasality, hoarseness, and others. For they may vary with the uniformity of the audible and vice versa. What is mentioned in al-Tawali' that letters are qualities that occur to sounds so that some are distinguished from others in heaviness and lightness is a statement that has no intelligible meaning. It is as if he made his phrase 'in heaviness' attached to an omitted element, i.e., 'from the like of it in heaviness', and he meant by lightness shrillness, and he omitted the qualification of distinction in the audible due to its fame. Sufficient is this as a corruption. The truth is that defining the letter by what was mentioned is a definition by what is more obscure, while the purpose was a further specification of the essence that is clear to the intellect, and a reminder of its particularities.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the criterion for distinguishing letters in the audible domain. The author (Sanusi) begins by examining the implication of the phrase 'distinction in the audible'—whether it requires that the distinguishing quality itself be audible. He notes that some qualities like shrillness and heaviness are indeed audible, unlike nasality and hoarseness. Then he states the correct view: the distinguishing quality does not have to be audible; rather, it causes distinction within the audible sound itself, such that the sound varies when this quality varies and remains uniform when it is uniform—like the letter itself. He contrasts this with qualities like nasality, which can vary while the sound remains the same. He then critiques the view in al-Tawali' that letters are qualities that occur to sounds, distinguishing them by heaviness and lightness. He finds this meaningless and suggests the author of al-Tawali' misinterpreted the phrasing. He concludes that defining the letter by what was said is defining it by something more obscure, whereas the intention was merely to elaborate on an essence already clear to the mind.

Key Terms

الحرف
الغنة
البحوحة
الطوالع

Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the distinguishing quality does not need to be audible; it only needs to produce distinction in the sound.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
