The Multiple Senses of 'Knowledge' in Kalam
Al-Sanusi enumerates technical usages of 'ilm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic term 'ilm (knowledge) is used by scholars in multiple technical senses—from absolute assent to certain, true, and stable belief—and how each sense connects to specific conditions and missing attributes.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not a fixed term—its meaning shifts with context and conditions.

In this passage, al-Sanusi introduces a key nuance in Islamic theology: the word 'ilm can be used in different ways depending on the scholar's technical framework. It may be restricted to certain, true, and stable belief, or it may be applied broadly to any assent. Understanding these distinctions is essential for following later discussions on faith, certainty, and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without worrying about details. Then read the translation to get a general sense of the different usages listed.
2
Identify the three conditions for certainty
The passage lists three conditions for an assent to be called certainty: decisiveness (jazzm), conformity (muṭābaqa), and stability (thubāt). Note each one.
These conditions are the core of the restricted sense of 'ilm.
3
Trace what happens when a condition is missing
For each missing condition, a specific term is used: non-decisive → opinion (ẓann), non-conforming → compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), non-stable → belief of an imitator (iʿtiqād al-muqallid).
This shows how technical terms are derived from missing conditions.
4
Notice the two additional usages
The passage then mentions two broader uses: belief without considering conformity (dividing into sound/unsound) and applying 'ilm to any assent (including opinion).
5
Reflect on the flexibility of the term
Ask yourself: how does this flexibility affect how scholars argue about knowledge and faith? The commentary will clarify.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يخص العلم بأحد أقسام التصديق - أعني اليقين منه، وهو ما يقارن الجزم والمطابقة والثبات فيسمى غير الجازم ظناً، وغير المطابق جهلاً مركباً، وغير الثابت اعتقاد المقلد وقد لا يعتبر في الاعتقاد المطابقة، فينقسم إلى الصحيح والفاسد وقد يطلق على مطلق التصديق فيعم العلم وغيره.
And it [the term 'knowledge'] may be restricted to one of the divisions of assent (tasdiq) — namely, certainty (yaqin) from it, which is what accompanies decisiveness (jazzm), conformity (mutabaqa), and stability (thubat). So what is not decisive is called opinion (zann), what is not conforming is called compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), and what is not stable is called belief of the imitator (i'tiqad al-muqallid). And sometimes conformity is not considered in belief, in which case it divides into sound and unsound. And it [the term 'knowledge'] may be applied to absolute assent (mutlaq al-tasdiq), so it includes knowledge and other things.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage explains the different technical usages of the word 'ilm' (knowledge) among scholars. The author states that 'ilm' may be restricted to one specific division of assent (tasdiq), namely certainty (yaqin). Certainty is defined as an assent that combines three properties: decisiveness (jazzm), conformity with reality (mutabaqa), and stability (thubat) — meaning it is firm and not easily shaken. If any of these is missing, the assent is given a different name: if not decisive, it is called opinion (zann); if not conforming (i.e., false), it is called compound ignorance (jahl murakkab); if not stable, it is called mere belief of an imitator (i'tiqad al-muqallid). The author then notes that some scholars do not consider conformity as a requirement in belief, so belief is divided into sound and unsound (irrespective of truth). Finally, 'ilm' may also be applied broadly to any assent, in which case it encompasses both knowledge and other types of assent (like opinion).

Key Terms
علم
تصديق
يقين
جزم
مطابقة
ثبات
ظن
جهل مركب
اعتقاد المقلد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the word 'ilm always means certain, true, and stable belief.
Correction: In reality, 'ilm is used flexibly by scholars, sometimes broadly covering any assent, and only in its most restricted sense does it require certainty, conformity, and stability.

Checkpoint

B) Non-conforming assent
C) Non-stable assent
D) Absolute assent

Key Takeaway
The term 'ilm (knowledge) is used in multiple technical ways in Islamic theology; its most precise meaning restricts it to certain, true, and stable belief, but scholars also employ broader senses that include opinion and mere belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you face a doubt, do you feel your certainty shake? What would it take for you to cultivate unwavering yaqin in your heart, not just in your mind?

Consider an area of your life where you are absolutely certain of something that may not be true—could there be compound ignorance hiding in your convictions? How can you actively test your beliefs against the truth, even the uncomfortable ones?

Al-Sanusi describes the muqallid's belief as lacking stability. In what matters of faith are you relying on the authority of others without personal verification? Is there a single theological belief you hold that you cannot defend with evidence? If so, are you content to remain an imitator, or will you seek certainty?

Definitions of Taqlīd, Yaqīn, and Zann
Defines muqallid, i'tiqād, yaqīn, and zann with their criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the muqallid, yaqīn, and zann, and the criterion for distinguishing certainty from conjecture.

Core Idea
Certainty is not mere stability; it requires a judgment that the opposite is impossible.

This lesson introduces three key epistemological terms: taqlīd (imitation in belief), yaqīn (certainty), and zann (conjecture). The author clarifies that belief may not correspond to reality, and then turns to the precise criteria for certainty and conjecture, emphasizing that both are simple beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation to get an overview of the definitions.
2
Understand the muqallid
Note that the muqallid's belief may be correct or incorrect, and that i'tiqād can mean any assent.
This is a broad definition.
3
Grasp the definition of yaqīn and zann
Learn the two views: the common view that they accompany judgments about the opposite, and the author's verification that they are such that if the opposite were considered, one would judge accordingly.
Focus on the criterion: impossibility vs possibility.
4
Reflect on simplicity
Understand that both yaqīn and zann are simple beliefs, not composed of two judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقلد وقد لا يعتبر في الاعتقاد المطابقة، فينقسم إلى الصحيح والفاسد وقد يطلق على مطلق التصديق فيعم العلم وغيره.
وقد يراد بالظن ما ليس بيقين، فيعم الظن الصرف والجهل المركب واعتقاد المقلد ثم ظاهر عبارة البعض أن اليقين يقارن الحكم بامتناع النقيض، والظن الصرف يقارن الحكم (بإمكان النقيض وإن كان مرجوحاً، لكن التحقيق هو أن المعتبر في اليقين أن يكون بحيث لو أخطر النقيض بالبال لحكم) (۱) بامتناعه، وفي الظن أنه لو أخطر الحكم بإمكانه حتى إن كلاً منهما اعتقاد بسيط لا يتركب عن حكمين، واعترض على اعتبار الثبات في اليقين بأنه إن أريد به عسر الزوال فربما يكون اعتقاد المقلد كذلك، وإن أريد به (۲) امتناع الزوال فاليقين من النظريات قد يذهل الذهن عن بعض مباديه فيشك فيه، بل ربما يحكم بخلافه .
والجواب أنه : إن أريد بالذهول مجرد عدم الحضور بالفعل عند العقل فإمكان طريان الشك حينئذ ممنوع ، وإن أريد الزوال بحيث يفتقر إلى تحصيل واكتساب فلا يقين حينئذ بالحكم النظري (۳) ، ونحن إنما نحكم بامتناع الشك في اليقين مادام يقينا فإن التصديق () على ما ذكرنا ينحصر في العلم والجهل المركب والاعتقاد الصحيح والظن لأن غير الجازم لا بد أن يكون راجحاً لأنه أقل مراتب الحكم أعنى قبول النفس وإذعانها لوقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها، وما ذكــر الإمام وجمع من المتأخرين أن غير الجازم إما أن يكون راجحاً فظن، أو مساوياً فشك، أو مرجوجاً فوهم محل نظر لأن الشك تردد في الوقوع واللاوقوع، والوهم ملاحظة للطرف المرجوح وكلاهما تصور لا حكم معه أصلاً . كذا .
The muqallid (one who follows authority without proof) may not consider correspondence (of belief with reality) in belief, so it divides into correct and incorrect. And it (belief) may be applied to absolute assent, thus including knowledge and other than it. And sometimes conjecture (zann) is intended as what is not certainty, so it includes pure conjecture and compound ignorance and the belief of the muqallid. Then the apparent wording of some is that certainty (yaqīn) accompanies the judgment of impossibility of the opposite, and pure conjecture accompanies the judgment of possibility of the opposite even if it is unlikely, but the verification is that the criterion in certainty is that it be such that if the opposite were brought to mind, it would judge its impossibility, and in conjecture that if the opposite were brought to mind, it would judge its possibility, so that each of them is a simple belief not composed of two judgments. And an objection was raised against considering persistence (thabāt) in certainty: if what is intended by it is difficulty of removal, then perhaps the belief of the muqallid is likewise; and if what is intended is impossibility of removal, then certainty from theoretical matters may have the mind become oblivious of some of its premises so it doubts it, rather it may judge its opposite. The answer is: if by oblivion is meant mere non-presence in actuality in the mind, then the possibility of the occurrence of doubt then is denied; and if is meant removal such that it requires acquisition and earning, then there is no certainty in the theoretical judgment at that moment. And we only judge the impossibility of doubt in certainty as long as it remains certainty, because assent, according to what we mentioned, is confined to knowledge, compound ignorance, correct belief, and conjecture, because the non-decisive must be preponderant, since it is the lowest rank of judgment, namely the soul's acceptance and submission to the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation. And what the Imam and a group of later scholars mentioned, that the non-decisive is either preponderant so conjecture, or equal so doubt, or outweighed so illusion (wahm), is subject to scrutiny, because doubt is hesitation in occurrence and non-occurrence, and wahm is consideration of the weaker side, and both are conceptualization (taṣawwur) without any judgment. Thus ends.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part epistemological definition and argument. It begins by defining taqlīd (imitation in belief) and noting that belief may not correspond to reality, thus it can be correct or incorrect. It then defines zann (conjecture) as what is not certainty, covering pure conjecture, compound ignorance, and muqallid belief. Next, it presents a common view that yaqīn (certainty) involves judging the opposite's impossibility, while zann involves possibility. The author's verification adds that the criterion for yaqīn is that if the opposite were considered, one would judge it impossible; for zann, one would judge it possible. Both are simple beliefs. Then an objection questions thabāt (stability) in yaqīn: if stability means difficulty of removal, it applies to muqallid belief too; if it means impossibility of removal, theoretical certainties can be forgotten. The answer distinguishes dhuhūl (oblivion): mere non-presence does not introduce doubt, but removal requiring reacquisition voids certainty. The author then limits assent (taṣdīq) to four categories: knowledge, compound ignorance, correct belief, and zann. He critiques the Imam Ghazālī's threefold division of non-jāzim into zann, shakk, and wahm, arguing that shakk (doubt) is hesitation (taṣawwur) not judgment, and wahm (illusion) is likewise conceptualization. Thus, only zann qualifies as a judgment among non-decisive states.

Key Terms
muqallid
i'tiqād
taṣdīq
yaqīn
zann

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That certainty is defined simply by stability of belief without consideration of the opposite.
Correction: The author argues that certainty requires that one would judge the opposite impossible if it were considered; mere persistence is not enough.

Checkpoint
According to the author's verification, what distinguishes yaqīn from zann?
A) Yaqīn is stable while zann is not
B) In yaqīn, if the opposite is considered, one judges its impossibility; in zann, one judges its possibility
C) Yaqīn is based on proof while zann is not
D) Yaqīn is certain while zann is uncertain

Key Takeaway
Certainty is defined not by mere persistence but by the disposition to judge the opposite impossible upon consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you consider your belief in Allah as yaqīn? If you seriously brought the opposite (e.g., that there is no God) to your mind, would you genuinely judge it impossible, or would you feel a slight doubt? What does your honest answer reveal about the strength of your certainty? What one step can you take this week to move closer to that disposition?
?
The muqallid believes without proof. In what areas of your religion—such as prayer rulings, attributes of Allah, or eschatology—do you still rely on blind acceptance without understanding the evidence? Are you willing to examine those evidences even if it challenges your comfort? Commit to studying one such topic with a scholar this month.
?
The passage teaches that conjecture includes 'compound ignorance'—thinking you know while being wrong. Is there a belief you hold about Islam that you assume is certain but have never tested against the Quran and Sunnah? How would you feel if that belief turned out to be compound ignorance? Will you actively seek clarity before judgment?

The Objection on Stability (Thabāt) and the Classification of Non-Jāzim Beliefs
Presents objection on thabāt, answer, and classification of non-jāzim beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to considering stability (thabāt) as a criterion for certainty, the author's answer, and the correct classification of non-decisive beliefs (zann, shakk, wahm).

Core Idea
Stability alone is insufficient for certainty; moreover, only preponderant belief (zann) qualifies as judgment among non-decisive states.

This lesson addresses a critical objection against the definition of certainty as requiring stability (thabāt). The author responds by clarifying two types of obliviousness. He then concludes by limiting judgment (taṣdīq) to four categories and critiquing the common threefold division of non-jāzim into conjecture, doubt, and illusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the objection: if thabāt means difficulty of removal, the muqallid also has that; if impossibility of removal, theoretical certainties can be forgotten.
2
Examine the answer
The author distinguishes between mere non-presence and removal requiring reacquisition. Only in the latter does doubt occur, voiding certainty.
Focus on the distinction of dhuhūl.
3
Learn the classification of assent
The author limits taṣdīq to four: knowledge, compound ignorance, correct belief, and conjecture. Non-decisive must be preponderant.
Note the critique of the threefold division.
4
Understand the critique of shakk and wahm
Doubt and illusion are not judgments but conceptualizations (taṣawwur). Only zann is a genuine non-decisive judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقلد وقد لا يعتبر في الاعتقاد المطابقة، فينقسم إلى الصحيح والفاسد وقد يطلق على مطلق التصديق فيعم العلم وغيره.
وقد يراد بالظن ما ليس بيقين، فيعم الظن الصرف والجهل المركب واعتقاد المقلد ثم ظاهر عبارة البعض أن اليقين يقارن الحكم بامتناع النقيض، والظن الصرف يقارن الحكم (بإمكان النقيض وإن كان مرجوحاً، لكن التحقيق هو أن المعتبر في اليقين أن يكون بحيث لو أخطر النقيض بالبال لحكم) (۱) بامتناعه، وفي الظن أنه لو أخطر الحكم بإمكانه حتى إن كلاً منهما اعتقاد بسيط لا يتركب عن حكمين، واعترض على اعتبار الثبات في اليقين بأنه إن أريد به عسر الزوال فربما يكون اعتقاد المقلد كذلك، وإن أريد به (۲) امتناع الزوال فاليقين من النظريات قد يذهل الذهن عن بعض مباديه فيشك فيه، بل ربما يحكم بخلافه .
والجواب أنه : إن أريد بالذهول مجرد عدم الحضور بالفعل عند العقل فإمكان طريان الشك حينئذ ممنوع ، وإن أريد الزوال بحيث يفتقر إلى تحصيل واكتساب فلا يقين حينئذ بالحكم النظري (۳) ، ونحن إنما نحكم بامتناع الشك في اليقين مادام يقينا فإن التصديق () على ما ذكرنا ينحصر في العلم والجهل المركب والاعتقاد الصحيح والظن لأن غير الجازم لا بد أن يكون راجحاً لأنه أقل مراتب الحكم أعنى قبول النفس وإذعانها لوقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها، وما ذكــر الإمام وجمع من المتأخرين أن غير الجازم إما أن يكون راجحاً فظن، أو مساوياً فشك، أو مرجوجاً فوهم محل نظر لأن الشك تردد في الوقوع واللاوقوع، والوهم ملاحظة للطرف المرجوح وكلاهما تصور لا حكم معه أصلاً . كذا .
The muqallid (one who follows authority without proof) may not consider correspondence (of belief with reality) in belief, so it divides into correct and incorrect. And it (belief) may be applied to absolute assent, thus including knowledge and other than it. And sometimes conjecture (zann) is intended as what is not certainty, so it includes pure conjecture and compound ignorance and the belief of the muqallid. Then the apparent wording of some is that certainty (yaqīn) accompanies the judgment of impossibility of the opposite, and pure conjecture accompanies the judgment of possibility of the opposite even if it is unlikely, but the verification is that the criterion in certainty is that it be such that if the opposite were brought to mind, it would judge its impossibility, and in conjecture that if the opposite were brought to mind, it would judge its possibility, so that each of them is a simple belief not composed of two judgments. And an objection was raised against considering persistence (thabāt) in certainty: if what is intended by it is difficulty of removal, then perhaps the belief of the muqallid is likewise; and if what is intended is impossibility of removal, then certainty from theoretical matters may have the mind become oblivious of some of its premises so it doubts it, rather it may judge its opposite. The answer is: if by oblivion is meant mere non-presence in actuality in the mind, then the possibility of the occurrence of doubt then is denied; and if is meant removal such that it requires acquisition and earning, then there is no certainty in the theoretical judgment at that moment. And we only judge the impossibility of doubt in certainty as long as it remains certainty, because assent, according to what we mentioned, is confined to knowledge, compound ignorance, correct belief, and conjecture, because the non-decisive must be preponderant, since it is the lowest rank of judgment, namely the soul's acceptance and submission to the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation. And what the Imam and a group of later scholars mentioned, that the non-decisive is either preponderant so conjecture, or equal so doubt, or outweighed so illusion (wahm), is subject to scrutiny, because doubt is hesitation in occurrence and non-occurrence, and wahm is consideration of the weaker side, and both are conceptualization (taṣawwur) without any judgment. Thus ends.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part epistemological definition and argument. It begins by defining taqlīd (imitation in belief) and noting that belief may not correspond to reality, thus it can be correct or incorrect. It then defines zann (conjecture) as what is not certainty, covering pure conjecture, compound ignorance, and muqallid belief. Next, it presents a common view that yaqīn (certainty) involves judging the opposite's impossibility, while zann involves possibility. The author's verification adds that the criterion for yaqīn is that if the opposite were considered, one would judge it impossible; for zann, one would judge it possible. Both are simple beliefs. Then an objection questions thabāt (stability) in yaqīn: if stability means difficulty of removal, it applies to muqallid belief too; if it means impossibility of removal, theoretical certainties can be forgotten. The answer distinguishes dhuhūl (oblivion): mere non-presence does not introduce doubt, but removal requiring reacquisition voids certainty. The author then limits assent (taṣdīq) to four categories: knowledge, compound ignorance, correct belief, and zann. He critiques the Imam Ghazālī's threefold division of non-jāzim into zann, shakk, and wahm, arguing that shakk (doubt) is hesitation (taṣawwur) not judgment, and wahm (illusion) is likewise conceptualization. Thus, only zann qualifies as a judgment among non-decisive states.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That doubt and illusion are types of judgment (ḥukm) like conjecture.
Correction: The author argues that doubt and illusion are conceptualizations (taṣawwur), not judgments, because they lack the soul's acceptance.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct classification of non-decisive (ghayr jāzim) states that count as judgment?
A) Zann only
B) Zann and shakk
C) Zann, shakk, and wahm
D) Zann and wahm

Key Takeaway
Only preponderant conjecture (zann) qualifies as a non-decisive judgment; doubt and illusion are conceptualizations, not judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may feel certain about many beliefs, but are they truly rooted in evidence or borrowed from others without verification? If your yaqīn is based on taqlīd, what would happen if you forgot one premise—would your certainty collapse? Today, resolve to trace the foundation of your most important beliefs back to their proofs.
?
When you experience doubt (shakk) about a matter of faith, do you treat it as a valid judgment or as a mere conceptual hesitation? How would your spiritual life change if you considered such doubts not as judgments but as passing ideas that lack preponderance? Will you commit to not giving equal weight to doubts that do not outweigh confidence?
?
The passage distinguishes between forgetting a premise and actually losing it. Have you ever assumed that a lapse in memory invalidated your certainty, only to realize the proof was still accessible? How can you intentionally practice recalling the proofs of your creed before they fade from your active mind, so that your certainty remains firm?

Simple and Compound Ignorance: Definitions and Opposition to Knowledge
Sanusi defines simple and compound ignorance and their relation to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of simple and compound ignorance, and why compound ignorance is considered opposite to knowledge in essence.

Core Idea
Ignorance is not merely lack of knowledge; compound ignorance is a positive belief that opposes knowledge.

This passage from Sanusi establishes the foundational definitions of ignorance in Islamic theology. He distinguishes between simple ignorance—the absence of knowledge—and compound ignorance—a firm but false belief. Understanding this distinction is crucial for further discussions on knowledge and certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of simple and compound ignorance
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi defines each type and the terms he uses.
Simple ignorance is defined in relation to assertoric knowledge, while compound ignorance is defined by firm belief and lack of correspondence.
2
Examine how simple ignorance relates to conception
Sanusi notes that opinion and doubt, though lacking certainty, involve conceptual knowledge. This nuance is important for understanding the full picture.
3
Understand why compound ignorance is opposite to knowledge
Sanusi gives two reasons: impossibility of coexistence and existential opposition. Reflect on why these make them true opposites.
This sets the stage for the Mu'tazila debate in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريان، والشك عدم ملكة للعلم التصديقي، فيكون جهلاً بسيطاً بالنظر إليه، وإن كان علماً من حيث التصور. وأما الجهل المركب أعني الاعتقاد الجازم الغير المطابق ويسمى جهلاً مركباً لأنه جهل بما في الواقع مع الجهل بأنه جاهل به فمضاد للعلم لصدق حد الضدين عليهما لكونهما معنيين يستحيل اجتماعهما لذاتهما، ولكونهما متقابلين وجوديين ليس تعقل أحدهما بالقياس إلى تعقل الآخر. وقالت المعتزلة: هما متماثلان لأن الحقيقة واحدة والاختلاف إنما هو بالعارض، أما أو لا فلأنهما لا يختلفان إلا بمطابقة الواقع ولا مطابقته، وذلك خارج لأن النسبة لا تدخل في حقيقة المنتسبين والاختلاف بالخارج لا يوجب الاختلاف بالذات. وأما ثانياً: فلأن من اعتقد أن زيداً في الدار طول النهار وقد كان فيها إلى قبل الظهر، ثم خرج كان له اعتقاد واحد مستمر لا اختلاف في ذاته، مع أنه كان علماً ثم صار جهلاً. والجواب أن المطابقة واللامطابقة أخص صفات النفس للعلم والجهل، فالاختلاف فيه يستلزم الاختلاف في الذات وظاهره معارضة، ويمكن تنزيله على المنع أي لا نسلم أن الاختلاف بالعارض لا يوجب الاختلاف بالذات، وإنما يكون كذلك لو لم يكن لازماً، ولا نسلم أن الذات واحدة بل الاعتقادات على التجدد فما دام زيد في الدار فالمتجدد علم، وحين خرج فجهل.
Opinion and doubt are absence of the faculty of assertoric knowledge, so they are simple ignorance with respect to it, even if they are knowledge from the perspective of conception. As for compound ignorance, namely, firm belief that does not correspond (to reality) and is called compound ignorance because it is ignorance of what is in reality combined with ignorance that one is ignorant of it, it is opposite to knowledge because the definition of two opposites applies to them, as they are two meanings whose coexistence is impossible in themselves, and because they are existentially opposite, the conceptualization of one not being by reference to the conceptualization of the other. The Mu'tazila said: They are similar because the essence is one and difference is only accidental. First, because they differ only in corresponding to reality or not, and that is external, because the relation does not enter into the essence of the related things, and difference by what is external does not necessitate difference in essence. Second, because one who believes that Zayd is in the house all day, and he was in it until before noon, then left, has one continuous belief with no difference in its essence, even though it was knowledge and then became ignorance. The answer: Correspondence and non-correspondence are the most specific attributes of the soul for knowledge and ignorance, so difference in them necessitates difference in essence. The apparent meaning is an objection, and it can be reduced to denial: we do not accept that difference by accident does not necessitate difference in essence; it would be so only if it were not necessary. And we do not accept that the essence is one; rather, beliefs are renewed, so as long as Zayd is in the house, what is renewed is knowledge, and when he left, it is ignorance.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage defines two types of ignorance and then presents and refutes a Mu'tazila objection. It begins by noting that opinion and doubt are absence of the faculty of certainty, so they are considered simple ignorance relative to certainty, though they are knowledge in terms of conception. Then it defines compound ignorance: a firm belief that does not correspond to reality, combined with ignorance of one's own ignorance. This is described as opposite to knowledge because the two are opposites that cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time; they are existential opposites, each defined in relation to the other. The Mu'tazila object that knowledge and ignorance are similar, with only an accidental difference: first, because they differ only in external correspondence, which does not affect essence; second, because a continuous belief that changes from knowledge to ignorance (e.g., believing Zayd is in the house, then discovering he left) shows the same essence. The author refutes: correspondence is an essential attribute, so difference in it implies difference in essence. Moreover, the belief is not continuous; it is renewed, so the later belief is different.

Key Terms
الجهل البسيط
الجهل المركب
الضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the definition of compound ignorance according to Sanusi?
A) Absence of all knowledge
B) Firm belief that does not correspond to reality combined with unawareness
C) Opinion and doubt
D) Lack of conception

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance is a positive belief that contradicts knowledge in essence, not merely a lack.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your religious understanding do you hold a firm belief, yet you have never seriously questioned whether it might not correspond to reality? How would you even know if you were in compound ignorance about it?
?
Can you recall a time when you were absolutely certain about a matter of faith, only to later discover you were entirely mistaken? How did you react when you realized you were both wrong and unaware of being wrong?
?
If compound ignorance is a positive belief that opposes knowledge, and it can exist in anyone who is confident but mistaken, what steps will you take this week to uncover at least one thing you believe without evidence, so that you are not guilty of this type of ignorance?

Refuting the Mu'tazila: Knowledge and Ignorance Are Distinct in Essence
Sanusi presents and refutes the Mu'tazila claim that knowledge and ignorance are similar in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that knowledge and ignorance are similar in essence, and Sanusi's refutation based on the essential nature of correspondence.

Core Idea
Correspondence to reality is not an accidental attribute; it defines the very essence of knowledge and ignorance.

The Mu'tazila presented a challenge: if knowledge and ignorance both involve firm belief, they must be the same in essence, differing only by accident. Sanusi counters by showing that correspondence to reality is not external but an essential property, and that beliefs are not static but renewed. This lesson examines the debate and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection and their two arguments
The Mu'tazila claim that knowledge and ignorance are similar because they share the same essence, differing only accidentally in correspondence to reality.
They use two arguments: first, external relation; second, continuity of belief.
2
Analyze the first argument: difference by external relation
The Mu'tazila say that correspondence is a relation external to the essence, so difference in relation does not change essence. Consider why this might seem plausible.
3
Analyze the second argument: continuous belief example
They give the example of a continuous belief about Zayd's location that changes from knowledge to ignorance. This suggests the belief itself remains the same in essence.
4
Understand Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi argues that correspondence is a specific attribute that defines knowledge/ignorance, so difference implies essence difference. He also rejects the continuity of belief, arguing that beliefs are renewed each moment.
Pay attention to the concepts of 'lazim' (necessary accident) and renewal of beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Opinion and doubt are absence of the faculty of assertoric knowledge, so they are simple ignorance with respect to it, even if they are knowledge from the perspective of conception. As for compound ignorance, namely, firm belief that does not correspond (to reality) and is called compound ignorance because it is ignorance of what is in reality combined with ignorance that one is ignorant of it, it is opposite to knowledge because the definition of two opposites applies to them, as they are two meanings whose coexistence is impossible in themselves, and because they are existentially opposite, the conceptualization of one not being by reference to the conceptualization of the other. Commentary
This passage defines two types of ignorance and then presents and refutes a Mu'tazila objection. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two firm beliefs that differ only in truth value are essentially the same.
Correction: Correspondence to reality is not an accidental attribute but an essential property that distinguishes knowledge from ignorance.

According to Sanusi, why does difference in correspondence to reality necessitate difference in essence?
A) Because correspondence is an attribute of the soul that is specific to knowledge and ignorance

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and ignorance are distinct in essence because correspondence to reality is a defining attribute, and beliefs are renewed each moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief, do you ever pause to consider whether it corresponds to reality, or do you assume it is true simply because you believe it? How does this lesson expose the gravity of being wrong?
?
Sanusi argues that beliefs are renewed each moment. If true, then each moment you have a choice to align your belief with truth or falsehood. In what area of your life are you most at risk of renewing falsehood rather than seeking truth?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that a continuous belief could change from knowledge to ignorance without changing its essence. Have you ever dismissed a past error by saying 'I was the same person, just misinformed'? How does this lesson call you to take responsibility for each belief as it stands before Allah?

8 min
Division of Knowledge into Eternal and Temporal and the Three Grades of Temporal Knowledge
Al-Taftazani divides knowledge into eternal and temporal and defines the three grades of temporal knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental division of knowledge into eternal and temporal, and the three grades of temporal knowledge: potential, summary, and detailed.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either eternal (Allah's) or temporal; temporal knowledge has three grades.

Al-Taftazani begins his discussion of the nature of knowledge by establishing a foundational classification. He distinguishes between the eternal knowledge of Allah and the temporal knowledge of creatures. Within temporal knowledge, he identifies three distinct grades, each with its own characteristics. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding how human knowledge relates to divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the overall classification structure.
The passage is dense; take your time to absorb the categories.
2
Identify the primary division
Find where al-Taftazani divides knowledge into eternal (qadim) and temporal (hadith). Understand that eternal is Allah's knowledge, temporal is that of creatures.
This is the first major split.
3
Examine the three grades of temporal knowledge
Look for the three grades: (1) potential (al-quwwa), (2) summary (ijmali), (3) detailed (tafsili). Note how each is defined.
Potential is preparedness; summary is a simple state that is the source of details; detailed is awareness of parts individually.
4
Reflect on the page example
Study the example of looking at a page: a single glance gives summary knowledge, while reading each letter gives detailed knowledge. This clarifies the distinction.
The example illustrates that summary and detailed are both valid forms of actual knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلم ينقسم إلى قديم وحادث ومراتب الحادث ثلاث، لأنه إما بالقوة وهو الاستعداد فاللضرورى بالحواس وللنظرى بالضروري، وإما بالفعل إجمالاً بأن يكون عنده أمر بسيط هو مبدأ التفاصيل، أو تفصيلاً بأن يلاحظ الأجزاء مفصلة، وذلك كما إذا نظر إلى الصحيفة جملة ثم حرفاً حرفاً، فالحاصل في الإجمال صورة واحدة تطابق الكل لا كل واحد، وفي التفصيلي ما قال الإمام أن الصورة الواحدة لا تطابق المختلفات، متعددة فيندفع صور والمتعددة تكون تفصيلاً. اللهم إلا أن يراد بالتفصيل حصولها مرتبة، وبالإجمال دفعة). إما قديم لا يسبقه العدم وهو علم الله تعالى وإما حادث سبقه العدم فهو علم المخلوق ومراتب الحادث ثلاث : الأولى : ما يكون بالقوة المحضة، وهو الاستعداد للعلم، وحصوله للضروريات يكون بالحواس الظاهرة والباطنة كما يستفاد من حس اللمس أن هذه النار حارة، فتستعد النفس للعلم بأن كل نار حارة، وعلى هذا القياس وللنظريات يكون بالضروريات بأن يرتب فيكتسب النظرى . والثانية : العلم الإجمالي كمن علم مسألة فغفل عنها ثم سئل فإنه يحضر الجواب في ذهنه دفعة من غير تفصيل، وحقيقته حالة بسيطة إجمالية هي مبدأ تفاصيل المركب. والثالثة : العلم التفصيلي : وهو حضور صورة المركب بحيث تعرف اجزاؤه متميزاً بعضها عن بعض ملاحظاً كل منها على الانفراد، وذلك كما إذا نظرنا إلى الصحيفة دفعة فلاشك أنا نجد حالة إجمالية من الإبصار، ثم إذا حدقنا النظر وأبصرنا كل حرف حرف على الانفراد حصلت لنا حالة أخرى مع أن الإبصار حاصل في الحالين. فالأولى : بمنزلة العلم الإجمالي، والثانية بمنزلة العلم التفصيلي وبهذا يتبين معنى كلامهم أن العلم بالماهية يستلزم العلم بأجزائها، لكن إجمالاً لا تفصيلا.
Knowledge is divided into eternal and temporal. The grades of temporal are three: because it is either potential (which is preparedness) – for necessary (knowledge) through the senses and for theoretical through necessary – or actual either summarily such that he has a simple thing that is the principle of details, or in detail such that he observes the parts in detail, like when one looks at the page as a whole then letter by letter. What is obtained in summary is one form that corresponds to the whole not each individual; in detailed, according to the Imam, one form does not correspond to different things, so multiple forms are obtained, and the multiple forms constitute detail. Unless by detailed is meant its occurrence in order, and by summary its occurrence at once. Either eternal not preceded by non-existence, and that is the knowledge of Allah, or temporal preceded by non-existence, and that is the knowledge of the creature. The grades of temporal are three: First: what is in pure potentiality, which is preparedness for knowledge. Its attainment for necessary things is through the external and internal senses, as from the sense of touch one learns that this fire is hot, so the soul becomes prepared for the knowledge that every fire is hot, and similarly for theoretical matters it is through necessary matters by arranging so that the theoretical is acquired. Second: summary knowledge, like one who knows an issue then forgets it, then is asked, so he brings the answer to his mind all at once without detail. Its reality is a simple summary state that is the principle of the details of the composite. Third: detailed knowledge: it is the presence of the form of the composite such that its parts are known distinguished from each other, each observed individually, like when we look at a page at once, undoubtedly we find a summary state of vision, then if we gaze intently and see each letter individually, another state occurs for us, while vision is present in both states. The first is like summary knowledge, the second is like detailed knowledge. And by this the meaning of their statement becomes clear: knowledge of the essence necessitates knowledge of its parts, but summarily not in detail.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of knowledge. The author, al-Taftazani, begins by stating that knowledge divides into two primary categories: eternal (qadim) and temporal (hadith). Eternal knowledge is that which is not preceded by non-existence, and this is the knowledge of Allah. Temporal knowledge is that which is preceded by non-existence, and this is the knowledge of created beings. He then explains that temporal knowledge has three grades or stages. First, he introduces the division into potential (bi al-quwwa) and actual (bi al-fi'l) knowledge. Potential knowledge is simply preparedness or readiness to know. Actual knowledge is further divided into summary (ijmali) and detailed (tafsili). Summary knowledge is a simple state that is the source of details, like knowing a subject in a general way without specifying details. Detailed knowledge is when the parts are observed individually and distinctly. He illustrates with the example of looking at a page: looking at the whole page at once gives a summary vision, while staring at each letter individually gives detailed vision. The author also addresses an objection by the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) that one form cannot correspond to multiple different things, but explains that the summary form corresponds to the whole, not each part. He concludes that knowledge of the essence of a thing necessitates knowledge of its parts, but in a summary manner, not in detail. This passage systematically defines the key terms and establishes the fundamental division of knowledge in Islamic theology.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
علم بالقوة
علم بالفعل
علم إجمالي
علم تفصيلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that summary knowledge is incomplete or lesser compared to detailed knowledge.
Correction: Summary knowledge is a valid form of actual knowledge that encompasses the essence of a thing without needing to detail its parts. It is not a deficiency but a different mode.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the grade of temporal knowledge that is described as a simple state serving as the principle of details?
A) Knowledge in potential (al-quwwa)
B) Summary knowledge (al-ijmali)
C) Detailed knowledge (al-tafsili)
D) Necessary knowledge

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is either eternal (divine) or temporal (created); temporal knowledge progresses from potential to summary to detailed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own knowledge of Allah, is it eternal (impossible) or temporal? Within temporal knowledge, are you content with a summary 'knowledge' of His attributes, or have you striven for detailed understanding through study of the Qur'an and creation? Which grade truly describes your current state, and what will you do to move from potential to actual, or from summary to detailed?
?
The passage shows that human knowledge is temporal and limited. How does this humbling truth confront your tendency to assume you 'know' enough about your faith? Name one area of Islamic knowledge—such as the conditions of prayer or the names of Allah—where you have only a summary grasp. What specific step will you take this week to move toward detailed knowledge?
?
Allah's knowledge is eternal and not preceded by non-existence; your knowledge is preceded by ignorance. When you become aware of your ignorance, does it drive you to seek knowledge or to ignore it? Reflect on a time you avoided learning because it was uncomfortable. How does the example of looking at a page—first seeing the whole, then each letter—encourage you to embrace gradual, patient learning in your spiritual journey?

Defending Homogeneity: Tasawwur vs. Tasdiq
Author presents and answers the objection that concept and assent differ, undermining homogeneity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the homogeneity of knowledge against the objection that concept and assent are fundamentally different.

Core Idea
Different types of knowledge can be homogeneous if 'ilm is interpreted as assent.

This lesson tackles a key objection to the author's earlier claim that all knowledge is homogeneous. The objector points out that concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) are different, so how can they be homogeneous? In response, the author offers several possible interpretations of the term 'ilm that preserve homogeneity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by the author's answer.
The answer includes clarifications about types of knowledge.
2
Understand the possible interpretations
The author suggests that 'ilm might mean assent only, or that homogeneity applies within each type, or that conversion is possible between necessary and theoretical knowledge.
Consider how each interpretation resolves the objection.
3
Reflect on the argument's strength
Ask yourself why this defense is sufficient in the theological context. Does it fully answer the objection?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد سبق أن التصور (۳) والتصديق (۳) مختلفان بالحقيقة قلنا (۱) العلم الضروري : هو ما يحصل من غير فكر وكسب والعلم الاكتسابي الذي يحصل بالنظر والبحث، وهو عقل وعمل، فالعقلي : هو ما يحصل بالنظر والتأمل، ويسمى بالعلم النظري، والعملي : هو ما يحصل بالعمل والتجربة. (۲) تصور الشيء : تخيله وتصور له الشيء، صارت له عنده صورة والتصور عند علماء النفس : هو حصول صورة الشيء في العقل وعند المناطقة: هو إدراك الماهية من غير أن يحكم عليها بنفي أو إثبات . (تعريفات الجرجاني). (۳) العلم عند الفلاسفة القدماء. إما تصور فقط، وهو حصول صورة الشيء في العقل، وإما تصور حكم وهو إسناد أمر إلى آخر إيجاباً أو سلباً، ويقال لهذا التصور المصحوب بالحكم معه تصديق . راجع القطب على الشمسية ص (٦) . والتَّصَوُّر يكتسب بالحد وما يجري مجراه والتصديق إنما يكتسب بالقياس أو ما يجري مجراه مثل تصديقنا بأن للكل مبدأ . (راجع النجاة ص ٣ : ٤ ) . ٣٢٢ لعله (۱) أراد بالعلم ما هو أحد أقسام التصديق على ما اشتهر فيما بين المتكلمين، أو ادعى أن حقيقة الكل هى الصفة الموجبة للتميز على ماسبق أو أراد أن التصورات متماثلة وكذا التصديقات، فيجوز على الضروري من كل منهما أن ينقلب إلى النظري منه .
If it is said: It has been established before that concept (taṣawwur) and assent (taṣdīq) are different in reality. We say: (1) Necessary knowledge: it is what is obtained without thought and acquisition, and acquired knowledge is what is obtained by reflection and investigation, and it is intellect and action. The rational is what is obtained by reflection and contemplation, and is called theoretical knowledge, and the practical is what is obtained by action and experience. (2) Concept of a thing: its representation, and when the thing is conceptualized, one has an image of it. Concept according to psychologists is the obtaining of an image of the thing in the mind, and according to logicians is the apprehension of the essence without judging it by negation or affirmation. (Definitions of al-Jurjānī). (3) Knowledge according to the ancient philosophers: either concept only, which is the obtaining of an image of the thing in the mind, or judgmental concept, which is ascribing one thing to another affirmatively or negatively, and this concept accompanied by judgment is called assent. Refer to al-Quṭb ʿalā al-Shamsiyya p. 6. And concept is acquired by definition and what is like it, and assent is only acquired by syllogism or what is like it, such as our assent that the whole has a beginning. (Refer to al-Najāt p. 3:4). Perhaps (1) he meant by knowledge one of the divisions of assent, as is well-known among the theologians, or he claimed that the reality of all is the attribute that necessitates distinction (tamyīz) as previously mentioned, or he meant that concepts are homogeneous and likewise assents, so it is permissible for the necessary of each of them to become theoretical.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This lesson addresses the objection that concept and assent are different, so homogeneity cannot apply. The author gives possible interpretations of the term 'ilm: it might be restricted to assent, or the claim is that both types are internally homogeneous, so conversion is possible within each type. The footnote definitions (which are editorial) clarify the terminology of necessary and acquired knowledge, concept, and assent. The commentary follows the order: objection, then the answer with alternative interpretations.

Misconception: Some may think that 'ilm always encompasses both tasawwur and tasdiq equally, so pointing out their difference disproves homogeneity.
Correction: The author shows that 'ilm might refer specifically to tasdiq, or that homogeneity applies within each separate category, preserving the claim.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one of the author's suggested interpretations of 'ilm in response to the objection?
A) 'ilm includes both tasawwur and tasdiq
B) 'ilm refers to tasdiq alone
C) 'ilm is irrelevant
D) 'ilm is only intuitive

Key Takeaway
The objection that concept and assent differ does not necessarily undermine the homogeneity of knowledge, because 'ilm may be understood as assent, or homogeneity may apply within each type.

?

Necessary Assent: Definition and Verbal Dispute
Clarifies the definition of necessary assent and the verbal nature of the dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of necessary assent in Islamic theology and why the disagreement over its nature is largely verbal.

Core Idea
The dispute over necessary knowledge is verbal, not substantive.

In this passage, the author clarifies what theologians mean by 'necessary assent'. He shows that apparent disagreements vanish when we distinguish between the inference needed for the judgment and the inference needed for the terms. Understanding this nuance is crucial for grasping how knowledge is built in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read carefully to follow the logical flow: a hypothetical, then a conclusion about verbal dispute, then the truth, then the real point of contention.
Notice the use of 'فإن' and 'فمن هاهنا'.
2
Identify the two interpretations of necessary assent
Find where the author presents two possible definitions of 'necessary assent': one that requires no inference at all, and one that requires no inference in the judgment itself.
The second definition allows inference for the terms.
3
Understand the author's resolution
The author states the truth: theologians use 'knowledge' for a type of assent, and 'necessary' means not attained by inference. Then he points to the real dispute: whether knowledge can be built on inferential knowledge.
This shifts the focus from definition to epistemology.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Ask yourself: why is it important to distinguish verbal from substantive disputes? How does this affect later arguments about the foundations of knowledge?
This connects to broader kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن سمي يكون التصديق المستغني عن النظر فيه مفتقراً إلى النظر في تصور الظرفين، مثله ضرورياً كان مستنداً إلى النظري، فمن هاهنا قيل إن هذا نزاع لفظي يرجع إلى تفسير التصديق الضروري أنه الذي لا يفتقر إلى النظر أصلا، أو لا يفتقر إلى النظر في نفس الحكم وإن كان طرفاه بالنظر، والحق أن مراد المتكلمين بالعلم ما هو من أقسام التصديق، وبالضروري منه ما لا يكون حصوله بطريق الاستدلال عليه، والمتنازع هو أنه هل يجوز أن يبتنى على علم حاصل بالاستدلال.
For if it is said that the assent that is independent of inference in it is dependent on inference in the conceptualization of the two terms, like it, it would be necessary, but based on the theoretical. Therefore, it was said that this is a verbal dispute that boils down to the interpretation of necessary assent: is it that which does not require inference at all, or that which does not require inference in the judgment itself even if its two terms are by inference? The truth is that the aim of the theologians with 'knowledge' is that which is among the divisions of assent, and by 'necessary' thereof they mean that whose attainment is not through the method of inference upon it. The point of dispute is whether it is permissible for it to be based on knowledge obtained by inference.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of necessary knowledge (التصديق الضروري) and clarifies that the apparent disagreement is largely verbal. The author begins by positing a scenario: if we say that an assent that is independent of inference in the act of judging is nonetheless dependent on inference for the conceptualization of its two terms, then it would be a necessary assent but based on theoretical reasoning. From this arises the verbal dispute: does necessary assent mean that which requires no inference at all, or that which requires no inference in the judgment itself even if its terms are known by inference? The author states that the truth (الحق) is that the theologians (المتكلمين) intend by 'knowledge' (العلم) a type of assent (تصديق), and by 'necessary' (الضروري) they mean that its attainment is not through the method of inference (الاستدلال). The actual point of contention (المتنازع) is whether it is permissible for knowledge to be built upon knowledge obtained by inference.

Key Terms

النظر
الاستدلال
العلم
النزاع اللفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary knowledge cannot be based on any inference at all.
Correction: Necessary knowledge may depend on inference for its terms, but not for the judgment itself; the dispute is whether it can be built on inferential knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the actual point of dispute among theologians regarding necessary knowledge?
A) Whether necessary knowledge exists.
B) Whether necessary knowledge requires no inference in the judgment or no inference at all.
C) Whether knowledge obtained by inference can be based on knowledge obtained by inference.
D) Whether necessary knowledge is acquired or innate.

Key Takeaway
The apparent dispute over the definition of necessary knowledge is verbal; the real substantive issue is whether knowledge can be founded on inferential knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief you considered 'necessary' (self-evident) only to realize it rested on assumptions you never examined? How will you test the foundations of that belief today?
?
This lesson distinguishes between a verbal dispute and a real one. In your own life, do you sometimes argue about words while missing the deeper issue? Commit to identifying one such argument this week and seeking the real point.
?
If even necessary assent can be based on inference, what does that mean for your certainty about the most fundamental truths of your faith? Will you investigate their grounds, or accept them on authority? What cost does each choice carry?

The Basic Disagreement: Does Contingent Knowledge Multiply with Known Objects?
Presents the core question and two opposing positions on multiplicity of contingent knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two primary positions on whether contingent knowledge multiplies when known objects multiply, and the role of relation (taʿalluq) in their reasoning.

Core Idea
Contingent knowledge multiplies if relation is internal; otherwise it is one.

The debate on whether contingent knowledge multiplies with its known objects hinges on the nature of relation (taʿalluq) within knowledge. This lesson presents the two basic camps: those who affirm multiplicity because relation is intrinsic, and those who deny it because relation is extrinsic, analogous to eternal knowledge. This foundational issue shapes later discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic chunk carefully, noting how the positions are introduced. Then read the translation to grasp the reasoning.
Notice the phrase 'لأن التعلق داخل فيه' – this is crucial.
2
Identify the two main views
The Sheikh (al-Ashʿarī) and many Muʿtazila say 'yes'; others say 'no'. Mark how each justifies its stance.
3
Understand the role of relation
The concept of relation (taʿalluq) is central. For the first group, relation is part of knowledge; for the second, it is external. Ask yourself why this matters.
This connects to the later disagreement on the definition of knowledge.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this disagreement is not merely terminological but affects the ontology of knowledge. The passage hints that it is a branch of the definition of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس اختلاف العلماء في تعدد العلم الحادث بتعدد المعلوم (قال : المبحث الخامس : هل يتعدد العلم الحادث بتعدد المعلوم؟ قال الشيخ، وكثير من المعتزلة . نعم، لأن التعلق داخل فيه ، وقيل : لا لكونه خارجاً كما في القديم فهو فرع الخلاف في تفسير العلم، وقيل: يتعدد إن كان المعلومان نظريين لئلا يلزم اجتماع النظرين في علم ، ورد بجواز أن يحصلا بنظر كما علما بعلم وهو ضعيف . وقال القاضي والإمام : يتعدد إن كان المعلومان مما يجوز انفكاك العلم بهما، وإلا لزم جواز انفكاك الشيء عن نفسه، ورد بأنه قد يعلم تارة بعلم وتارة بعلمين . اتفق القائلون بالعلم القديم على أنه واحد يتعلق بمعلومات متعددة، واختلفوا فى الحادث فذهب الشيخ وكثير من المعتزلة إلى أن الواحد منه يمتنع أن يتعلق بمعلومين . وهذا هو المعنى بقولنا بتعدد العلم بتعدد المعلوم . وذهب بعض الأصحاب إلى أنه يجوز ، وجعل الإمام الرازي الخلاف مبنياً على الخلاف في تفسير العلم أنه إضافة فيكون التعلق بهذا عين (۱) التعلق بذاك، أو صفة ذات إضافة فيجوز أن يكون للواحد تعلقات بأمور متعددة كالعلم القديم .
The Fifth Topic: The disagreement of scholars concerning the multiplicity of contingent knowledge with the multiplicity of the known. (He said: The Fifth Topic: Does contingent knowledge multiply with the multiplicity of the known? The Sheikh and many of the Mu'tazila said: Yes, because relation (taʿalluq) is included in it. It was said: No, because it is external, as in the eternal [knowledge], so it is a branch of the disagreement in the interpretation of knowledge. And it was said: It multiplies if the two knowns are theoretical, to avoid the conjunction of the two acts of theoretical reasoning (naẓar) in one knowledge. This was responded to by the permissibility of their being obtained by one theoretical reasoning just as they are known by one knowledge, but it is weak. Al-Qadi and the Imam said: It multiplies if the two knowns are such that the separation of knowledge of one from knowledge of the other is possible; otherwise, the separation of a thing from itself would be possible. This was responded to by [the fact that] sometimes it is known by one knowledge and sometimes by two knowledges. Those who affirm eternal knowledge agreed that it is one and relates to multiple knowns, but they disagreed concerning contingent [knowledge]: the Sheikh and many of the Mu'tazila held that it is impossible for one contingent knowledge to relate to two knowns. This is what is meant by our saying: the multiplicity of knowledge with the multiplicity of the known. And some of the companions went to its permissibility. Imam al-Razi made the disagreement based on the disagreement in the interpretation of knowledge: [whether] it is a relation, such that the relation to this is identical to the relation to that, or an attribute having relation, so that it is possible for the one [knowledge] to have relations to multiple things, like eternal knowledge.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth topic, surveying the major positions on whether contingent knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḥādith) multiplies when its known objects (al-maʿlūmāt) multiply. The author first lists several views: the Sheikh (likely referring to al-Ashʿarī) and many Muʿtazila affirm multiplicity because relation (taʿalluq) is internal to knowledge; others deny it because relation is external, as in God's eternal knowledge, making the issue a branch of the definition of knowledge. A third view restricts multiplicity to theoretical knowns to avoid combining two acts of theoretical reasoning (naẓar) in one knowledge, but this is rebutted weakly. Al-Qāḍī (al-Bāqillānī) and the Imām (al-Juwaynī) condition multiplicity on the possibility of separate knowledge of each known, lest a thing separate from itself. The passage then notes consensus that eternal knowledge is one relating to many knowns, but disagreement on contingent knowledge: the Sheikh and Muʿtazila forbid a single contingent knowledge from relating to two knowns. Finally, it presents Imam al-Rāzī's framing: the disagreement reduces to whether knowledge is a pure relation (ʿayn al-taʿalluq) or an attribute possessing relation (ṣifa dhāt iḍāfa), the latter allowing multiple relations like eternal knowledge. The author systematically catalogs positions, showing how foundational definitions shape the debate.

Key Terms
العلم الحادث
تعدد المعلوم
التعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the disagreement is merely about whether we can know multiple things at once.
Correction: The disagreement is about the nature of knowledge itself: whether relation is essential or accidental, which has implications for the unity or multiplicity of knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the Sheikh and many Mu'tazila affirm that contingent knowledge multiplies with known objects?
A) Because relation is external to knowledge.
B) Because relation is included in knowledge.
C) Because they follow the example of eternal knowledge.
D) Because they define knowledge as an attribute without relation.

Key Takeaway
The multiplicity of contingent knowledge depends on whether relation is considered internal or external to knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new attribute of Allah, do you treat it as an isolated piece of information (adding another 'knowledge') or as an opportunity to deepen a single, coherent understanding? Which position in this debate does your approach reflect, and what does that say about how you view the nature of your relationship with Allah?
?
The passage shows that the disagreement hinges on whether relation is internal or external to knowledge. Consider your own knowledge of Allah: is it a set of facts externally related to you, or is it a living, internal relation that transforms you? Take one attribute you struggled to connect with—how can you make that relation internal?
?
If the Sheikh and the Mu'tazila are right that each known object requires a distinct relation, then what you know demands a separate connection. How many distinct knowledges of Allah do you actually have right now? What would it take to have a real, living relation with each of His attributes, not just an intellectual list?

Nuanced Positions: Theoretical Knowns and Condition of Separability
Presents additional positions and the persistent disagreement on contingent knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional positions on the multiplication of contingent knowledge: the restriction to theoretical knowns and the condition of possible separation of knowledge, along with their rebuttals.

Core Idea
Even among those who allow multiplicity, there are conditions and objections.

After the basic disagreement, the passage presents two more refined views. One restricts multiplicity to theoretical knowns to avoid combining two acts of reasoning in one knowledge; another conditions it on the possibility of knowing each known separately. Both are met with counterarguments, showing the complexity of the debate. This lesson unpacks these subtler positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the chunk starting from 'وقيل: يتعدد إن كان المعلومان نظريين' through 'واختلفوا فى الحادث فذهب الشيخ...'. Pay attention to the rebuttals.
2
Identify the third view
The view that multiplicity applies only when the knowns are theoretical (naẓarī). Understand why: to avoid two acts of reasoning (naẓar) in one knowledge.
Note the rebuttal: it is possible that both knowns are obtained by a single act of reasoning.
3
Analyze the view of al-Qadi and al-Imam
They condition multiplicity on the possibility of disconnection of knowledge from one known and not the other. Their reasoning is that otherwise a thing would be separate from itself.
The rebuttal points out that the same object can be known by one knowledge at one time and by two knowledges at another.
4
See the consensus and the ongoing disagreement
The passage then notes consensus on eternal knowledge being one with multiple relations, but disagreement on contingent knowledge. The Ash'ari/Mu'tazila position forbids one contingent knowledge relating to two knowns, while some companions allow it.
This reinforces the centrality of the relation concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس اختلاف العلماء في تعدد العلم الحادث بتعدد المعلوم (قال : المبحث الخامس : هل يتعدد العلم الحادث بتعدد المعلوم؟ قال الشيخ، وكثير من المعتزلة . نعم، لأن التعلق داخل فيه ، وقيل : لا لكونه خارجاً كما في القديم فهو فرع الخلاف في تفسير العلم، وقيل: يتعدد إن كان المعلومان نظريين لئلا يلزم اجتماع النظرين في علم ، ورد بجواز أن يحصلا بنظر كما علما بعلم وهو ضعيف . وقال القاضي والإمام : يتعدد إن كان المعلومان مما يجوز انفكاك العلم بهما، وإلا لزم جواز انفكاك الشيء عن نفسه، ورد بأنه قد يعلم تارة بعلم وتارة بعلمين . اتفق القائلون بالعلم القديم على أنه واحد يتعلق بمعلومات متعددة، واختلفوا فى الحادث فذهب الشيخ وكثير من المعتزلة إلى أن الواحد منه يمتنع أن يتعلق بمعلومين . وهذا هو المعنى بقولنا بتعدد العلم بتعدد المعلوم . وذهب بعض الأصحاب إلى أنه يجوز ، وجعل الإمام الرازي الخلاف مبنياً على الخلاف في تفسير العلم أنه إضافة فيكون التعلق بهذا عين (۱) التعلق بذاك، أو صفة ذات إضافة فيجوز أن يكون للواحد تعلقات بأمور متعددة كالعلم القديم .
The Fifth Topic: The disagreement of scholars concerning the multiplicity of contingent knowledge with the multiplicity of the known. (He said: The Fifth Topic: Does contingent knowledge multiply with the multiplicity of the known? The Sheikh and many of the Mu'tazila said: Yes, because relation (taʿalluq) is included in it. It was said: No, because it is external, as in the eternal [knowledge], so it is a branch of the disagreement in the interpretation of knowledge. And it was said: It multiplies if the two knowns are theoretical, to avoid the conjunction of the two acts of theoretical reasoning (naẓar) in one knowledge. This was responded to by the permissibility of their being obtained by one theoretical reasoning just as they are known by one knowledge, but it is weak. Al-Qadi and the Imam said: It multiplies if the two knowns are such that the separation of knowledge of one from knowledge of the other is possible; otherwise, the separation of a thing from itself would be possible. This was responded to by [the fact that] sometimes it is known by one knowledge and sometimes by two knowledges. Those who affirm eternal knowledge agreed that it is one and relates to multiple knowns, but they disagreed concerning contingent [knowledge]: the Sheikh and many of the Mu'tazila held that it is impossible for one contingent knowledge to relate to two knowns. This is what is meant by our saying: the multiplicity of knowledge with the multiplicity of the known. And some of the companions went to its permissibility. Imam al-Razi made the disagreement based on the disagreement in the interpretation of knowledge: [whether] it is a relation, such that the relation to this is identical to the relation to that, or an attribute having relation, so that it is possible for the one [knowledge] to have relations to multiple things, like eternal knowledge.
Source pages p. 325

A third view restricts multiplicity to theoretical knowns to avoid combining two acts of theoretical reasoning (naẓar) in one knowledge, but this is rebutted weakly. Al-Qāḍī (al-Bāqillānī) and the Imām (al-Juwaynī) condition multiplicity on the possibility of separate knowledge of each known, lest a thing separate from itself. The passage then notes consensus that eternal knowledge is one relating to many knowns, but disagreement on contingent knowledge: the Sheikh and Muʿtazila forbid a single contingent knowledge from relating to two knowns. Finally, it presents Imam al-Rāzī's framing: the disagreement reduces to whether knowledge is a pure relation (ʿayn al-taʿalluq) or an attribute possessing relation (ṣifa dhāt iḍāfa), the latter allowing multiple relations like eternal knowledge. The author systematically catalogs positions, showing how foundational definitions shape the debate.

B) That a thing can be known by one knowledge at one time and by two at another.

D) That the relation is external.

Key Takeaway
The debate on multiplicity includes conditions like being theoretical or separable, but each condition has rebuttals, showing the issue is nuanced.

?

Al-Razi's Resolution: The Role of Definition
Al-Razi traces the disagreement to the definition of knowledge as relation or attribute-with-relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's framing that the disagreement on multiplicity of knowledge reduces to the definition of knowledge as either a pure relation or an attribute with relation.

Core Idea
The whole debate hinges on whether knowledge is essentially a relation or an attribute that has relations.

After surveying the various positions, the passage concludes with Imam al-Razi's insightful analysis. He traces the entire disagreement to a foundational question: the nature of knowledge itself. Is knowledge a pure relation (iḍāfa) such that its relation to one known is identical to its relation to another, or is it an attribute that possesses relation (ṣifa dhāt iḍāfa), allowing one knowledge to have multiple relations like eternal knowledge? This lesson examines al-Razi's diagnosis and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the final Arabic segment
Read the last part of the passage: 'وجعل الإمام الرازي الخلاف...'. Focus on the two options: knowledge as 'إضافة' vs 'صفة ذات إضافة'.
2
Understand al-Razi's move
He argues that the disagreement is not just about multiplicity but about what knowledge is. If knowledge is a pure relation, then multiple relations would mean multiple knowledges. If it is an attribute that has relations, one knowledge can have many relations.
This is reminiscent of the earlier distinction about relation being internal or external.
3
Connect to the positions
Relate this to the earlier positions: the Sheikh and Mu'tazila likely view knowledge as a pure relation, hence they require multiplicity. The companions who allow one knowledge for two knowns likely view it as an attribute with relations.
4
Appreciate the synthesis
Al-Razi's framing shows that the debate is not just a set of independent opinions but logically derives from a more basic metaphysical choice.
This is a characteristic of kalam: reducing disputes to foundational definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

Will: Distinct from Desire and Aversion
Al-Taftazani defines will and distinguishes it from desire and aversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that will (al-irāda) is a distinct psychic quality from desire and aversion, and you will grasp its intuitive yet conceptually subtle nature.

Core Idea
Will is not the same as desire — you can will what you dislike.

Al-Taftazani begins by acknowledging that will seems obvious to the mind, yet its true essence is difficult to capture. He then clarifies that will differs from desire just as aversion differs from antipathy. This distinction is illustrated with everyday examples that show how we often will things we do not desire and desire things we do not will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
2
Notice the author's claim about will's clarity and difficulty
Al-Taftazani says will's meaning is clear to the intellect but its true essence is hard to know and express.
This sets up the need for careful distinction.
3
Identify the distinctions
Find where he distinguishes will from desire, and aversion from antipathy. Observe the parallel structure.
The distinction is made by contrasting opposites.
4
Examine the examples
Consider the example of bitter medicine (willed but not desired) and delicious harmful food (desired but not willed). These clarify the difference.
These examples are grounded in everyday experience.
5
Reflect on the importance
Why does this distinction matter? In theology, it helps us understand divine will as separate from human desires and passions.
This connects to later discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي من الكيفيات النفسانية، الإرادة ويشبه أن يكون معناها واضحاً عند العقل غير ملتبس بغيرها، إلا أنه تعسر معرفتها بكنه الحقيقة، والتعبير عنها بما يفيد تصورها وهي تغاير الشهوة كما أن مقابلها وهي الكراهية تغاير النفرة ولهذا قد يريد الإنسان ما لا يشتهيه كشرب دواء كريه ينفعه وقد يشتهي ما لا يريده كأكل طعام لذيذ يضره
One of the psychic qualities, will, and it seems that its meaning is clear to the intellect, not confused with other [qualities], except that its knowledge in its true essence is difficult, and expressing it in a way that gives its conception [is difficult]. And it is different from desire just as its opposite, which is aversion, is different from antipathy. For this reason, a person may will what he does not desire, like drinking a bitter medicine that benefits him, and he may desire what he does not will, like eating a delicious food that harms him.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage introduces the definition of will (al-irāda). The author begins by stating that will is one of the psychic qualities, and its meaning is intuitively clear to the intellect and not confused with other concepts. However, knowing its true essence (kunh al-ḥaqīqa) is difficult, as is expressing it in a way that conveys its conception. Then he distinguishes will from desire (shahwa): just as the opposite of will, which is aversion (karāhiyya), differs from antipathy (nafra), so too will differs from desire. He provides examples: a person may will something he does not desire, such as drinking a bitter medicine that benefits him, and he may desire something he does not will, such as eating a delicious food that harms him. This shows the distinction between will and desire, and similarly between aversion and antipathy.

الشهوة
الكراهية
النفرة
الكيفيات النفسانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will is identical to desire or aversion.
Correction: Will is distinct: one can will without desiring and desire without willing, as shown by the examples of bitter medicine and harmful delicious food.

Checkpoint

B) Eating a delicious food that harms
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
Will is a distinct psychic quality from desire and aversion, as shown by examples of willing beneficial bitter medicine and desiring harmful delicious food.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage admits that will is clear to the intellect yet difficult to express. When have you struggled to explain why you willed something to someone else? How does the difficulty of articulating inner states remind you of the limits of human knowledge and the need for divine guidance in understanding the soul?
