The Two Cases: Stone and Bird
Presents two cases and the author's ruling on motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that motion is not simply a change of adjacencies, as demonstrated by the stone in water and the bird in wind.

Core Idea
Motion is defined by internal change, not just external adjacency.

The author presents two perplexing cases that challenge a simplistic definition of motion. He then gives a clear judgement rooted in reason and custom. Let's examine these cases closely before we consider the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage describing the two cases
Read the Arabic passage once. Note the two examples: stone in flowing water and bird in wind.
The stone appears still but its adjacencies change due to water movement.
2
Identify the author's ruling
Find where the author states which case is motion and which is rest. The phrase 'الحق أن الأول حركة، الثاني سكون' is key.
3
Reflect on the stone case
Consider why the stone, despite no apparent movement, is classified as motion. Think about the internal parts.
The author relies on reason and custom, not just appearance.
4
Reflect on the bird case
Consider why the bird, despite changing adjacencies due to wind, is classified as rest.
The bird itself does not change its position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : حال الجسم المستقر المتبدل محاذياته بواسطة حركة بعض ما يحيط به من الأجسام كالحجر المستقر على الأرض في الماء الجاري، وكالطير الواقف في الجو عند هبوب الرياح والحق أن الأول حركة ، الثاني سكون بشهادة العقل والعرف . وقد يستدل على الأول بأنه لو كان ساكناً مع حركة باقي الأجزاء لزم الانفكاك أي انفصال بعض الأجزاء عن البعض، وبأن الأجزاء الباطنة في الأجزاء الظاهرة، وهي في الحيّز فتكون الباطنة أيضاً فيه، وقد انتقل منه إلى آخر، وعلى الثاني بأنه لو كان متحركاً لزم التحرك في حالة واحدة إلى جهتين مختلفتين عند اختلاف جهات حركات الأجسام المحيطة به عليه، بأن يتحرك البعض عليه آخذاً من يمنته إلى يسرته والبعض بالعكس والكل ضعيف .
Second: The case of a stationary body whose adjacencies change by the motion of some of the bodies surrounding it, like a stone resting on the earth in flowing water, and like a bird standing still in the air when winds blow. The truth is that the first is motion, the second is rest, according to the testimony of reason and custom. And it may be argued for the first: that if it were stationary while the rest of its parts were moving, then separation (infikāk) would necessarily occur, i.e., the separation of some parts from others; and because the internal parts are within the external parts, and they are in a place (ḥayyiz), so the internal parts are also therein, and they have moved from it to another. And for the second: that if it were moving, then it would have to move in one state to two different directions when the directions of the motions of the bodies surrounding it differ, such that some move upon it taking from its right to its left, and others the opposite. And all of this is weak.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage presents two cases that challenge the simple definition of motion as change of place. The first case is of a stone resting on the ground in flowing water: the stone's adjacencies change because the water around it moves, but the stone itself does not move. The author states that this is actually motion (because the internal parts of the stone move relative to the external parts? Actually the argument for the first case says if the stone were stationary while its surrounding parts (like the water) move, then the internal parts would separate from the external—so it is motion). The second case is of a bird hovering in the wind: the bird remains in place but the air around it moves, so its adjacencies change; this is rest. The author provides arguments: For the first, if the stone were stationary while the surrounding water moves, the internal parts of the stone (which are in the same place as the external parts) would have to move away, causing separation. For the second, if the bird were moving, it would have to move in two opposite directions at once due to different wind directions, which is impossible. The author concludes that all those arguments are weak, indicating that the matter is more nuanced and will be addressed with objections and answers later.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
محاذيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that any change in surroundings constitutes motion for an object.
Correction: The author shows that only an internal change, not mere external adjacency change, defines motion. The bird's surroundings change but it remains at rest.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered motion?
A) A bird hovering in the wind
B) A stone resting on the ground in flowing water
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Motion requires an internal change of the body, not just a change in its surroundings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When the 'winds' of your life—opinions of others, changing circumstances—blow around you, are you internally at rest like the bird, or are you being moved like the stone? What does this passage expose about the state of your heart?
?
The stone in water is called motion because its internal parts are genuinely affected. Are there areas in your life where you have convinced yourself that you are 'at rest' spiritually, but the world around you has actually changed your interior? What will you do this week to check your internal state?
?
If the truth of motion and rest is determined by internal change and not by external appearance, what does that mean for your deen? Are you merely standing still while the winds of this dunya rush around you, or are you actively changed by your relationship with Allah? Commit to one action that proves your motion is real.

The Arguments and Their Weakness
Presents arguments for the two rulings and declares them weak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments given to support the rulings and why the author considers them weak.

Core Idea
The arguments for motion and rest are ingenious but ultimately flawed.

After establishing the rulings, the author presents arguments that seem compelling. However, he concludes they are all weak. This lesson examines those arguments and the author's critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the arguments carefully
Read the Arabic passage containing the arguments for both cases. The first argument is introduced by 'ويستدل على الأول' and the second by 'وعلى الثاني'.
2
Understand the argument for motion (stone)
The argument says: if the stone were stationary while other parts move, separation (infikāk) would occur. The internal parts are within the external ones and occupy the same place, so they would have to move.
This argument is based on the impossibility of separation.
3
Understand the argument for rest (bird)
The argument says: if the bird moved, it would have to move in two opposite directions at once when winds blow from different sides, which is absurd.
This uses the impossibility of contradictory motions.
4
Note the author's verdict
The author says 'and all of this is weak' (والكل ضعيف). Understand that the author is not endorsing these arguments.
The weakness indicates that the real proof lies elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Objections and Answers on Motion and Rest
Presents objections, answers, and reduces the dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how disputes about motion and rest reduce to disagreements about the nature of spatial position (ḥayyiz), and why the author insists this is a real, not merely terminological, dispute.

Core Idea
The dispute over motion and rest is really about what 'place' means.

This passage presents objections to earlier arguments about motion and rest in the stone and bird cases, along with answers. The author then reveals that the entire controversy boils down to how we define the spatial position (ḥayyiz) of a body. Understanding this clarifies why the dispute is substantive and not just about naming.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections and answers
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the arguments and counterarguments. Notice the two distinct objections (stone case and bird case) and their respective answers.
The objections challenge earlier claims about motion and rest.
2
Identify the two cases and key concepts
The first objection concerns the internal part of the stone not leaving its place; the second concerns the bird attaining a new place. Key concepts: ḥayyiz, al-buʿd al-mafṭūr, and muḥādhāt (adjacencies).
Understand how each side defines motion.
3
Reduce the dispute to definitions of ḥayyiz
The author shows that the disagreement reduces to two questions about what constitutes ḥayyiz: whether it is the occupied dimension, surrounding atoms, or innate dimension. Follow the reasoning carefully.
This reduction is the heart of the passage.
4
Reflect on the author's rejection of terminological dispute
The author explicitly rejects the claim in al-Mawaqif that the dispute is merely about terminology. He insists it is about real essences, because the definitions are meant to capture the reality underlying the words across languages.
Why does the author think this is important?
5
Consider the role of essences in kalām
The passage ends by emphasizing that judgments like 'this is in a place' require a real understanding of essences, not just names. Ask yourself how this affects the validity of the arguments.
This connects to broader kalām methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المخالف في الأول بأن الجزء الباطن لم يفارق حيزه الذي هو الأجزاء المحيطة به ولا حركة بدون مفارقة الحيز وأجيب بأن حيز الكل حيزه ، وقد فارقته وفي الثاني بأنه حصل في حيز هو ما يحيط به من الجواهر بعد الحصول في حيز آخر ، ولا معنى للحركة سوى هذا وبأنه قد تبدلت عليه محاذياته وهو نفس الحركة أو ملزومها . وأجيب بأن حيزه البعد المفطور وهـو بعـد حـاصل فيه، ولو سلم فالحصول في الحيز الثاني إنما يكون حركة إذا كان بزواله عن الأول دون العكس، وبأن تبدل المحاذيات إنما يستلزم الحركة إذا كان من جهة المتحيز بأن يزول من محاذاة إلى محاذاة، فظهر أن الخلاف في الأول عائد إلى الخلاف في حيز الجزء الباطن إنه حيز الكل أعني البعد المشغول به أو الجواهر المحيطة به أم ماله خاصة من البعد أو الأجزاء المحيطة به . وفي الثاني إلى الخلاف في أن الحيز هو البعد المفطور الذي لا يفارقه المستقر بتحرك الجواهر المحيطة، وتبدل المحاذيات بذلك أم الجواهر المحيطة به على ما يناسب قول الفلاسفة من أنه السطح الباطن من الحاوي، وعلى هذا التقدير هل يتوقف حصول الحركة على أن يكون مفارقة الحيز وتبدل المحاذيات من جهة المتحيز ،البتة أم يحصل بأن يزول الحيز عنه، وعن محاذاته، وعلى الأول يمتنع حركة الجسم في حالة واحدة إلى جهتين مختلفتين وعلى الثاني لا يمتنع كما إذا تحرك بعض الجواهر المحيطة يمنة، والبعض يسرة على ما يلزمه الأستاذ أبو إسحق، وإن شدد غيره النكير عليه حقاً فإن العقل جازم بأن ذلك ليس بحركه وأن حركة الجسم في حالة واحدة لا تكون إلى جهتين، وما ذكر في المواقف من أن هذا نزاع في التسمية ليس على ما ينبغي لأن ما ذكره الأستاذ وغيره في بيان الحيز أو الحركة، أنه هذا أو ذاك ليس اصطلاحا منهم على أنا نجعله اسماً لذلك، وإلا لما كان لجلعه من المسائل العلمية والاستدلال عليه بالأدلة العقلية معنى بل تحقيقاً للماهية التي وضع لفظ الحيز أو الحركة وما يرادفه من جميع اللغات بإزائها، وإثبات ذاتياتها بعد تصورها بالحقيقة حين يحكم بأن هذا في حيز، وذاك في حيز آخر وأن هذا متحرك وذاك ساكن .
The opponent objected to the first case: that the internal part did not leave its place (ḥayyiz), which is the parts surrounding it, and there is no motion without leaving the place. And it was answered: that the place of the whole is its place, and it has left it. And to the second case: that it obtained a place which is the atoms surrounding it after having been in another place, and motion means nothing other than this; and that its adjacencies changed, and that is motion itself or its necessary concomitant. And it was answered: that its place is the innate dimension (al-buʿd al-mafṭūr) which is a dimension already existing in it; and even if it is granted, obtaining the second place is only motion if it is by leaving the first, not the opposite; and that change of adjacencies only entails motion if it is from the side of the thing occupying place (al-mutaḥayyiz), i.e., that it moves from one adjacency to another. Thus it appears that the dispute in the first case reduces to a dispute about the place of the internal part: whether it is the place of the whole, i.e., the occupied dimension, or the surrounding atoms, or its own particular dimension or surrounding parts. And in the second case, to a dispute about whether place is the innate dimension which the stationary thing does not leave by the motion of the surrounding atoms, and change of adjacencies occurs by that, or the surrounding atoms, as agrees with the statement of the philosophers that it is the inner surface of the container. On this basis, does the occurrence of motion require that the leaving of place and change of adjacencies be from the side of the thing occupying place absolutely, or does it occur by the place itself moving away from it and its adjacencies? On the first, motion of a body in one state to two different directions is impossible; on the second, it is not impossible, as when some surrounding atoms move to the right and some to the left, as required by Master Abu Ishaq, though others strongly criticized him, rightly, for reason is certain that this is not motion and that the motion of a body in one state cannot be to two directions. And what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif that this is a dispute about terminology is not as it should be, because what the Master and others mentioned in explaining place or motion—that it is this or that—is not a mere convention of theirs that we make a name for that; otherwise there would be no meaning to making it a scientific problem and proving it by rational proofs; rather it is a verification of the essence (al-mahiyya) for which the word 'place' or 'motion' and its equivalents in all languages are placed, and establishing their essential attributes after conceiving them in reality when one judges that this is in a place and that is in another place, and that this is moving and that is stationary.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange that clarifies the true nature of the dispute about motion and rest. The opponent raises two objections: (1) In the stone case, the internal part of the stone does not leave its place (the surrounding parts), so there is no motion. The answer: the place of the whole stone is its place, and it has left that. (2) In the bird case, the bird attains a new place (the surrounding atoms) after having been in another, so it is motion; also its adjacencies change, which is motion or its concomitant. The answer: its place is the innate dimension (al-buʿd al-mafṭūr) which it always retains, and even if we grant attainment of a new place, it is only motion if it leaves the previous place, not merely because the surroundings change; also change of adjacencies only entails motion if it comes from the side of the thing itself moving from one adjacency to another. The author concludes that the disagreement reduces to two questions: (A) In the first case, what is the place (ḥayyiz) of the internal part? Is it the place of the whole (the occupied dimension), or the surrounding atoms, or its own specific dimension or parts? (B) In the second case, is place the innate dimension (which the stationary thing never leaves) or the surrounding atoms (as philosophers say it is the inner surface of the container)? Depending on these definitions, motion may or may not require the thing itself to leave its place and change adjacencies. The author then rejects the claim found in al-Mawaqif that this is merely a terminological dispute. He insists it is a real dispute about the essences of place and motion, because the definitions given are not mere conventions but are intended to capture the realities underlying the words across languages. The passage ends by emphasizing that the judgment 'this is in a place, that is in another' and 'this is moving, that is stationary' requires a real understanding of the essences, not just names.

Correction: The author argues that the definitions of place and motion are not mere conventions but aim to capture the realities, so the dispute is substantive and concerns the essences themselves.

Two Philosophical Definitions of Motion
Al-Taftazani presents two definitions of motion with explanatory commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classical philosophical definitions of motion and the key conditions attached to each.

Core Idea
Motion is gradual actualization or first perfection of potentiality.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents two ways the philosophers defined motion. The first focuses on the gradual process of actualization, while the second defines motion as the first perfection of a potential being. Both definitions grapple with the relationship between potentiality, actuality, and time. Let us examine each definition and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with 'قيل' (it was said), indicating received definitions.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the key phrases: 'الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على التدريج' for the first definition, and 'كمال أول لما هو بالقوة' for the second. Note the qualifications that follow each.
The first definition includes the condition 'لا دفعة' (not all at once).
3
Understand the conditions
Examine the conditions for the second definition: 'قيد القوة' (the condition of potentiality) and 'قيد الحيثية' (the condition of 'insofar as'). What do they indicate about motion?
The author uses these conditions to clarify that motion requires a goal and that something remains potential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني (قيل الحركة (۱) الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على التدريج أو يسيراً يسيراً أو لا دفعة وبناه على أن تصور هذه المعاني التي حاصلها الاتصال الغير القار بديهي لا يتوقف على تصور الزمان الموقوف على تصور الحركة ليلزم الدور، وقيل كمال أول لما هو بالقوة، من حيث هو بالقوة، وأريد بالكمال حصول ما لم يكن واحترز بالأول عن الوصول، فإنه يحصل ثانياً، والتوجه أولاً، (۲) ونبه بقيد القوة على أنه لا بد لمتعلق الحركة من مطلوب يتوجه إليه، وأن يبقى شيء منه بالقوة، ولقيد الحيثية على أن كون الحركة كمالاً
The second investigation: It was said (about) motion (1): the exit from potentiality to actuality gradually, or little by little, or not all at once. And this is based on the idea that the conception of these meanings, the sum of which is non-continuous continuity, is intuitive, and does not depend on the conception of time which depends on the conception of motion, to avoid circularity. And it was said: the first perfection of what is in potentiality, in terms of it being in potentiality. And by perfection is meant the attainment of what was not; and by 'first' he excludes the arrival, for it occurs second, and the orientation occurs first (2). And by the condition of potentiality he indicates that the subject of motion must have an object toward which it tends, and that something of it remains in potentiality; and by the condition of 'in terms of' he indicates that motion is a perfection.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage presents two definitions of motion (al-haraka) from the philosophers. The first definition is 'al-khuruj min al-quwwa ila al-fi'l 'ala al-tadrij' (gradual exit from potentiality to actuality), with the qualification that it is not instantaneous ('la daf'a'). The author notes that this definition is based on the idea that the conception of these meanings—summarized as 'non-continuous continuity' (al-ittisal ghair al-qar)—is self-evident (badihi) and does not depend on the conception of time, which itself depends on motion, to avoid circularity. The second definition is 'kammal awwal lima huwa bi al-quwwa min haythu huwa bi al-quwwa' (first perfection of what is in potentiality insofar as it is in potentiality). Each part is explained: 'al-kamal' (perfection) means the attainment of something that was not present; 'al-awwal' (first) distinguishes it from 'al-wusul' (arrival), which occurs second, and from 'al-tawajjuh' (orientation) which occurs first. The author then clarifies the conditions: the condition of 'bi al-quwwa' (in potentiality) indicates that motion must have an object toward which it tends, and that something of the subject remains in potentiality; the condition of 'min haythu' (insofar as) indicates that motion is itself a perfection.

Key Terms

القوة
الفعل
الكمال الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think motion is simply change of location or a single type of change.
Correction: These definitions cover any change from potentiality to actuality, including qualitative change, not just movement in space.

Checkpoint
According to the second definition, motion is described as:
A) Gradual exit from potentiality to actuality
B) First perfection of what is in potentiality
C) Change of location
D) Instantaneous transition

Key Takeaway
The philosophers defined motion in two ways: as gradual actualization or as first perfection, both emphasizing transition from potentiality to actuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The second definition says motion is 'first perfection'—not the final arrival. Are you content with just the first step, or do you desire the final arrival? How does this apply to your relationship with Allah?

The passage emphasizes that motion requires a goal (matalub). What is the ultimate goal of your personal 'motion'? Are you moving toward Allah or away from Him?

First Definition of Motion and Its Circularity
Definition of motion and its circularity critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first definition of motion leads to a vicious circle and how this critique motivates the need for a better definition.

Core Idea
Defining motion using gradualness makes it dependent on time, which itself depends on motion — a logical circle.

This lesson examines the first definition of motion attributed to some philosophers: motion as 'the exit from potentiality to actuality gradually.' The definition appears intuitive, but a closer look reveals that its reliance on the concept of gradualness brings in time, and since time is defined in terms of motion, the definition becomes circular. The passage exposes this flaw and sets the stage for an alternative definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the key terms: motion, potentiality, actuality, gradual, non-fixed continuity.
The definition has three phrasings: 'gradually', 'little by little', 'not all at once'.
2
Understand the claim of intuitiveness
Notice that the proponent of this definition claims the meanings involved are self-evident and do not require prior understanding of time. Why might this be problematic?
3
Grasp the circularity argument
The passage concludes that this leads to circularity. Trace the circle: the definition uses gradualness which implies time, but time itself is defined through motion. Explain this in your own words.
The circle is vicious because each concept presupposes the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني (قيل الحركة (١) الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على التدريج أو يسيراً يسيراً أو لا دفعة وبناه على أن تصور هذه المعاني التي حاصلها الاتصال الغير القار بديهي لا يتوقف على تصور الزمان الموقوف على تصور الحركة ليلزم الدور،
The second investigation: it was said: motion is the exit from potentiality to actuality gradually or little by little or not all at once, and he built it upon [the idea] that the conception of these meanings, the gist of which is non-fixed continuity, is intuitive, not dependent on the conception of time which depends on the conception of motion, so that circularity results.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage introduces the first definition of motion attributed to some philosophers. Motion is defined as 'the exit from potentiality to actuality gradually' (or 'little by little' or 'not all at once'). The proponent of this definition constructs it on the claim that the concepts involved (gradualness, etc.) are self-evident and do not require prior conception of time. The author points out the problem: time itself is defined in terms of motion (e.g., time is the measure of motion), so if we define motion using time-like concepts, we end up with a circular definition. The phrase 'non-fixed continuity' (al-ittiṣāl al-ghayr al-qārr) describes motion as a continuous change that is not stable. The passage ends by stating that this leads to a vicious circle (al-dawr). It serves to reject this definition and motivate the need for a better one, which will be given in the next section.

Key Terms
الحركة
القوة
الفعل
التدريج
الاتصال الغير القار
الزمان
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The concept of 'gradualness' is simple and independent, so the definition of motion as gradual exit is unproblematic.
Correction: Gradualness inherently involves a temporal dimension, and time is itself defined in terms of motion, leading to circularity.

Checkpoint
Why does the first definition of motion lead to circularity?
A) Because motion is defined using time, and time is defined using motion.
B) Because the definition uses the term 'potentiality' which is unclear.
C) Because motion cannot be defined at all.
D) Because the definition assumes that motion is gradual, but motion could be instantaneous.

Key Takeaway
The first definition of motion fails because it implicitly relies on time, which itself is defined by motion, creating a vicious circle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an intuitive belief you hold deeply—perhaps about time, change, or even your own existence. Without looking it up, could that belief contain hidden assumptions that lead to circular reasoning? How would you feel if you discovered that it does?
?
The proponents claimed that the concept of gradualness is self-evident and needs no further definition. Are there any aspects of your faith or worldview that you consider 'self-evident' but have never critically examined? What might be the implicit dependencies in those beliefs, and are you willing to investigate them?

Second Definition of Motion: First Perfection of Potentiality
Presents and explains the second definition of motion as 'first perfection of what is in potentiality'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second definition of motion as 'first perfection of what is in potentiality' and how it resolves the circularity in earlier definitions.

Core Idea
Motion is the first perfection of potentiality, avoiding circular definitions.

The second definition of motion addresses defects in earlier definitions that relied on time, which is itself defined by motion. This passage presents a precise definition that focuses on the essence of motion as a first perfection relative to potentiality. By carefully unpacking each term, the author shows how motion is a leading towards a goal without invoking circular reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the second definition of motion
Begin by reading the Arabic definition: 'Al-ḥaraka kamāl awwal li-mā huwa bi-l-quwwa min ḥaythu huwa bi-l-quwwa' and its translation. Notice each key term.
2
Understand 'perfection' and 'first'
The author explains 'perfection' as attainment of what was not, and 'first' to exclude arrival (wuṣūl) which is a second perfection. Grasp why direction (tawajjuh) precedes arrival.
3
Analyze the conditions of potentiality and aspect
The condition 'in potentiality' ensures a sought goal and remaining potentiality; 'insofar as' restricts perfection to the potential arrival. Consider how this excludes other perfections like squareness.
The goal is to depict the essence of motion, not to distinguish it from other things.
4
See how the definition avoids circularity
Read the critique of the first definition (gradual exit from potentiality), which depends on time, causing circularity. Then confirm how the second definition avoids time by focusing on perfection and potentiality.
This resolution is a key advantage of the second definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل كمال أول لما هو بالقوة، من حيث هو بالقوة، وأريد بالكمال حصول ما لم يكن واحترز بالأول عن الوصول، فإنه يحصل ثانياً، والتوجه أولاً، (٢) ونبه بقيد القوة على أنه لا بد لمتعلق الحركة من مطلوب يتوجه إليه، وأن يبقى شيء منه بالقوة، ولقيد الحيثية على أن كون الحركة كمالاً للمتحرك، إنما هو في الوصول الذي له بالقوة فتخرج كمالاته التي ليست كذلك، كالمربعية مثلا، والمقصود تلخيص المعنى المسمى بالحركة على الإطلاق، وتحقيقه لا تمييزه (١) وتصويره عند العقل، فلا يضيره كون المعرف أخفى، وكون الكمالين. أعني التوجه والوصول في الحركة المستديرة بمجرد الفرض والاعتبار نظراً إلى أن حال الجسم بالنسبة إلى كل نقطة من حيث طلبها توجه ومن حيث الحصول عندها وصول). (٢) والمبحث الأول منه غني عن الشرح وأما الثاني فبيانه أن بعض الفلاسفة فسر الحركة بالخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على التدريج أو يسيراً يسيراً أو لا دفعة وبنى ذلك (٢) على أن معنى هذه الألفاظ واضح عند العقل من غير احتياج إلى تصور الزمان المفتقر إلى تصور الحركة، ونظر بعضهم إلى أن معنى التدريج أن لا يكون دفعة ، ومعنى الحصول دفعة أن يكون في آن وهو طرف الزمان، وهو مقدار الحركة فيكون التعريف دورياً. ففسرها بأنها كمال أول لما هو بالقوة من حيث هو بالقوة والمراد بالكمال ههنا حصول ما لم يكن حاصلاً، ولا خفاء في أن الحركة أمر ممكن الحصول للجسم، فيكون حصولها كمالاً، واحترز بقيد الأولية عن الوصول فإن الجسم إذا كان في مكان وهو ممكن الحصول في مكان آخر كان له إمكانان إمكان الحصول في ذلك المكان، وإمكان التوجه إليه وهما كمالان فالتوجه مقدم على الأصول فهو كمال أول والوصول كمال ثان ثم إن الحركة تفارق سائر الكمالات من حيث إنها لا حقيقة لها إلا التأدي إلى الغير والسلوك إليه، فلا بد من مطلوب ممكن الحصول، ليكون التوجه توجهاً إليه، ومن أن يبقى من ذلك التوجه ما دام موجوداً شيء بالقوة، إذ لا توجه بعد الوصول، فحقيقة الحركة متعلقة بأن يبقى منها شيء بالقوة وبأن لا يكون المتأدى إليه حاصلا بالفعل، فتكون الحركة بالفعل كمالاً للجسم المتحرك الذي هو بالقوة من جهة التأدى إلى المقصود الذي هو الحصول في المكان المطلوب فيكون كمالاً أول لما بالقوة،
And it was said: [motion is] the first perfection of that which is in potentiality, insofar as it is in potentiality. And by 'perfection' is meant the attainment of what was not. And he restricted by 'first' [to exclude] arrival (al-wuṣūl), for it occurs second, while direction (al-tawajjuh) occurs first. And he alerted by the condition of potentiality that there must be for the object of motion a sought goal toward which it is directed, and that something of it remains in potentiality. And by the condition of the aspect [i.e., 'insofar as'] [he indicates] that motion being a perfection for the moving thing is only in the arrival that it has in potentiality; thus it excludes perfections that are not like that, such as squareness, for example. And the purpose is to summarize the meaning called motion absolutely, and to realize it, not to differentiate it (or: not to distinguish it with something else), and to depict it in the mind. So it does not harm it that the definer is more obscure. And the two perfections, namely direction and arrival, in circular motion are merely by supposition and consideration, looking at the fact that the state of the body with respect to each point—in terms of seeking it is direction, and in terms of attainment at it is arrival. And the first investigation of it is in no need of explanation. As for the second, its clarification is that some philosophers interpreted motion as the exit from potentiality to actuality gradually or little by little or not all at once, and he built that upon [the claim] that the meaning of these expressions is clear to the mind without needing the conception of time which depends on the conception of motion. And some of them considered that the meaning of 'gradual' is that it is not all at once, and the meaning of 'occurring all at once' is that it occurs in an instant (ān), which is a limit of time, and time is the measure of motion; thus the definition becomes circular. So he interpreted it as the first perfection of what is in potentiality insofar as it is in potentiality. And what is meant by 'perfection' here is the attainment of what was not attained. There is no concealment that motion is a possible attainment for the body, so its attainment is a perfection. And he restricted by the condition of firstness to exclude arrival, for if the body is in a place and it is possible to be in another place, it has two possibilities: possibility of attainment in that place, and possibility of direction toward it; these are two perfections. Direction precedes attainment, so it is the first perfection, and arrival is the second perfection. Then motion differs from other perfections in that it has no reality except leading to the other and journeying to it. So there must be a possible attained goal so that direction is direction toward it, and that something of that direction remains in potentiality as long as it exists, for there is no direction after arrival. Thus the reality of motion is connected to the fact that something of it remains in potentiality and that the goal toward which it leads is not actually attained. So motion in actuality is a perfection of the moving body which is in potentiality, from the aspect of leading to the goal which is attainment in the desired place; thus it is the first perfection of that which is in potentiality.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the second definition of motion, which is preferred over the first. It begins by stating the definition: motion is 'the first perfection of that which is in potentiality, insofar as it is in potentiality'. The author then unpacks each component. 'Perfection' (kamāl) means the acquisition of something previously absent; 'first' (awwal) distinguishes it from arrival (wuṣūl), which is a second perfection that occurs after motion is complete. The condition 'insofar as it is in potentiality' (min ḥaythu huwa bi-l-quwwa) ensures that the motion is directed toward a goal that remains partly potential. The author notes that this definition focuses on the essence of motion, not on distinguishing it from other things, and that it is acceptable even if the definiens is 'more obscure' than the definiendum, because the goal is to convey the meaning to the mind, not to provide a logical classification. The passage then recapitulates the problem with the first definition (gradual exit from potentiality) to contrast with this definition. It explains that 'gradual' implies time, and time is the measure of motion, so the first definition is circular. The second definition avoids this by not referencing time. The author then elaborates on the two perfections (direction and arrival) and how motion relates to them. In circular motion, these are only conceptually distinct. Finally, the passage clarifies that motion is essentially a 'leading toward another' (ta'diya ilā al-ghayr), which requires that the goal is potentially attainable and that something remains potential during motion. The last part concludes that motion in actuality is the first perfection of the moving body that is in potentiality with respect to reaching the desired location.

القوة

التأدي إلى الغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'first perfection' means the motion itself is a perfection in an absolute sense, independent of potentiality.
Correction: The passage clarifies that motion is a perfection relative to the potentiality of the moving body and specifically excludes other perfections like squareness. It is a perfection only insofar as it leads to a goal that is in potentiality.

Checkpoint
Why does the second definition of motion avoid circularity?
A) Because it does not use the concept of time.
B) Because it defines motion as 'gradual' which is simpler.
C) Because it uses 'perfection' which is a clear term.
D) Because it focuses on circular motion only.

Key Takeaway
Motion is essentially the first perfection of potentiality, a leading toward a goal that remains partially unattained, and this definition avoids circularity by not depending on time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are you more concerned with 'arrival' (achieving goals) or with the 'direction' (the process of moving)? The passage says motion is essentially the first perfection, the direction. How does your focus affect your contentment and perseverance? What would change if you prioritized direction over outcome today?
?
The passage warns against circular definitions that depend on time. Consider a habit or spiritual practice you're trying to change. Are you defining your progress in terms of time (e.g., 'I need 30 days') rather than the intrinsic movement toward the goal? How can you shift to seeing your motion as the first perfection of your potential—a process that doesn't require a fixed timeline?
?
The author notes that motion 'has no reality except leading to the other and journeying to it.' In your relationship with Allah, are you treating acts of worship as static 'perfections' (ticking a box) or as movements toward Him? Reflect on one act you do mechanically: how can you restore the sense of motion—the direction toward your Lord—rather than just arriving at the act?

Positional Motion Defined and the Part-Whole Objection
Defines positional motion and defends against the parts objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of positional motion (al-haraka al-wad‘iyya) and how it differs from spatial motion, as well as the author's response to the objection that the whole sphere must move spatially if its parts do.

Core Idea
Positional motion is a change of relation without change of place.

This lesson introduces positional motion, a key concept in Aristotelian physics that explains how a rotating sphere moves without leaving its location. The author defines it and immediately defends it against a logical objection based on the parts of the whole.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of positional motion
Start by reading the definition: positional motion is like a sphere rotating on itself, changing positions without leaving its place.
Notice the contrast with spatial motion (al-ayniyya).
2
Identify the first objection
The objector claims that every part of the sphere undergoes spatial motion, so the whole must as well.
3
Study the author's reply
The author replies that even if there are actual parts, the judgment on each part does not necessarily apply to the whole.
This is a logical principle.
4
Understand the detailed explanation of positional motion
Read the section that explains positional motion as a category of motion involving gradual change in the relation of parts to external things, without changing place.
It mentions al-Farabi and Ibn Sina.
5
Reflect on the necessity of positional motion
The author asserts that positional motion of the whole is necessary (daruri). Consider why it might be considered self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأربع الأول الأين وهو ظاهر الثانية الوضع كما في الحركة الكرة على نفسها بأن تتبدل أوضاعها من غير أن يخرج عن مكانها .
فإن قيل : لكل جزء حركة أينية ضرورة تبدل أمكنتها، فكذا للكل .
قلنا : لو سلم أن هناك جزءاً بالفعل فقد لا يكون للكل حكم كل جزء.
فإن قيل : فعلى هذا لا نسلم حركة الكل وتبدل وضعه وإنما ذلك للأجزاء.
قلنا هو ضروري.
أي ما تنسب إليه الحركة من المقولات العشر أعني الجنس العالي الذي يتغير الموضوع بالتدريج من نوع منه إلى نوع آخر أو من صنف من نوع منه إلى صنف آخر، واقتصر الإمام على المتغير من صنف من المقولة إلى صنف آخر أي سواء كانا من نوعين أو من نوع والحركة الوضعية مما صرح به الفارابي (١) ، وإن كان في كلام ابن سينا ما يوهم أنه تفرد بالاطلاع عليها وبالجملة فالذي يحققها هـو أن للفلك حركة لا يخرج بها عن مكانه، وإنما يتبدل بالتدريج نسبة أجزائه إلى أمور خارجة عنه، إما محوية فقط كما في الفلك الأعظم وإما حاوية ومحوية كما في غيره، فتتبدل الهيئة تلك النسبة وهو الوضع، ولا نعني بالحركة في الوضع إلا التغيير من وضع إلى وضع على التدريج من غير تبدل المكان.
فإن قيل : كل جزء قد خرج مكانه فكذا الكل لأنه ليس إلا مجموع الأجزاء.
قلنا : لو سلم أن (۲) هناك أجزاء بالفعل فثبوت الحكم لكل حكم (٣) لا يستلزم ثبوته بمجموع الأجزاء كما مر غیره مرة . على أن ما ذكر لا يتم في الفلك الأعظم عند من لا يثبت له المكان بناء على أن المكان هو السطح الباطن من الحاوي واللاحاوي له .
فإن قيل : الثابت بالدليل من حركات الأفلاك بالمشاهدة من حركة الكرة على نفسها ليس إلا تبدل نسبة الأجزاء المفروضة، وإذا لم يكن ثبوت الحكم لكل جزء مستلزماً ثبوته للكل فلا نسلم أن للفلك أو الكرة حركة وتبدل من وضع .
قلنا : هو ضروري فإنه لا معنى لوضع الكل إلا هيئة نسبة أجزائه بعضها إلى البعض وإلى الأمور الخارجية، ولا معنى لحركته في الوضع إلا تبدل ذلك على التدريج هذا . لكن يؤول على أن الحركة الأينية للأجزاء الفرضية حركة وضعية بالإضافة إلى الكل.
The first of the four [categories] is 'where' (al-ayn), which is obvious; the second is position (al-wad'), like the movement of a sphere upon itself, in that its positions change without its leaving its place. If it is said: Every part has a motion in 'where' (al-ayniyya) necessarily due to the change of their places, so too for the whole. We say: If it is granted that there is a part in actuality, the whole may not have the same judgment as each part. If it is said: Then on this basis we do not concede that the whole moves and changes its position; rather that is for the parts. We say: It is necessary (daruri). That is, that to which motion is attributed among the ten categories, i.e., the highest genus under which the subject changes gradually from one species of it to another species, or from one variety of a species to another variety of the same species. The Imam restricted it to change from one variety of a category to another variety, i.e., whether they are from two species or from one species. Positional motion (al-haraka al-wad'iyya) is something that al-Farabi affirmed, although in Ibn Sina's words there is something that suggests he alone discovered it. In summary, what verifies it is that the celestial sphere has a motion by which it does not leave its place; rather, the relation of its parts to external things changes gradually, either only as containing (muhawiya) as in the outermost sphere, or both containing and contained as in others. Thus the configuration of that relation changes, and that is position (al-wad'). By motion in position we mean nothing other than change from one position to another gradually without change of place. If it is said: Every part has left its place, so too the whole, because it is nothing but the sum of the parts. We say: If it is granted that there are parts in actuality, the affirmation of the judgment for each part does not entail its affirmation for the sum of the parts, as has been stated many times. Moreover, what was mentioned does not apply to the outermost sphere for those who do not affirm a place for it, on the basis that place is the inner surface of the container and that which contains it. If it is said: What is established by evidence from the observed motions of the celestial spheres, i.e., the movement of a sphere upon itself, is only the change of the relation of the hypothetical parts; and if the affirmation of the judgment for each part does not entail its affirmation for the whole, then we do not concede that the sphere or the ball has a motion and change of position. We say: It is necessary (daruri), for there is no meaning to the position of the whole except the configuration of the relation of its parts to each other and to external things, and there is no meaning to its motion in position except the gradual change of that. However, it can be interpreted that the motion in 'where' of the hypothetical parts is a positional motion relative to the whole.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage defines positional motion (al-haraka al-wad'iyya) and defends it against an objection based on the parts of the whole. It is a definition followed by an objection-and-answer format. The author begins by listing the four categories of motion, noting that the first (al-ayn, spatial motion) is obvious, and the second is positional motion. Positional motion is exemplified by a sphere rotating upon itself: its positions change without leaving its place. Then an objector argues: every part of the sphere has spatial motion because its place changes, so the whole sphere must also have spatial motion. The author replies: even if we grant that there are actual parts, the judgment on each part does not necessarily apply to the whole. The objector then retorts: if so, we deny that the whole moves positionally; only the parts do. The author asserts that positional motion of the whole is something necessarily known (daruri). He then explains the nature of positional motion: it belongs to the category of position (al-wad'), one of the ten categories. The subject (the sphere) changes gradually regarding the relation of its parts to external things, without leaving its place. He notes that al-Farabi explicitly affirmed this kind of motion, while Ibn Sina's words might suggest he claimed exclusive discovery. The author further clarifies that for the outermost sphere, which has no container (hawi) as place, the argument about parts leaving their places does not apply. Finally, he concludes that the observed motion of the sphere is indeed a change of the configuration of its parts' relations, and this is exactly what is meant by positional motion of the whole. The author's final remark allows that the spatial motion of hypothetical parts can be considered positional relative to the whole.

الأين
الوضع
الفلك الأعظم
الحاوي
المحوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The whole sphere must have the same kind of motion (spatial) as its parts because it is the sum of the parts.
Correction: The whole can have a different kind of motion (positional) even if the parts have spatial motion, as the judgment on parts does not entail the same judgment on the whole.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what distinguishes positional motion (al-haraka al-wad'iyya) from spatial motion (al-ayniyya)?
A) Positional motion involves a change of place, while spatial motion does not.
B) Positional motion is a change of the relation of parts without change of place, while spatial motion involves change of place.
C) Positional motion applies only to the outermost sphere, while spatial motion applies to sublunary bodies.
D) Both are the same, but positional motion is a subtype of spatial motion.

Key Takeaway
Positional motion is a change in the relation of parts without changing location, and its existence is necessary based on observation of the rotating sphere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been like the objector, assuming that because your outer situation hasn't changed, you haven't moved? When has your heart's orientation toward Allah shifted gradually while your physical circumstances remained the same? How will you now pay attention to that inner motion?
?
If you believe that true change requires leaving your place, how might you be missing the quiet, gradual positional changes Allah is working in your life right now? What would it take for you to recognize them and respond faithfully without waiting for a dramatic relocation?

The Outermost Sphere and the Necessity of Positional Motion
Defends positional motion against objections concerning the outermost sphere and the nature of whole-part motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deeper objection concerning the outermost sphere's lack of place and the author's final defense that positional motion of the whole is a necessary (daruri) reality.

Core Idea
The whole's positional motion is necessary and irreducible to its parts' spatial motions.

The author faces a more refined objection: even if we accept that parts have spatial motion, how does that constitute positional motion for the whole? He further argues that for the outermost sphere, the concept of place does not even apply, yet positional motion is observed. This lesson consolidates the defense and clarifies the meaning of positional motion of the whole.

Guided Path
1
Read the new objection about the sum of parts
The objector argues that if all parts leave their places, then the whole, being the sum of the parts, must also leave its place.
2
Study the author's reply regarding judgment on parts vs. whole
The author repeats the logical principle: the judgment on each part does not entail the same judgment for the sum of the parts.
He notes that this has been mentioned before.
3
Consider the special case of the outermost sphere
The author points out that for those who deny a place for the outermost sphere (since place is the inner surface of the container), the earlier objection does not even apply.
4
Examine the final objection from observation
The objector claims that observed motion is only a change in relation of hypothetical parts, not a real motion of the whole.
5
Grasp the concluding argument
The author asserts that positional motion of the whole is necessary (daruri) because the position of the whole is nothing but the configuration of its parts' relations, and change in that is exactly what is observed.
He adds that the spatial motion of parts can be seen as positional relative to the whole.

Source Text

According to the author, why does the objection about parts leaving their place not apply to the outermost sphere?
A) Because the outermost sphere has no parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author declares that positional motion is necessary (daruri) and known by immediate perception. When you look at the night sky, do you sense a unified rotation of the whole, or do you reduce it to individual star movements? Which perspective aligns with the author's argument, and why might resisting the unified view be a failure of spiritual insight?
?
The refutation here hinges on the idea that a whole can have a motion that its parts do not share. In your own life, are there 'wholes'—community, family, your own soul—whose movements you ignore because you focus only on their parts? Will you commit today to perceiving the larger motion and responding to it as a whole?
?
Consider the outermost sphere: it has no place in the Aristotelian sense because there is no container. What does it mean for you to have a relationship with something utterly transcendent—like the divine—that cannot be contained or reduced to its parts? Are you content with a fragmented view, or will you strive to perceive His unified actions in your life?

The Four Types of Motion in Quantity
Sanusi enumerates the four types of motion in quantity with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of motion in quantity: growth, wilting, rarefaction, and condensation, and how they are classified based on increase/decrease and addition/separation of matter.

Core Idea
Motion in quantity is either growth, wilting, rarefaction, or condensation.

In this passage, Sanusi classifies motion in the category of quantity. He distinguishes between changes that involve material addition or removal and those that do not, using everyday examples like the air in a flask. Understanding these four types is fundamental to kalam physics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pairs
Notice that motion in quantity is divided into two pairs: growth/wilting and rarefaction/condensation. Each pair represents opposite directions of change.
Growth and wilting involve matter change; rarefaction and condensation do not.
2
Understand the role of matter
Growth occurs when new matter arrives; wilting when matter leaves. Rarefaction and condensation change magnitude without matter exchange.
The text gives examples: the air in a flask being sucked or blown.
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider how the flask examples illustrate rarefaction (air expands when sucked) and condensation (air compresses when blown). These show magnitude change without adding or removing air.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قال : الثالثة الكم(٢) : والانتقال فيه إما من النقصان إلى الزيادة لورود مادته وهو النمو أو بدونه التكاثف وهو التخلخل، وإما بالعكس بانفصال مادته وهو الذبول أو بدونه وهو التكاثف . الحركة في الكم تقع باعتبارين أحدهما النمو والذبول، وثانيهما التخلخل والتكاثف ويقال في بيان ذلك أن الانتقال في الكم (٣) إما أن يكون من النقصان إلى الزيادة أو من الزيادة إلى النقصان والأول إما أن يكون بورود مادته تزيد في كمية الجسم وهو النمو أو بدونه وهو التخلخل كما في هواء باطن القارورة عند مصها، والثاني إما أن يكون بنقصان جزء وهو الذبول كما في المدقوق أو بدونه وهو في هواء باطن القارورة عند النفخ فيها، ويتمسكون في إمكان التخلخل والتكاثف بأن الجسم مركب من الهيولي والصورة . والهيولي لا مقدار لها في نفسها قابلة للمقادير المختلفة بحسب ما سبق (٤) من الأسباب المعدة فيجوز أن وإنما هي ينتقل من المقدار الصغير إلى الكبير وهو التخلخل وبالعكس وهو التكاثف . وإنما بنوا ذلك على الهيولي لأنها عندهم محض تحايل يتوارد عليه الصور والمقادير المختلفة من غير غير أن يقتضي معيناً من ذلك، بخلاف ماذا جعل الجسم بسيطاً واحداً متصلاً كما
(He said: The third is quantity (al-kamm). Transition in it is either from deficiency to increase by the arrival of its matter, which is growth (al-numuw), or without it, [which is] condensation? (al-takathuf) which is rarefaction? (al-takhalul); or the opposite by the separation of its matter, which is wilting (al-dhubul), or without it, which is condensation (al-takathuf). Motion in quantity occurs according to two aspects: one is growth and wilting, and the second is rarefaction and condensation. In explaining this, it is said: Transition in quantity is either from deficiency to increase or from increase to deficiency. The first is either by the arrival of its matter that increases the quantity of the body, which is growth (al-numuw), or without it, which is rarefaction (al-takhalul), as in the air inside a flask when it is sucked. The second is either by a decrease of a part, which is wilting (al-dhubul), as in a crushed [thing], or without it, which is in the air inside a flask when blowing into it. They adhere to the possibility of rarefaction and condensation because the body is composed of matter (hayula) and form (sura). Matter has no magnitude in itself, and is receptive to different magnitudes according to preparatory causes; thus it is possible that it transitions from a small magnitude to a large one, which is rarefaction, and vice versa, which is condensation. They built this upon matter (hayula) because for them it is pure potentiality (tahayyul) upon which different forms and magnitudes supervene without requiring a specific one, unlike if the body were made a simple continuous unity, as...
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage classifies motion in quantity (al-haraka fi al-kamm) into four types: growth (numuw), wilting (dhubul), rarefaction (takhalul), and condensation (takathuf). It is a classification with explanation. The author begins by citing the Imam (likely al-Razi or al-Taftazani) who states that transition in the category of quantity is either from deficiency to increase (growth or rarefaction) or from increase to deficiency (wilting or condensation). Growth occurs when new matter arrives and increases the body's quantity; rarefaction occurs without new matter, as in air expanding when a flask is sucked. Wilting occurs when a part separates; condensation occurs without separation, as in air compressed by blowing. The author then explains the philosophical basis for rarefaction and condensation: they argue from the composition of body from matter (hayula) and form. Matter is pure potentiality with no inherent magnitude, so it can receive different magnitudes depending on causes. This allows a body to transition from small to large (rarefaction) or large to small (condensation) without adding or removing matter. This is contrasted with the view that body is a simple continuous unity, which would not permit such change. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is complete enough: it defines and defends the possibility of motion in quantity through these four modes.

The Philosophical Basis for Rarefaction and Condensation
Sanusi provides the philosophical argument for rarefaction and condensation based on matter and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the composition of body from matter (hayula) and form (sura) allows for changes in quantity without addition or removal of matter, explaining rarefaction and condensation.

Core Idea
Matter is pure potentiality, receptive to different magnitudes, enabling rarefaction and condensation.

After classifying the four types of motion in quantity, Sanusi turns to the underlying philosophical justification for rarefaction and condensation. He explains that the body's composition from matter and form allows it to change magnitude without adding or subtracting matter. This section is crucial for understanding why these two types are possible within the kalam framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
The passage states that rarefaction and condensation are possible because the body is composed of matter (hayula) and form (sura). Matter itself has no fixed magnitude but can receive different magnitudes.
This is the key philosophical premise.
2
Understand matter as potentiality
For the philosophers, matter is pure potentiality (tahayyul). It does not necessitate a specific magnitude but can take on various magnitudes depending on causes.
This justifies how a body can expand or contract without material change.
3
Contrast with the alternative view
The passage contrasts this with the idea of body as a simple continuous unity, which would not allow such changes. The argument depends on the composition view.

Classification of Motion: Essential, Accidental, Forced, Voluntary, Natural
Taftazani classifies motion into essential and accidental, and then into forced, voluntary, and natural.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of motion into essential versus accidental and into forced, voluntary, and natural, and how accidental motion depends on the other three types.

Core Idea
Motion is classified by its source: essential/accidental, then by the mover: forced, voluntary, or natural.

This passage from Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid introduces a foundational classification of motion used in Islamic theology and philosophy. Before exploring deeper arguments about creation and change, one must understand how motion is categorized based on its relationship to the moving object and the nature of its mover. Let us unpack these definitions and see how they relate to each other.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish essential vs. accidental motion
Read the first part of the passage focusing on al-mutaḥarrik (the moving thing). Note the example of the ship (essential) and its rider (accidental). Ask yourself why the rider's motion is accidental.
Essential motion belongs to the object itself; accidental motion is borrowed from another.
2
Identify the three types of essential motion
Move to the classification of al-muḥarrik (the mover). The passage gives three types: forced, voluntary, and natural. See how each depends on whether the mover is external, or internal with/without intention.
3
Understand the fourfold division of absolute motion
The commentary explains that absolute motion divides into four: accidental, forced, voluntary, and natural. But note that accidental motion is not a separate fundamental type; it is always a consequence of one of the other three.
This resolves a potential misconception.
4
Clarify the meaning of 'mover in the moving thing'
The passage expands 'mover in the moving thing' to include not only parts but also special connections like the soul to the body. This allows natural and voluntary motions to be properly categorized, as with the stone falling, human moving, and sphere rotating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما المتحرك : فإن كانت الحركة فيه بالحقيقة فمتحرك بالذات كحركة السفينة وإلا فبالعرض كحركة راكبها . قال : وأما المحرك : فإن كان خارجاً عن ذات المتحرك، فالحركة قسرية، وإلا فإن كان قصد وشعور فإرادية، وإلا فطبيعية . يريد انقسام الحركة بالذات إلى الأقسام الثلاثة، وأما مطلق الحركة فينقسم إلى أربعة عرضية وقسرية وإرادية وطبيعية وإن كانت العرضية لا تخلو أو عن الأقسام الثلاثة، ولهذا قيل الحركة إن كانت تبعاً لحركة جسم آخر فعرضية ، وإلا فإن كان محركها موجوداً في غير الجسم المتحرك فقسرية، وإن كان موجوداً فيه نفسه، فإن كان من شأنه الشعور والقصد فإرادية، وإلا فطبيعية، والمراد بكون المحرك في المتحرك أعم من أن يكون جزءاً منه أو به التعلق المخصوص كتعلق النفوس الإنسانية بأبدانها، والنفوس الفلكية بأفلاكها، فيعم تحرك الحجر هبوطاً والإنسان يمنة ويسرة، والفلك استدارة .
He said: As for the moving thing (al-mutaḥarrik): if motion is in it in reality, then it is essentially moving (mutaḥarrik bi-dhāt), like the movement of a ship; otherwise, it is accidentally moving (bi-l-ʿaraḍ), like the movement of its rider. He said: As for the mover (al-muḥarrik): if it is external to the essence of the moving thing, then the motion is forced (qasriyya); otherwise, if there is intention and consciousness, then it is voluntary (irādiyya); otherwise, it is natural (ṭabīʿiyya). He intends the division of motion essentially into three divisions. As for absolute motion, it divides into four: accidental, forced, voluntary, and natural, although the accidental is not free from the three divisions. For this reason it is said: Motion – if it is a consequence of the motion of another body, then it is accidental; otherwise, if its mover exists in something other than the moving body, then it is forced; and if it exists in itself, then if it is of the nature of consciousness and intention, then it is voluntary; otherwise, it is natural. And the meaning of the mover being in the moving thing is more general than it being a part of it or having that special connection, like the connection of human souls to their bodies and celestial souls to their spheres. So it includes the movement of a stone downward, a human right and left, and the sphere rotating.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a classification of motion (al-ḥaraka). The author first quotes a prior text (likely the matn) distinguishing between the moving thing (al-mutaḥarrik) as essentially or accidentally moving, illustrated by the ship and its rider. Then the mover (al-muḥarrik) is classified: external to the moving thing yields forced motion (qasriyya); internal with intention yields voluntary (irādiyya); otherwise natural (ṭabīʿiyya). The commentator then explains that motion in itself (al-ḥaraka bi-dhāt) has three divisions (essential, forced, voluntary, natural are considered for absolute motion, but the essential is already covered by the first division). However, absolute motion is divided into four: accidental, forced, voluntary, and natural. Accidental motion is not a separate fundamental type but is derived from one of the other three. The classification is then restated with more precision: if motion is a consequence of another body's motion, it is accidental; if the mover is external, forced; if internal with consciousness and intention, voluntary; otherwise natural. Finally, the phrase 'mover in the moving thing' is clarified to include not only parts of the body but also special connections like the soul to the body or celestial souls to their spheres, exemplifying with stone falling, human voluntary movement, and sphere rotation.

المتحرك بالذات
المتحرك بالعرض
الحركة القسرية
الحركة الإرادية
الحركة الطبيعية
الحركة العرضية
المحرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidental motion is a separate fundamental type on par with forced, voluntary, and natural.
Correction: Accidental motion is always a consequence of one of the other three types; it is not an independent category of motion in itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, motion that occurs when the mover is external to the moving body is called:
A) Accidental motion (ʿaraḍiyya)
B) Forced motion (qasriyya)
C) Voluntary motion (irādiyya)
D) Natural motion (ṭabīʿiyya)

Key Takeaway
Motion is classified by whether it is essential or accidental, and if essential, by whether the mover is external (forced), internal with intention (voluntary), or internal without intention (natural).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you performed today. Was it voluntary (with intention), forced (by external pressure), or natural (without conscious thought)? In light of this lesson, how can you ensure that more of your daily actions are voluntary and consciously directed towards pleasing Allah?
?
The passage mentions that accidental motion is a consequence of another body's motion. Are there areas in your spiritual life where you are merely being moved by others—following routines without personal conviction? What would it take to become an 'essential mover' in your own faith journey?

Motion Type: Determined by Start, End, and Medium Only
Sanusi explains that motion type depends only on its start, end, and medium.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the type of a motion is determined exclusively by its starting point, ending point, and medium, not by the mover, moved object, or time.

Core Idea
Motion type depends only on where it starts, where it ends, and its path.

Sanusi continues his discussion of motion by examining what determines its specific type. He argues that only three factors matter: the starting point, the ending point, and the medium or path. Differences in the mover, the thing moved, or the time do not change the essential nature of the motion. This principle is then applied to examples and used to critique alternative positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the core thesis
Read the first few lines of the Arabic passage (up to 'فبنوا على ذلك...') and its translation. Notice the main claim: motion type is determined by unity of start, end, and medium.
The word 'ما فيه الحركة' means the medium or path of motion.
2
Understand the general principle
The author supports the claim with a general principle: diversity of subjects or causes does not necessitate diversity of accidents. Think of the example of heat existing in different things from different causes.
This principle is key to understanding why different movers do not change motion type.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the example of upward motion: fire (natural), stone (forced), bird (voluntary). Why are they the same type? Because start, end, and medium are identical.
This shows that the cause of motion is irrelevant to its type.
4
Consider the time objection
Read the part about time and the critique of al-Mawaqif. The author argues that time cannot change motion type because time itself does not differ in essence, and even if it did, it could still attach to one type.
The author also refutes the idea that difference in time implies difference in motion type.
5
Summarize the types of difference
Finally, note when motion type does differ: when start and end differ, or when medium differs. Contrast the examples of straight vs. curved motion and different intermediate qualities.
This confirms that only start, end, and medium matter.

فبنوا على ذلك، أنه إذا اتحد المبدأ والمنتهى وما فيه الحركة لتحدث الحركة بالنوع ، وإن اختلف المتحرك أو المحرك أو الزمان، لأن تنوع المعروضات أو الأسباب لا يوجب تنوع العوارض والمسببات لجواز قيام نوع منها كالحرارة بموضوعين مختلفي الماهية كالإنسان والفرس وحصوله المؤثرين مختلفين كالنار والشمس، وبهذا يظهر أن لا أثر للاختلاف بالقسر والطبع والإرادة. فالحركة الصاعدة للنار طبعاً، وللحجر قسراً وللطير إرادة، لا تختلف نوعاً . وأما الأزمنة فلا يتصور فيها اختلاف الماهية ولو فرض فلا خفاء في جواز إحاطتها بحقيقة واحدة والتمسك بأنها عارضة للحركة واختلاف العارض لا اختلاف المعروض ضعيف لما سبق من أن هذا التعلق بالزمان غير تعلق الحركة التي جعل الزمان عارضاً لها، فإنها إنما هي حركة الفلك الأعظم، وإذا اختلفت المبدأ والمنتهى اختلفت الحركة، وإن كان ما فيه واحداً أما في الأين فكالحركة الصاعدة مع الهابطة وأما في الكيف فكالحركة من البياض إلى السواد على طريق التصفر ثم التحمر ثم التسود مع الحركة من السواد إلى البياض على طريق التحمر ثم التصفر ثم التبيض . وكذا إذا اختلف ما فيه وإن اتحد المبدأ والمنتهى كالحركة من نقطة إلى نقطة على الاستقامة معها على الانحناء وكالحركة من البياض إلى السواد على طريق الأخذ في الصفرة ثم الحمرة ثم السواد معها على طريق الأخذ في الخضرة ثم النيلية ثم السواد وما ذكر في المواقف من أنه لا بد من وحدة ما فيه وما منه وما إليه، إذ لو اختلف ما فيه اختلف النوع كالتسخن والتسود ليس على ما ينبغي لأن هذا إنما يصح للتمثيل دون التعليل، وكأنه أراد أنه يختلف النوع عند اختلاف مجرد ما فيه كما يختلف عند اختلاف الأمور الثلاثة مثل التسخن والتسود أو كان الأصل كالتسخن والتبرد فصحف إلى التسود
They built upon that: if the starting point, the ending point, and the medium of motion are unified, then the motion occurs as a single type, even if the moved object, the mover, or the time differ, because the diversity of subjects or causes does not necessitate the diversity of accidents and effects, since it is possible for one type of them, like heat, to subsist in two subjects of different essences, like man and horse, and to derive from two different causes, like fire and the sun. And by this it becomes clear that difference by constraint, nature, and will has no effect. Thus the upward motion of fire naturally, of a stone by constraint, and of a bird by will do not differ in type. As for times, no difference in essence is conceivable in them, and even if it were assumed, there is no doubt that it is possible for them to be encompassed by a single reality. And the insistence that time is an accident of motion and that the difference of the accident does not imply difference of the subject is weak, because, as previously mentioned, this attachment to time is different from the attachment of motion to time which was made an accident of it; for it is a motion of the highest sphere. And if the starting and ending points differ, the motion differs, even if the medium is one. As for the category of location (al-ayn), it is like upward motion with downward motion. As for the category of quality (al-kayf), it is like motion from whiteness to blackness via yellowness then redness then blackness, compared to motion from blackness to whiteness via redness then yellowness then whiteness. Likewise, if the medium differs while the start and end are unified, like motion from point to point along a straight line compared to motion along a curved line, and like motion from whiteness to blackness via the path of taking on yellowness then redness then blackness, compared to motion via the path of taking on greenness then indigo then blackness. And what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif—that unity of the medium, the starting point, and the ending point is necessary, for if the medium differs, the type differs, like heating and blackening—is not correct, because this is only valid for illustration (tamthil) and not for demonstration (ta'lil). It is as if he intended that the type differs when merely the medium differs, just as it differs when the three things differ, like heating and blackening; or perhaps the original was 'heating and cooling' and it was corrupted to 'blackening'.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a proof and classification regarding the determination of motion type. It begins by stating the core thesis: a motion's type is determined solely by the unity of its starting point, ending point, and medium—where 'medium' means the subject or the path (e.g., the location or quality through which the motion occurs). Differences in the moved object (e.g., this stone vs. that stone), the mover (e.g., a human vs. a machine), or the time do not change the type. The author supports this with a general principle: diversity of subjects or causes does not necessarily diversify the accidents that inhere in them or the effects produced, as a single accident like heat can exist in different subjects and come from different efficient causes. Applying this to motion: upward motion of a flame (natural), a stone (forced), and a bird (voluntary) are all the same type—translation in location—because the start (lower), end (higher), and medium (space) are identical. Next, the author addresses time: even if time were diverse in essence (which is impossible), it could still be attached to one type of motion. The objection that time is an accident of motion and that accident difference implies subject difference is weak because the motion whose accident time is—namely, the motion of the celestial sphere—is a particular motion, whereas here we are discussing motion in general. The author then clarifies when motion type does differ: when the starting and ending points differ, even if the medium is the same (e.g., upward vs. downward motion), and when the medium differs, even if start and end are the same (e.g., straight vs. curved motion, or passing through different intermediate qualities). Finally, he critiques the position in al-Mawaqif which requires unity of medium, start, and end for type unity, arguing that that position is only valid for illustration, not as a causal explanation, and may be a textual corruption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that forced, natural, and voluntary motions are different types because they have different causes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that if the start, end, and medium are identical, such motions are the same type; the cause (mover) does not affect the type.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which factor alone does NOT change the type of motion?
A) The starting and ending points
B) The medium (ما فيه الحركة)
C) The mover (المحرك)
D) The category of motion (الأين أو الكيف)

Key Takeaway
The type of a motion is defined solely by its starting point, ending point, and medium, not by the mover, moved object, or time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your daily actions: do you judge them by their outward causes (e.g., peer pressure, habit) or by their true starting intention and end goal? How many of your actions would change type if you examined the intention and the path? This week, choose one action and deliberately align its start (niyyah), end (pleasure of Allah), and path (Shari'ah) to ensure it is of the right type.
?
The lesson teaches that forced, natural, and voluntary motions can be the same type if start, end, and medium align. In your spiritual life, are you sometimes forced into good deeds, or do you only do them naturally when it's easy? What would it take for you to perform an act of worship with the same type as the prophets—with pure intention, seeking Allah, and following the Sunnah?
?
The author shows that time does not affect the type of motion. Yet we often postpone good deeds because 'now is not the right time' or feel that a past sin cannot be erased. How does this lesson challenge your excuse about time? What is one specific action you have delayed because of time, and what is its true type in the sight of Allah?
